THE RED PAGE.
Albert Dorrington, whose book (“Castro’s Last
Sacrament, and Other Stories ”) is published this
week, is well-known to readers of The Bulletin as
“ Alba Dorion.” He avows himself an Australian
by adoption ; but his birthplace was Stratford-on-
Avon, and his birthtime 29 years ago. There is a
family legend that a gipsy astrologer foretold the
advent of Shakespeare Secundus, who would write
tragedies with a pen in one hand and a pie in the
other; but the only tragedy Dorrington tried to
write was his own, and he didn’t succeed. The
education of this Shakespeare manqu4 was con-
ducted partly by the masters of King Edward’s
School, Birmingham, partly by a Brum prize-
fighter named Bosh —honoured in small capitals
as the Larry Foley (and more) of his epoch; and
the mixture of lessons resulted in a Shakespearian
pirate drifting to Australia at the age of sixteen.
He carried a heavy purse, which vanished
in three weeks on the unseen wings of a
sharper’s ace. The pirate faced the situation
and became a canvasser. He canvassed back
to the Barrier, up to Bourke, through Queensland,
and into nameless places, where Gehenna lay be-
tween tank and tank. He laid snares for the cash
of the lonely Chinaman in N.S.W. ranges, the
affluent, paid-off Kanaka of the Isis scrub, and
the thrifty German settlers round Tanunda and
Kapunda. He sold millinery to Afghan camel
drivers, cameras to the Jap shelters of the Straits,
and camped in the Jardine country with the gay
aboriginal who comes back for a coupon when he
has stolen your clothes. In between there were dis-
appearances to Egypt, England, and outlandish
places; but to Australia he always returned. At
present lie lives in Sydney, having seen more of
this country, and lived closer to its earth, than
ninety-nine in a hundred men born here. He has
the experience-equipment of a dozen story-tellers,
and the brains of one—a good one. And his
advice, humbly offered to young Australian writers,
is : Smell the East and West and North, and
you ’ll come back with a better notion of the
South.
—♦
James Hebbleth waite, whose booklet of verses,
“A Rose of Regret,” is also braving the fierce
light that beats upon a bookseller’s counter, is
perhaps the first Tasmanian to find poetic pro-
minence—and he is an Englishman, born at Pres-
ton in 1857. His youth came under dominion of
an old library in a green bird-haunted square;
and the lofty timbered roof, the huge stone fire-
place with its winter glow upon long dark
Georgian portraits, the time-stained books rich in
engravings of a quaint and vanished England,—
these were among the sources of his life. He
entered with a scholarship St. John’s College,
Battersea, London; and remained two years
(1877-8), spending his holidays on the Thames, or
4ji Westminster Abbey, or in rambles after Lamb
'and Dickens through the never-ending streets.
For twelve years thereafter he was teaching, and
lie lectured on English literature at the Harris
Institute, Pre3ton. Later he came to Tasmania,
and after employment at schools in Hobart
entered the Congregational ministry. He is now
principal of Queen's College, Latrobe, Tas. A
booklet of his verses, published in 189 G by The
Hobart Mercury, is basis of the collection now
issued by The Bulletin.
Simpson Newland, author of “ Blood Tracks of
the Bush ” (Gay and Bird, 1900), is also author of
“ Paving the Way,” another bush romance—and
somebody lias told him to make his title wilder for
sake of sale. His work remains tame. He has a
fund of Australian experience, admirable energy,
but not the literary brain. He cannot make his
words live. The medical profession knows that
your fine, large, high-stepping woman is often a
poor mother of children : she is fitted to hold, but
not to transmit vitality. On the other hand, you
will often find the mother of fourteen or sixteen
stalwart sons and daughters in a thin little rat of
a creature who looks as if a puff would blow her
away : she is a tough aboriginal built to bestow
life and die after cooking the dinner at eighty.
The same in literature. It is not energy, but
specialised energy, which makes a book. “Paving
the Way ”is advertised in a seventh edition; but it
is the “ founded-on-fact’ interest which has sold it.
Simpson Newland’s imagination flares from a
dramatic episode of life; but when he writes it
down the episode warms, not because of what he
has written, but almost despite. And he
lacks the rare architectonic faculty: his books are
welded scraps crying for the furnace and the
mould. T. A. Browne is at the head of the local
fact-romancers because he has more skill to weave
a coherent plot, subordinating his details to the
whole; though at his best his art falls far short of
Marcus Clarke’s, and at its worst it is no better
than nephew William S. Walker’s, another
energetic producer of excellent material-for-litera-
ture who is just now pounding away, pounding
away. Every one of these men who know and
can’t write should be pen-married to a man who
doesn’t know but can write. Then we should
have progeny worth buying type-clothes and ink-
boots for.

The twenty-seventh chapter of J. M. Barrie’s
“Tommy and Grizel” saunters through Sept.
Scribner's at a charming gait: it wants strength,
blood —yea, ber-lud —
The blood of the bullock that falls down and dies with
a thud.
Even in the dramatic moment when hidden
Grizel listens to Tommy wooing the Strange
Woman in the arbour, and breathes hard, and is
discovered, there is no intensity, nothing to melt
your teeth and freeze your ears as should be in
the tropic-arctic pendulum-swing of emotion.
Barrie has found his situation (anybody’s old
situation) and lost the wages. Only his wooing is
enthralling—
Alice, I love you, for you are love itself ; it is you I
nave been chasing since first it rose like a bird at my feet,
I never had a passing fancy for any other woman ; I
always knew that somewhere in the world there must be
you and some time this starless night and you for me.
You were hidden behind walls of ice ; no man had passed
them ; I broke them down and love leapt to love, and \ ou
lie here, my beautiful love, in the arms of its lover.
“Its”? Balance the “your,” and decide if
Barrie chose bettor. I say not : to preserve the
figure he lost the personal appeal, and he intruded
a warning hiss where the fascinating voweliquid
might have snaked the bird.
The preliminary play lias been interesting:
Tommy does not love the Strange Woman, but
she piques him, tantalises him, and he burns to
lower that defiant flag. One sign of surrender,
and he will give up the siege . . . and is carried
off on the blood-current. (John Reay Watson holds
the psychology less daintily, more firmly, in an
unpublished story.) And the Strange Woman,
never yielding, yields—a in lieu
of the ancient peripheral obsession, the reacting
cerebral stimulus unnoticed by Meredith in
“Modern Love”—she too knowing that she can
refrain. But Tommy flutes on—
What does the world look like to you, my darling?
You are in it for the first time. You were born but a
moment ago. It is dark, that you may not be blinded
before you have used your eyes. These are your eyes,
dear eyes that do not yet know their purpose ; they are
for looking at me, little Alice, and mine are for looking
into yours. I cannot see you. I have never seen the.
face of my love—oh, my love, come into the light that I
may see y r our face.
The Strange Woman had no wild humour to think
of the blind kittens boy Tommy had drowned :
woman’s lack of humour is the salvation of her
lovers. “ Her head had fallen on his shoulder ”
“and then the Other Woman comes,” and
Barrie’s porcelain art shivers. “ His face was
terrible,” says Barrie, giving Tommy away like a
stage villain with a pound of grease-paint. “(To
be continued.) ”
“ Never the time and the place and the loved
one all together.” What is an ideal all-together ?
Half-a-guinea will be paid for the best plan and
specification of the trio—in not more than 200
words sent before Dec. 1. Mem.— This must
be taken seriously, passionately, devoutly, in
choice words almost worthy of the ideal. P.S.—
No blushes, unless behind the fan.

“It isn’t had’’—said Stephen Crane, nearing
his end—“it isn’t bad when you come to the
hedge that we must all go over.' You feel sleepy
and—you don’t care. Just a little dreamy curio-
sity—which world you ’re really in—that’s all.”
The first number of The Monthly Review arrives
—presumably edited by Henry Newbolt as an-
nounced, though his name does not appear. His
poem praising Gordon of Khartoum does appear—
to no particularly poetic end. The best verse—
Yet there have been on earth
Spirits of starry birth,
Whose splendour rushed to no eternal setting :
They over all endure,
Their course through all is sure,
The dark world’s Tight is still of their begetting.
Though the long past forgotten lies,
Nile 1 in thy dream remember him
Whose like no more shall rise
Above our twilight s rim
Until the immortal dawn shall make all glories dim.
The opium reminiscences of the late Terence
Kelly, of Liverpool, cel. 52
I have no doubt that the taking of fifty to sixty grains
of opium daily for fifteen years has had - everything to do
with my present slate. To save trouble at the enquiry, I
may say 1 have gone so low at last that I have to commit
suicide. Some time since I tried halt'-an-ounce of lauda-
num, then one ounce, then one-eighth of an ounce of
opium itself, then a-quarter of an ounce, and, at last,
three-eighths of an ounce of opium—but all without
effect; and this time I intend to take half-an-ounce, or,
say, 220 grains, and that ought to finish me, when six or
eight grains are enough to kill an ordinary Christian.
I am sorry I have no better legacy to leave my chil-
dren, but as I went through life I had not the gift of
making money in quantities. I can understand why Lord
Clive, with all his advantages of wealth and station, com-
mitted suicide after using the drug for years, and I may
mention the operation of it. When an opium-eater starts
out on his career, each time he takes his daily dose in
about half an hour the action of the heart increases, and
that organ does double duty by sending the blood danc-
ing through the veins at double-quick pace. The opium-
eater feels a better man under its influence, and this
without the brain becoming cloudy, as with liquor. The
sensatfon is glorious, and in addition to the heart doing
double duty it is for all the world as though some in-
visible hand were wrapping a warm blanket round that
organ. No one can imagine the pleasure and strength—
mentally and physically—that the drug gives, but then
comes a time for punishment.
I must not forget the only important point about
opium, and that is this : During the fifteen years I took
it—or, say, from thirty to forty years of age—l found the
brain as well as the heart quickened in action. Now my
brain will not receive any impression, and it is quite soft,
and I have no more thinking faculty than a boy ten years
of age, and have forgotten almost all I ever knew. My
poor heart sometimes beats wild, as though it was going
to burst outright, and at other times stops, as though it
was going to beat no more. So, all things considered, if
anyone thinks of commencing to eat opium, my advice to
him is to leave it alone, or he will very likely come to
what I am myself, and then, perhaps, when he gets on in
life, he will have to elect between the workhouse and
committing suicide. For my part, I prefer the latter as
a choice.
Then the wooden jury delivered the unsound-
mind verdict.
Always, for ever mine! Her mother brow,
Even before her kisses, told me ; and her low,
Ravishing voice ; and those dear fluttering doves
She calls her eyes ; and all the tender loves
That hide in every dimple a shy vow.
Yet—and to-day ! —she doubts. This bird, this bough,
This song—will they endure if passion go?
Will it be summer in my lover’s groves
Always, for ever ?
Ah, if she only knew ! if she knew how
I yearn to hold her, make her happy, strow
Love-petals for tired feet: that never roves
My good thought from her, howe’er folly moves :
She would believe that I can love her now,
Always, for ever.
ci. G.H.T.
“ Peter K.’s” aftermath—mostly labels:
Psychology recognises two main forms of imagination :
(1) Reproductive Imagination or Memory; and (2) Pro.
ductive or Constructive Imagination. We are chiefly con-
cerned with the second of these.
Reproductive Imagination is wholly representative of
the past, and in this it differs from Perception, in which
the ideal element does not involve the reproduction of
any particular fact. In the Reproductive process the
images are mere copies of past ; but what is
commonly known as Imagination involves more th n this.
When we imagine an unrealised event of the future, or
when we imagine a place which is described to us, hut
which we have never seen, we are going beyond on'r actual
experience.' Images of memory are being in some way
modified, transformed, and recombined, and h.oiee this
process is marked off as Productive or Constructive imagi-
nation. It is the Productive process which is specially
referred to by the common term Imagination. While,
however, we thus mark off Productive Imagination as a
stage of psychological elaboration going bey ond reproduc-
tion, we can draw no sharp line ol demarcation between
the turn.
Productive or Constructive Imagination assumes various
forms. Thus we have Active and Passive Imagination,
Passive being that in which there is no conscious effort
(as in dreams), whilst Active is that which implies a con-
scious effort. Again, we have Receptive and Creative
Imagination. Receptive is illustrated in the realizations
of another’s ideal grouping,'as when we read a poem and
form a mental picture of the scenes and incidents des-
cribed. While this is a comparatively simple operation,
Creative Imagination, or Creative Productive Imagina-
tion (as distinguished from Receptive Productive Im
agination) is a much more difficult process, and as an
example we may take the imagination of (he poet who
first created the combination of words and sentences.
The act of construction here is of a much higher order,
involving more complex processes of reproduction, rejec-
tion, and selection, and directed solely by an internal
sense of what is beautiful and harmonious.
And there is another written page of labels,
which is thrown back to “Peter K.” without
much misgiving of loss.

“F.S.D.” chats to the. The Bulletin from
Berlin, Sept. 14-
In Berlin, September is the month when the red
geraniums and the china : asters come out, and all the
host of theatres open, and Wagner and the Muses come
back to the Opera House. For these Spree “ Athenians ”
(as the Germans’dub the Berliners) are in their way ardent
lovers of the arts. On Sunday evenings the great melo-
dious bells of the new Gediichtniss Kirche boom out, or
the old belfry of the S. Nicolas kirk calls sweetly, and
everybody' thinks “Mein Gott! how the afternoon’s
slipped away. It’s time we were getting ready to start
for the theatre,”—and fraus and fraxUeim are soon all
agog for the play. There’s Mozart’s “ Don Juan ” at the
Opera, and “ Hamlet ” at the Royal, ail'd Hauptmann
at the Deutsches Theatre* Ibsen’s “ Comedy of Love ” at
the Secession, and Anzengruber at the Berliner—and so
on. And, above all, there’s Eleonora Duse and her com-
pany at the Lessing. All the dressing-rooms are full of
beautiful-winged perfumed creatures disrobing, the buzz
and hurry and bustle of anticipation, the last touches to
hair and cheeks before the smug servile mirrors, till
the whole bevy of them—their arms and bosoms and
diamonds and silks—are ushered into place be-
neath the unsparing electric-light. Aristocratic German
mammas and their fair-haired daughters hump and mingle
with the rouml-nos-d scions of Abraham—Jew and Gen-
tile, Czech and Teuton, tumbled together as in the sheet
let down from Heaven (Acts, x, 11): Oh, to forget them
all! Oh, that there were some dimness or mystery about
an audience before a play starts—something of that
cathedral twilight which should hallow art as well as
religion—and tiie theatre a great mystic! pillared temple
of Art!
It is a new piece of D’Annunzio’s the Duse is playing—
for the first time to-night— (7m Sogno d’un Maltino di
Primavera—A Spring Morning’s Dream. Whit an
actress! Italian finely spoken inebriates like a Southern
vintage. But, ahiimS ! what trash is this drama, what a
disillusion. As for Duse herself, she reminds me of
Novelli by her simplicity, an uorress in the grand style.
There is a fragrance, a languor, a fire and diablerie, a
pathos, a will-o’-the-wisp loveliness about this woman, a
rich swift movement and fine finish of tone and gesture
and feeling—that only Italy can produce. What a delight
to listen to Shakespeare Spoken passionately and finely
without the use of that rant the English call elocu-
tion ! Only the Italians can play Shakespeare nowadays.
In ordinary life Signora Duse is a quiet, refined lady with
big questioning black eyes and beautiful hands (D’An-
nunzio dedicated La Giocouda to her—“ alia donna
dalle belle man! ”), and on her face the half-secret lines of
tragic passions and hopes that have been fought down.
Who would ever dream her capable of that panther-like
voluptuousness that speaks from her whole being in
Cleopatra! In the scene where the slave announces
Antony’s marriage with Octavia she is unrivalled. She
gives you a key which unlocks the inmost nature of this
Egyptian queen, this serpent of old Nile. Girlish wim
someness, and devilish lust and rage, and mortal jealousy,
are mingled in tins magnificent scene. And, next, night,
she is the charming dainty Venetian of Goldoni Miran-
dolina, with all her transformations of butterfly grace
and coquetry and mischief! But 1 shalf hover forget,
never, the desperate horror and wantonness of Cleo-
patra’s hands as she flings the asp to her bosom. It is
not acting death, it is dying. . . .
Bqt of this “ dramatic poem ” of D’Annunzio’s, this
“ Spring morning’s dream.” It is a thorough hybrid.
The episode is in Zola’s worst taste (it is that horribly-
affected motive which those who have read “ Rome ’>
will remember in the death of Benedettag The treatment
is an imitation of Maeterlinck, and the superb phrasing of
the flaccid characters is D’Annunzio's own. Briefly, the
“plot” is this. The “heroine” of the piece is a de-
mented young lady who has lost her lover. This lover
has been stabbed to death (by somebody mystically re-
ferred to as the Duke) while lying in Isabella’s arms, and
all night long she has lain with his warm blood flowing
over her, and lias felt his dying tremors ; and when dawn
comes she is demented. A brother of the murdered man
comes riding “ through the dewy springtide morning,”
and he has always silently been enamoured of the lady he
now finds mad, just as Isabella’s sister Beatrice is
enamoured of him. And now the demented Isabella
imagines herself becoming a part of the earth and the
springtime, a sister of the grass and flowers, and say's
many pretty things out of the middle of a laurel-bush.
Till suddenly she starts forward shrieking, because a red
insect has alighted on her arm, and she imagines herself
wrapped in blood again. Then the physician comes and
tries to quieten her with illusions—a butterfly has passed
with a message for her, and . . . and . , . and there the
matter ends. The curtain falls, and you turn to your
neighbor and ask what it all means. You can’t laugh,
and you can’t weep, and your neighbor shrugs his shoul-
ders, and turns his hands downwards from the artist and
his eyes to Heaven, and murmurs “ Chi lo mgg’ !”
Surely madness is out of place on the stage except when
by its pathos it serves to show better the heauty and value
of sanity. Shakespeare knew better than to write a drama
in which Ophelia was the only figure. Does D’Annunzio
wish to climax by contrasting madness with the fresh
light of spring, and leave the rest to our sense for sym-
bolism? His failure to stir the heart condemns him
There is at present an eager worship of D’Annunzio in
Germany, especially in South Germany and Austria.
The leader is young Hugo von Hoffinansthal, an
astonishing parallel of the Italian maestro ; and he repre-
sents the reaction against the naturalist and realistic
school of Hauptmann and Sudermann. D’Annunzio,
Maeterlinck, Hofi'mnnsthal— each wishes renaissance of
beauty in the deama, in form, conception and mysticism ;
and away with Ibsen 1 The aspiration is a good one;
but -but -as often before, the spirit is willing, the flesh
weak. '
This year sees the 70th birth-anniversary of Paul Heyse,
the Bavarian novelist and poet—“ heir of Goethe,” as his
admirers call him. Two of the best novels in German
literature are his work—“ Die Kinder der Welt” and
“Im Paradiese.” By his short stories, translations, &c.
he has dme more to make Italian poetry arid Italy
beloved than any other German, hiving in Munchen, in
touch with the best of German art, he holds one hand
north-ward to Germany, the other southward to Italy,
whither his eyes have always turned.
Let green weeds gather in our garden ways,
Let summer suns hum down our favourite blooms
But let no happy-hearted brides and grooms
Cross the dear paths, for sake of those dear days.
Let Time rot down our homestead walls and rase,
Let gray dost gather and the grief of tombs,
But never to those laughter-loving rooms
Give laughter entrance while a roof-beam stays.
Let them stand lonely by the outer gate,
The late-won lovers and the tardy friends ;
And ever, while one tear for memory starts,
Drop their kind hands : let us be brave and wait,
The world’s new loves can make us no amends.
Let us be loyal to our empty hearts.
N.S.W.

SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in tlie face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could not be
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears’ Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it with him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
, “ matchless for the complexion.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Silence of Mullock Creek.
He was dubbed the Lisping Infant when he came
to Mullock Creek;
Most confiding was his nature, and his manners
they were meek;
He was fair and wore an eyeglass, and a Sunday
suit for days;
He’d a soft, engaging simper, and such fascinating
ways.
’Twas a time of sore adversity, and sinful men and
weak
Said that Fate created Clyde to be the prey of
Mullock Creek;
For he’d booked himself at Hogan’s pub. effusively
and rash
As the travelling representative of eighty thou-
sand, cash.
He was buying mining properties. His syndicate
in town
Had the greatest faith in Mullock Creek. The
terms were money-down.
So if any man amongst us wished to sell a likely
show,
Would he kindly state his price, “ and furnish
samples, don’t you know.”
’Twas the softest thing on earth beneath. The
Creek dissembled well—
Not a man about the field but had a score of shows
to sell —
But from Cooper’s Hump to Fly bite they were
roaring down below
At the Johnnie buying leases “as per sample,
don’t you know.”
Soon the joke was bandied freely, miners yelled it
near and far.
“ Where’s yer samples ? ” was the greeting on the
brace and in the bar,
And each grimy digger answered he was ‘ ‘ trav -
lin’ fer his chief,
With a lovely lot o’ samples of ihe latest lines o’
reef! ”
But the Infant was oblivious, and when any of
the throng
Tried to sell a golden lode, and took no specimens
along,
He would answer very cutely : “How on earth’s
a chap to know
What he’s buying if you haven’t any samples here
to show ? ”
So for bits of golden stone arose a wonderful
demand ;
They were prigged from stopes and hoppers, they
were gathered in the land.
Treasured specimens from Bendigo and half-a-
hundred fields
Served to advertise the local lodes and guarantee
the yields.
Peter Hirst with lumps of barren quartz and seven
weights of gold
Made the sweetest lot of “samples” (’twas a
cunning trick of old),
And the stranger placed the specimens in little
canvas bags,
With the vendors’ names and figures neatly stated
on the tags.
Clyde was eager to submit the splendid offers he’d
received,
With the “samples,” to his people in the city, we
believed, ,
And in some way every owner knew his cat was
in the pot,
Though the Infant rather fancied that his firm
would buy the lot.
Now, its wondrous expectations worked the creek
a moral ill,
And so cold and proud the diggers grew they
wouldn’t lift a drill,
But they drank of Hogan’s whisky till the sinners
couldn’t see,
And the town and district started on a bucking
jamboree.
Still from far and near the miners came with pro-
perties to sell,
Bringing “samples” down in sacks, and some cn
sleds and drays as well.
When the Infant took receipt they joined the
dissipated throng
Charming snakes in Hogan’s bar until their
cheques should come along.
When at length the vendors sobered they went
searching everywhere
Round the township for the Infant, but the Infant
wasn’t there.
He had fled. A studious absence on the part of
Mrs. Hirst
Was coincidental maybe, but her husband feared
the worst.
Then a letter came to Hogan, which he kindly
read aloud:
“As I’ve cleaned you out at Mullock Creek, it’s
fair to tell the crowd
How those lovely ‘samples’ yielded,” so the
Infant’s letter ran.
“ I have had them milled ; they ran to sixty
ounces in the pan ! ” t
Not a syllable was spoken, stunned and silently
the men
Turned and drifted off, and silently they sought
their holes again.
Should you visit Mullock Creek to-day, you’ll find
they can’t forget,
And that awful silence brooding o’er the stricken
township yet. i
v.
EDWARD DYSON.

OFFICIAL
Gentlemen,
Public Works Department,
Sydney, 23rd July, 1900.
In reply to your letter of the 14th Inst, with reference to the 3-ply Ruberoid Roofing which was laid down upon the floor
of the Phillip Street entrance to this Department on the 12th December, last, in lieu of floor cloth. I have the honor to inform
you that the average number of persons who passed over the part of passage from which the sample submitted was taken, was
about 8,000 per week, or about 250,000 during the period the covering has been in use. The sample of Ruberoid removed
from the floor is returned herewith as requested.
1 have the honor to be, Gentlemen,
Your obedient servant,
ROBERT HICKSON,
Under Secretary and Commissioner for Roads.
Messrs. E. Rich & Co., Ltd.,
24 and 26 O'Connell Street,
Sydney.
NOTE. —It is fair to presume that fully 400,000 people have now walked, over the 1* & B Ruberoid which still covers the floor of the
passage above referred to, and still appears unchanged.

SATURDAY. NOVEMBER, 3, 1900.
A Policy for the Commonwealth—A
Federal Life Assurance Department.
The Parliament shall, subject to this Constitution, have
power to make laws for the peace, order, and good
government of the Commonwealth with respect to ... .
14. Insurance, other than State Insurance ; also, State
Insurance extending beyond the limits of the State con-
cerned.—Commonwealth Constitution, clause 51.
As the Federal Government is entrusted
with power to make laws dealing with insur-
ance matters in every form, except as regards
State insurance when the business is con-
fined within the limits of the State con-
cerned, it is fairly obvious that it has power
to establish a Federal Life Assurance
Department doing business all over the
Commonwealth territory. As to the desira-
bility of using that power, there is, in The
Bulletin’s opinion, no possible shadow of
doubt whatever. Life assurance, above all
otherthings, requires to befounded on a rock.
There is no rock too large or too solid to
found it on. It is pre-eminently a thing for
the State to undertake, for the State can
give better security than any company or
society whatsoever. The A.M.P. Society is
one of the grandest and most solid things of
its kind, but another A.M.P. Society, with, in
addition to the guarantee of its £16,000,000
of assets, the added guarantee of all Aus-
tralia and Tasmania, would be a great deal
grander and much more solid. It is impos-
sible to have too much security behind an
institution which is responsible for the for-
tunes of so many thousands of prospective
widows and orphans. Moreover, life assur-
ance is now compulsory with a large class of
the community. In a considerable portion
of Australia civil servants are required
to take out a life policy as one of
the fixed conditions of their employment.
It will presumably be the sanie, dr something
like the same, with the Commonwealth’s
servants. And no Government has a right
to compel anybody to assure his life unless
it also provides him with an absolutely safe
office to do it in. At present the public
servant is merely bidden to go out and buy
a life policy somewhere or anywhere, and if,
in his ignorance, he is beguiled into doing it
in an office which comes to grief and doesn’t
pay up, the State offers him no redress.
* * * «
Next to providing an absolutely safe office
it is essential to provide a cheap one. The
Commonwealth Government can do both.
It can build up a large institution where the
expenses, after the unavoidable preliminary
outlay of the first year, won’t be over 5 per
cent, of the receipts. It can work its
institution at little more than half
the cost of even the marvellously
cheap A.M.P. Society. The great outlay
with the ordinary life office is the very heavy
commission and other preliminary charges
that have to be paid to secure new busi-
ness. The Commonwealth Government, by
compulsorily insuring all its own servants to
begin with, can get a large amount of good
and permanent business without paying any
commission. It can also collect the pre-
miums without any commission, by the
simple process of deducting the amount
from its servants’ salaries. As its servants
increase in number so also will its business
increase. It can also, probably, make ar-
rangements for doing business with the
servants of the State Governments. As
regards the general public it can reach them
by allowing a number of its employes to
act as agents on a very small commission.
The commission would certainly be much
smaller than the rate they could get from
private offices if they were allowed to act
as agents for private offices, but as they
are not allowed to do so they would lose
no existing income, and would gain a
small new one. The competition of the
Federal Government might injure the busi-
ness of existing offices, but if there isn’t
enough business to go round, and someone
must be injured, it is to the public interest
that the cheapest office with the best security
should win, and that the more expensive
offices with the second-best security should
lose.
• • • •
Even if the extra competition ended in the
closing up of some of the existing offices it
would be rather beneficial than otherwise.
Several of the small proprietary offices of
this country exist more for the interests of
the shareholders and the management than
for the good of the policy holders, and as
far as concerns the insuring public—which is
the community at large—their destruction
would be a blessing. Some otherwise respect-
able mutual societies are managed so expen-
sively that, if they could be replaced by a far
more cheaply-coutrolled State institution,
the advantage to the insuring public in the
shape of increased profits would be very
great, and their vanishment would also be
something of a blessing to the community in
general. In some cases it might even be
beneficial to the existing members if these
institutions were compelled, by the extra
pressure of State competition, to give up
doing the new business which they buy so
expensively, and go into a gradual process of
winding up. It would be bad, no doubt, for
the glory and vanity and salary of the
management if an office ceased growing and
began to die away, but the existing policy-
holders in these extravagantly-managed
concerns gain nothing by the struggle to
attract new business. It is an open ques-
tion if the new business does not, in some
cases, cost so much as to seriously diminish
the old members’ profits, in which case these
old members might gain a visible cash ad-
vantage if their institutions went into a
process of slow extinction.
• * • 4
The Commonwealth’s Life Assurance De-
partment would need no palatial offices
to make kniwu its existence. It wouldn’t
require to spread itself over a huge mass of
advertising space. Its security and the
handsome profits it made by the economy of
its management would be its advertisement.
It would have a considerable body of clients
secured to it, without expense, among the
public servants, and it could take whatever
other business came in its way, and wait.
It needn’t scatter its money in frantic and
costly efforts to make itself within a few
years the biggest office in Australasia ; size
would come to it. If it never became the
biggest office in Australasia that wouldn’t
matter; it would be the safest, which is
much better, and it would be large enough
for all ordinary purposes.
• • * •
Such an institution wouldn’t require to
hustle after a large, insecure mortgage
business with a view to making extensive
profits. If it invested all its pile at
an average rate of per cent, (and it
could probably get that in good State and
municipal securities) and kept its expenses
down to 5 per cent., it would be
able to pay practically the same bonus
rates as the A.M.P. Society, and no-
thing much better could be asked for.
It could pay much larger bonuses than any
of the A.M.P.’s large rivals. The expense
of managing its funds would be greatly re-
duced by this method of investment, and that
would be an additional factor in diminishing
the expenses Further, there would be no
heavy losses through the periodical slump in
the value of the foreclosed property. With
practically no commission to pay. no need
for display, no lavish advertising, no pon-
derous investment department to maintain,
no foreclosed properties to manage, and
no losses through slump in the foreclosed
properties to write off, there is no visible
reason why a Commonwealth Life Assurance
Department should not be managed at a
cost of 5 per cent, or even less. It would
bear no resemblance at all—except in the
matter of its State security—to the Maori-
land State Life Office, which hustles for
business in the ordinary expensive fashion.
There would be no need for any special
judgment in the selection of securities, for
the rules laid down would make it almost
impossible to commit serious error, and
there would be no vast and elaborate system
of pushing business to organise at the out-
set. Under these circumstances one small
and reasonably honest man and an office-boy,
with a two-roomed office to start in and a
sign-board, could lay the inexpensive but
solid foundations of the most reliable life
assurance department in the world. The
loud and bitter cursing of the Fat
Man press would give it a good
send-off gratis in the way of adver-
tising. It could be further advertised
cheaply on the cover of Hansard and on all
government publications, blue-books, books
of statistics. Bills and Acts of Parliament ;
and a notice-board (not the kind that dis-
figures the landscape) conld bo hung outside
every Government office, benevolent asylum,
hospital, gaol, lock-up, railway-station, &c.,
setting forth its advantages at length. The
Federal Old Age Pension office could be run
in the same building and under the same
management to further reduce the expenses.
Old Age Pensions are a kind of life assurance,
and the association of the two establish-
ments would emphasise the fact.
m * • •
Some day, no doubt, in a more advanced
state of civilisation, life assurance will be
made general and compulsory. When that
time comes there will be a magnificent
future before the Commonwealth’s Life
Assurance Department, but that stage of
civilisation can’t be reached until there is
such a department; the public can’t be com-
pelled to insure unless the same power which
compels it also supplies a safe place to insure
in. There is no definite reason why the
Commonwealth should not some day add fire
and accident insurance to its other avoca-
tions ; the latter, in particular, is an essen-
tial feature in every scheme of civilisation,
and at present it is solely in the hands of
proprietary companies whose rates, if the
vastness of their profits is any criterion, are
woefully exorbitant. In a certain indirect
way, too, such a Department as that sug-
gested above would do a good work apart
from the ostensible reasons of its existence.
By investing its accumulations mostly in
State securities it would help a little towards
buying out the British money-lender who
has such a stupendous lien on this country,
and aid in making Australia independent of
that external incubus. And every agency
which helps, however slightly, in that direc-
tion is a blessing to the much-mortgaged
land of Australia, whose three great products
are wool, gold and I O U’s, with a few
obscure vegetables in the background.
A Plea for the Loving Woman.
Invited to open a maternity home recently,
a lady occupying a high social position in
Maoriland is reported to have publicly said
that the need of such homes was a blot
upon civilisation ; and their establishment might
tend to increase instead of curing the evil, unless
some legal penalty was imposed upon the parents
of illegitimate children, who were morally and
often physically defective. “She thought that a
second offence clearly proved some moral defect,
and that in such cases legal detention should be
resorted to, say for two years on the birth of a
second, and three years on the birth of a third
illegitimate child.” And The Bulletin is sorry
for the lady.
* * * *
For this reason, among others. If it were her
own daughter who sinned —under stress of temp-
tation, or in the urgency of circumstances, or from
a woman’s need of love, in the woman’s glory of
loving too much—would this severe moralist send
her own daughter to gaol, to herd with criminals
and prostitutes ? Or to a penitentiary, to have her
mind shattered, her spirit stifled, her health per-
haps destroyed by routine and confinement ?
Without knowing the lady, imagining her the
ordinary, ignorant, well-meaning, often well-
doing woman, The Bulletin is very sure she
would not. She would surely say with
Ingebsoll : “ Go where you will; commit
what crime you may ; fall to what depths of
degradation you may ; you can never commit any
crime that will shut my door, my arms, my heart
to you.” And if she would say this, by what
right, what standard of justice, does she mete out
to another woman’s child, a less fortunate woman’s
unfortunate child, the cruel punishment which
she would deny to her own ?
* * * *
It is not singular, yet it is singular, that all this
Christianity should contain so little charity.
Jesus was the friend of sinning women ; even to
the woman taken in adultery he refused con-
demnation with, “Go in peace”; “Yea, till
seventy times seven ” he enjoined forgiveness of a
brother’s sins. And love outside marriage is not
a crime ; illegitimacy is not a crime. They have
been made legal crimes in darker generations ; but
light has banished them from most civilised
statute-books. It is true that in Australian com-
munities the illegitimate has still some legal
disabilities —and more shame to the communities.
The penalty which follows the incontinent woman
(and not the incontinent man) is a social penalty,
and it is sufficient for even the highest moralist
who has not gained his morals at the expense of
his reason.
* * * *
The edifice of morality is based on human
interests, and beneath human interests lie human
instincts. Next to Hunger, Love is the most
obvious human instinct, the greatest controlling
human force. You can no more stifle love than
you can stifle hunger—even if it were wise to try.
All that you can do is to endeavor to regulate love
for the benefit of the individual, the benefit of
the race.
* * * »
Possibly this earth may some day breed men
and women whose passions will be strong, yet held
firmly under restraint; but that day D hardly yet
within even imaginable distance. The history of
humanity, for the comparatively short time of
which we have record, shows always the same
animal hidden under a greater or less veneer of
polish. And it shows, too, fortunately or
unfortunately, that civilisation as we know it
carries the seed of its own decay; and that the
hyper-cultured nation tends sooner or later to
emasculation, with the. result of destruction by
some more brutal, but more virile, tribe.
• * * *
The virtuous woman would not send another
woman’s daughter to the penitentiary for being
hungry : why should she send her for loving, and
producing children as the fruit of love ? Loving
is as natural to a woman as hunger, and almost as
necessary. Starvation from love is just as real
and almost as fatal as starvation from hunger. It
brings in its train a special series of morbid phy-
sical and psychical effects—ailments and diseases
so numerous that many medical men, if they
dared, would counsel the love-cure at whatever
cost of conventional morality. To those who
understand it, the number of prematurely aged
and blighted women, withering for want of love,
is quite as great a social evil and cause of social
scandal as the number of women prostituted to love.
It is easy for the woman happily married to
counsel her suffering sister to press her breast
against the thorns of celibacy, and utter no cry ;
but that is not humane counsel, and its accept-
ance is an unnatural acceptance.
» * * *
The woman who revolts against the social con-
vention is punished by social ostracism; and if
she becomes more than once a mother it is urged
that she should be punished by imprisonment as
well. Imprison a woman for fulfilling her highest
instincts ! Imprison maternity ! Of what perver-
sion of Nature is not convention capable when it
issues advice such as that ! Did the lady
who is so shocked at illegitimacy ever con-
sider the prevalence of abortion in Australian
communities even now, when illegitimate
child - bearing is legally unpunished ? Did she
ever consider how abortion would increase if
continued illegitimate child-hearing were legally
punishable by imprisonment ? Of the two evils,
illegitimacy or abortion, would even this patroness
of chastity prefer abortion ?
* * * *
An illegitimate child is likely to be no more
“morally and physically defective” than a legi-
timate child. Indeed, conceived in passion, it is
more likely to be vigorous than the child of a jaded
marriage - bed. The moral and physical defects
of which the M.L. lady speaks are the gift of social
convention—the convention which decrees that
an unmarried woman shall bear her child in fear
and nourish it in trembling—the mark of social
obloquy, the object of the contemptuous pity of
“good” women with the pride of the Pharisee.
Social convention brands a child “illegitimate,”
and degrades it in estimation until it is degraded
in fact. And the remedy is not to punish the
mother, but to reform the convention.
* * * •
Illegitimacy is an evil partly because the social
convention is too deeply rooted for reform ; but
chiefly because it destroys that family life upon
which the best human progress is based. “No-
body’s child ” is heavily handicapped in the effort
to become a good citizen. The community should
endeavour to reduce illegitimacy to the minimum
which human frailty allows; but not by im-
prisoning the mother. It can only be done by
improving social conditions, with the end of
popularising marriage. If the community so well
governed itself that it would be possible for every
man to marry and found a comfortable home
before he was 30, every woman before she was 20,
the question of illegitimacy might almost be
neglected. In a word, to cure illegitimacy, you
must solve the whole economic problem ; and the
increase or decrease of illegitimacy is proportioned
to the community’s success in solving the economic
problem. Wherever there is human nature there
will always be—so far as we can see in the future
—some illegitimacy. But the ideal of simple
living, the inculcation of self-restraint within
reasonable limits, the care of fathers and mothers,
the equalisation of fortunes, the honesty and
capacity of governments, the justice of laws—
these are some of the things which will reduce
illegitimacy to its lowest terms. The Maoriland
lady’s remedy is much simpler, certainly. And
the cure would be ten times worse than the disease.
* * * *
The pity of illegitimacy (while the tribe of frigid
women is increasing) is that it almost always
befalls the children of the loving woman. The
girl who yields is often the richest in womanhood,
the best fitted to do credit to her sex. It is the
very wealth of her nature which brings her to
poverty. And it is often the poor in spirit, the
weak in sex, who in the social topsyturveydom
have the “virtue” (frigidity) and “the good
name ” (absence of temptation) which license them
to condemn her.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Banks of the Poor and Miserable.
Some weeks ago Australia’s alleged Freetrade
leader, George Reid, delivered a much-advertised
address at Melbourne on the blessings and advan-
tages of the policy which he never dared to prac-
tise during the five years of his alleged Freetrade
Premiership. In that address he effusively com-
pared the number of factory-workers in N.S.W.
and Victoria, greatly to the latter’s disadvantage,
the Victorian figures being, as usual, a perfectly
inaccurate set, made especially for the purpose—
something like the famous invented list of Vic-
torian prices which Reid once presented to Par-
liament, and about which he had to crawl down so
abjectly. He also spoke about the great increase
of England’s wealth under Freetrade, carefully
suppressing the fact that, according to so eminent
a Freetrade authority as Mui.hall’s “Dictionary
of Statistics,” Protectionist Germany, France and
the United States are all, in proportion
to population, increasing in wealth faster
than England. Likewise lie spread himself
in the same fashion, and with the same suppres-
sions, on manufactures and other things, and in a
wild, loud moment he said this :
The great millions of England were rising under it
(Freetrade). The Savings Banks of England have
£150,000,000 deposited in them—an increase of £36,000,000
in four years. THERE they saw the vitality of the
divine principle of human freedom breaking through the
clouds that encompassed it, bringing down the high and
raising the poor and miserable.
This seems a handy and accessible test, so The
Bulletin is quite willing to meet G;. Reid on his
own ground, and to assume that savings bank de-
posits—-being mainly the deposits of the work-
ing classes—are the_best criterion as to which
policy makes the people most prosperous and does
most to raise the poor and miserable. And on
that ground Freetrade —or what Reid calls Free-
trade—is utterly damned. The fact that under
Freetrade the pile of wealth in the British
People’s Banks is increasing doesn’t prove


that Freetrade is better than Protection ; it is
necessary to prove that the pile of the poor and
miserable in the leading Freetrade country in-
creases faster than in the leading Protectionist
countries. Apropos of which the following figures
are taken from the latest edition of the “ Dic-
tionary of Statistics,” published by that vigorous
English Freetrader, Mulhall :
From 1840 to 1889 (Mulhall, Page 86.)

No particulars about the United States are given

garbled tales about the raising of the poor and

If, as George Reid says, savings bank deposits
are the fairest test of the average prosperity and
comfort of the people, then Freetrade is utterly
lost. In this matter, as in almost every other,
Britain was ahead of the world before it left off
Protection. And in this matter, as in almost
every other, Britain has been losing its lead ever
since it left off Protection. When G. Reid
hunted up his British Savings Bank figures to aim
at the head of a weary public, he could hardly
avoid coming across the others also. The fact
that he suppressed the truth as regards these
others is just like G. Reid—probably the most
unscrupulous suppresser in Australian politics.

Rookwood. Asylum.
“ Old Hand ” to The Bulletin :
One way of commemorating in N.S. Wales the nativity
of Australian Federation would be to abolish the cruel
convict-system of treating the chronic sick and aged at
Rookwood Asylum. The convict stain of this State is now
apparent chiefly in the Botany Bay Government Bureau-
cracy which has been evolved from precedents established
by the tyrants and bullies who came out to the country
in connection with the convict system. Many of their
descendants, now in office, adhere to the principles of their
progenitors, and strive to “ block ”. all humani-
tarian progresss. Despite the burning-down of
the Garden Palace, allegedly to destroy unpleasant
records, and the mutilation, with the same
purpose, of sundry books in the Public Library,
that birthstain will never be completely wiped out unti.
Rookwood Asylum is done away with as a place for penal-
izing poverty conjoined with age and disease. In any
case, the establishment of a gigantic invalid settlement,
to which all the pauper sick of the State, are to be de-
ported, close to a reservoir connected with the water-
supply of Sydnev, is a danger to the health of the metro-
polis. Both these questions were decided some years ago
by expert witnesses before a Parliamentary com-
mittee, which reported adversely on the Rookwood
hospital scheme, and a large sum of money
was voted for building, in a suitable place, a hos-
pital for the up-to-date treatment of chronic
cases. Where is that money now? And where is
the hospital? Rookwood Asylum is known to the in-
mates of the Government institutions as the slaughter-
house. And there is only one other place in the civilised
world where the indigent sick are kept out of touch with
their friends and relatives and are practically imprisoned.
That is in Queensland—old Moreton Bay—which derived
its ideas of philanthropy from the Botany Bay regime of
the older State. The legacy of Lagdom, in fact, is
answerable for the semi-penal establishments of Dunwich
in Leperland and Rookwood in Lagland. These insti-
tutions are types of a barbarous and antiquated system
that would hot be tolerated in any community laying
claim to being in line with the progress of the age.

The A.M.P. iUcens on Vote.
The referendum of the A.M.P. policy-holders o i
the question of spreading the business to Great
Britain and “ other parts of the Empire ” panned
out badly for the cocksure and arrogant extension
party. The mock vote which was first taken gave
119,505 for the spreading process and only 28,820
against—a total vote of 148,325. On that occasion
the policy-holders were not asked to express an
opinion for or against extension. They merely
received papers to sign, appointing Joseph
Abbott and another director their proxies at a
meeting at which Joseph Abbott and the other
man would kindly vote for the extension on their
behalf. Most of them were in the habit of signing
such proxies as a mere matter of form, and
appointing anybody whose name happened to
appear thereon to represent them, as a mere
matter of form, at a merely formal meeting. The
document didn’t allow them to say whether
they were for or against the extension, so
they naturally didn’t say. There was a stamped
and addressed envelope enclosed for return,
and with these facilities it was really
easier to return it signed than to leave it
lying about the house. There were no means
provided for voting against the directors’ pro-
posals 5 the policy-holders weren’t even told where
to send their proxies if they wanted them used
against the Abbott party’s (that is to say, the
office party’s) scheme. It was practically the
same as if Lyne stood against Reid for King
Division and the official announcement read:
“ Electors who want to vote for Lyne can do so in
a large and commodious polling-booth in King-
street, Sydney. All votes polled there will be
counted "for Lyne. Another polling-booth has
been opened for the use of electors who want to
support Reid ; the address isn’t given, and if
Reid’s supporters can’t find the place, it is
their own look-out.” Moreover, the thing
was done with a rush, and many people
sent in their proxies before they really knew
what it was all about. But the opposition
demiuded a straight “ Yes ” or “No” vote, and
when this was taken, those policy-holders who
voted at all had to think about the matter and
form an opinion. The result was a much larger
vote of 200,018—and this time the extension party
was beaten by 9524. This changing of a majority
of 90,685 for extension into a majority of 9524
againstit clearly showed the perfunctory' character
of the.first vote, if it. could be called such, when
the policy-holders were" not asked to express, an
opinion on the merits of the case, -Mt only to give
their proxy to Joseph Abbott and ask no ques-
tions. That lamentably bad prophet, Sydney
Telegraph, which wildly declared that the vic-
tory of the extension party was dead certain, now
clamors that the question has not “been judged on
its merits.” It holds that a direct _ Yes or No
vote isn’t judging a question on its merits. Also
it points out t..at “not half the referendum
papers were returned,” though if that is a reason
against holding the second and direct vote to be
good, then the first or indirect one, where 51,000
fewer papers were returned, must have been very
bad indeed. The D.T.’s struggles to show the big
vote was bad because it wasnt bigger, and that
the much smaller one was quite good because it
was big enough, and that a straight ves or
“no” vote isn’t a fair way to ascertain the views
of the policy-holders, reveal it as a paper which
doesn’t know when to come in out of the rain.
It also points out indignantly that the first vote
was taken in the only way prescribed by the
Society’s Act, which isn’t true—there was noth-
ing in the Society’s Act to prevent the directors
giving the name of some person who would receive
proxies on the other side of the question, and thus
allowing the anti-extensionists the same show
which they secured for themselves. Altogether the
Telegraph’s soured and embittered walloping of
its dead horse is a most dismal spectacle.
“ A General Practice.”
The Bulletin reprints without comment S.M.
llerald’s report of a. N.S.W. Equity-Court judg-
ment of last week
A QUESTION OF COSTS.
Moss and others v. Moss and others.
His Honor gave judgment on Monday in an application
by the Permanent Trustee Co., for whom Mr. Knox, in-
structed by Messrs. Norton, Smith and Co., appeared, in
the suit of Moss and others v. Moss and others. Mr.
Lansbr Owen, instructed by Mr. L. F. Hetdon, appeared
to oppose. His Honor said the facts which were material
to the matter were not disputed. A Suit was instituted
in 1896 by the children of W. G. Moss and the children of
Mrs M'Daid for the administration of the estate of the
late’ William Moss. It involved the determination of
complicated questions arising on the construction of the
will and the validity of the will. By decree dated April,
1897, Mr. Justice Manning ordered the costs of all parties
as between solicitor and client to be paid out of the estate.
There were nine different sets of solicitors employed, and
the taxed costs payable out of the estate amounted to
£5068. Mr. Heydon acted throughout as solicitor for the
plaintiffs. Shortly before or at the institution of the
proceedings Mr. Heydon was requested by five of the
defendants to act for them, but as he could not do so he
introduced them to other solicitors, who acted for them,
Mr. Heydon at the same time arranging with such solici-
tors that he, Mr. Heydon, should receive a percentage
of their profit costs. It was admitted that moneys
were received by Mr. Heydon under this arrange-
ment, and that the existence of this arrangement
was not disclosed to the trustees of this estate, nor
to the Master, nor to the Taxing Officer. Mr. Hky-
don said he did not specifically tell any of the defen-
dants affected by the arrangement of its existence ; but
that W. G. Moss, Mrs. M'Daid, and a Mrs. Smith knew of
it. On the taxation of the bills—on further conside tion
—of the solicitors nominated by Mr. ll eydon that gentle-
man was appointed by the Master to tax down the bills,
and was the only solicitor present at the taxation, for
that purpose. The existence of the arrangement re-
ferred to had only lately become known to
the Permanent Trustee Company, which now, on
b:half of the infant plaintiffs, asked that the
money received under the arrangement by the
plaintiffs’ solicitor might be refunded to the estate, and
that the bills of costs of the solicitors mentioned might be
retaxed. The plaintiffs’solicitor disputed his liability to
refund, and contended that the Court had no jurisdiction
to order a refund except in a suit instituted for that pur-
pose. It seemed to his Honor that there were two well-
settled principles applicable to the matter. One was
that no person in a fiduciary position might use that
position to obtain a private advantage, and the other was
that no person in a fiduciary position might enter into
any engagement in which his personal interest conflicted
or might possibly conflict with his duty. The arrange-
ment entered into by the plaintiffs’ solicitor was opposed
to both those principles. It was the duty of the plaintiffs
solicitor to oppose the allowance of more than one
set of the defendants’ costs out of the estate, and
it was to his interest to allow' the costs of all defendants
solicitors nominated by him to be allowed. He did not
imagine that the conduct of the plaintiffs soucitor was
in fact influenced by his interest; but that the court
would not go into, it being sufficient if he came within
the rule. It was contended that the Court had no juris-
diction to order any refund on any application made in
this suit, but the cases in re Dangar, and Marsh v.
Joseph, seemed to him decisive authorities to the con-
trary. All sums received by the plaintiff’s solicitor,
therefore, under the arrangement he had described must
be refunded, and the bills of costs brought in by the
nominee solicitors must in any’ case be re-taxed. In fair-
ness to Mr. Heydon it should, however, be pointed out
that in making the arrangement complained of he fol-
lowed a general practice. In his affidavit of August 25,
paragraph 2, Mr. lleydon said : “ The practice is most
general. I have known it to prevail habitually through-
out my 20 years’practice in (Sydney, and it is not con-
fined in any way to minor or unprofessional practitioners,
but is commonly and generally acted on. Of my
personal know'ledge I have known it followed by a
wide range of the offices, including the very highest
in the profession.” Mr. Heydon explained how the
practice had grown up, and his statements were not con-
tradicted. That he thought was sufficient to exonerate
Mr. Heydon from moral blame, although it could not be
used to support a practice which was opposed to equit-
able principles. In his opinion the practice was in-
defensible, and he hoped it would cease for the future.
As the costs were ordered to come out of the estate, it
seemed to him the refund must be to the estate. An
inquiry muse be directed to ascertain what sums the
plaintiff’s solicitor received under the arrangement, un-
less the parties agreed on the amount. As the plaintiff s
solicitor had thought fit to fight the matter, he had no
choice but to make an order that the plaintiffs solicitor
pay the costs oE the application, and the costs incurred
by the taxation which had taken place of the hills of the
solicitors mentioned in the notice of motion. Further
consideration and further costs would be reserved.
Sydney Telegraph and Bathurst National
Advocate and sundry other organs now maintain
that N.S.W. has practically the sole right to
select the Federal capital, and that the other five
States—though they will supply among them most
of the money to build and maintain the capital,
and though it will be as much their political centre
as the political centre of N.S.W.— have no pos-
sible light to any voice in the matter. Of course,
the Federal Parliament will select the site, but
N.S.W. State Parliament can veto its selection by
refusing to surrender the place selected unless it
is one exactly suited to N.S. W.’s interests. Then,
in the ordinary course of things, the Pederal par-
liament will just go on sitting in Melbourne till
an agreement is arrived at. And if N.S.W. State
Parliament won’t fall in with the Federal Parlia-
ments selection business may continue in. Mel-
bourne indefinitely. The Telegraph’s simple
idea of a way out is this :
The Supreme Court would certainly maintain that the
Federal Government must, with all reasonab i expedition,
select a Capital site and establish a seat of Government
in N.S.W.
Unfortunately there is a large hole in this logic,
and if the D.T. sools N.S.W. to insist on its own
selection, and no other, for a Federal capital, it will
lead its followers into the usual ditch. The
Federal Government’s simple answer to the
Supreme Court would be that it had done its duty
and selected a capital site, only N.S.W.
hand over the same. The Supreme Court couldnt
order it to select the site which the JN.S.W.
State Parliament desired it to select, for
there is nothing in the Constitution to that effect.
So the Federal Parliament would stay at Axel-
bourne till the N.S.W. State Legislature changed
its mind. Even if the Supreme Court, without
any legal warrant, did order the Federal Parlia-
ment to select the site N.S.W. offered, it eouldn t
well put the hostile Parliamentary majority in
gaol for contempt if it didn’t obey. And if it did
put the members in gaol the Privy Council would
let them out again by cablegram.

Constance Cl yde in reply t > “ Skotos/’ :
“ Skotos ” wants to keep women disfranchised because
there have been no geniuses among them. He might as
well allege as reasons the fact that they have fewer color-
blind persons among them. A genius is usually a Per-
son with one side of his nature much developed to the
disadvantage of his various other sides. the average
genius, especially the inky one, has generally less voting
sense than an intelligent artizan. “Skotos” wants to
keep women down because they can show no geniuses.
This present writer hereby wants to present a Bill that
the male sex be disfranchised merely because it is that
half of humanity among which those diseased specimens
are mainly found. Woman’s want of genius is the greatest
guarantee of her sense, practicality, and foresight that can
possibly be given. When once she begins produce
geniuses, other than in the sense that Mrs; Shakespeare,
sen., was responsible for William, then it will be necessary
to agitate for her exclusion from all public life. “ Men are
more logical than women.” They are—in conversation.
Men talk logic, but women act it. A man will be most
eloquent on the way to manage the Empire and the
Transvaal war, all the time he is walking about in bulging
boots and slipping out of one situation after another. A
woman plans her life ; her scheme may be very trivial,
and have chiefly to do with the annexing of somebody
called Jack. But the point is that she does not talk elo-
quently about how Jack ought to be annexed ; she.merely
goes up and annexes him—if she can. And when her in-
terests are broadened— as they are broademngever.\ daj
she will carry into public-life her peculiar idiosyncrasy of
knowing just exactly what she is able to do and then
rising up and doing it. Women’s Privileges, Am! the
amusements that “ are ordinarily supplied by the male
bread-winner ” ! Many of the privileges that are loosely
referred to as due to sex are really conferred on
social rank and wealth. Above a certain rank the
women are waited upon; below it they do the
waiting. The latter class is’much the larger. In fact,
it is the nation. The others are exceptions of no numeri-
cal importance. In the vast lower and middle classes,
when a woman gets taken to a theatre, a dance, or on a
holiday visit, it is in return for work done or favor given.
In the lower middle clas9 the girl of the house takes the
place of the kitchen slavey, with the duties of assistant
hostess added, and general dressmaking for the family
thrown in. She gets no wages, but by the splendid
chivalrv of man,” and “ because she is not able to forego
one miserable privilege of her sex,” she is graciously
taken to the theatre three times a year, the brother
nobly paying for her out of his own pocket. She is his
domestic servant, laundress and general attendant all the
year round for mere board, lodging and clothes—and a
very rare trip to the 2s. or Is. department of the melo-
drama. The “ splendid chivalry of man 1

goorlie (W.A.) weekly, is said to be a verbatim
quote from that most amazing Melbourne h ree-
trade organ, the Arena. Tins scribe didn t
happen to notice it in the Arena, but it is just
the sort of thing that is almost sure to be there :
The question of questions for Victorian workers, especi-
ally those blessed with marriageable daughters, is what
to do with the sweet girls. ... For the boys have taken
the matter into their own hands; have left the colon;/,
and now there are not enough here to marry the girl*.
The loss by emigration over immigration during the last
8| years consisted of 101,182 males and 299,737 female .
The young men have gone, and the young women are
condemned to lead lives of single unblessedness. We might
try polygamy, or, as a last resource, perhaps Freetrafie.
Both would be equally efficacious in getting husbands for
our girls.
This is beautiful beyond even the ordinary beauty
of the ordinary Freetrade horror. It sets forth
that about three times as many women as men
have left Victoria, and this huge departure of
women from Victoria has made men so scarce in
Victoria that women in Victoria cant get hus-
bands. The total net departure from Victoria is
alleged to be about 401,000 in 83 years. In the
same period the population of Victoria has in-
creased 40,000. Therefore there must have been
an addition of 440,000 to the population ln
years by excess of births over deaths. In other
words, there must have been about 880,000 births,
and every married woman in Victoria of what is
commonly regarded as the child-bearing age must
have had about six children in 83 years. This is the
most awful case of twins on record. It is a
pretty bad thing to be a liar, but to be an
absolutely unconvincing liar is dreadful. A/iropos,
if Freetrade is a remedy for emigration, how is it
that the United Kingdom has, since it became
Freetrade, lost nearly twice as many people by
emigration as any Protectionist country m
Europe ; that Freetrade Ireland is the only coun-
try in Europe which has lost nearly half its people
by emigration ; and that the European country
which attracts immigrants on a huge scale is not
Freetrade Britain, but Protectionist France,
which, according to Mulhall’s “ Dictionary of
Statistics,” has four times as many foreign resi-
dents as the United Kingdom? Why do people
bolt from Freetrade Britain as if it had a pesti-
lence?

His Honor Judge Hoop, of Victoria, must have
been in a vile mood at tl e Sessions for October,
when he parcelled out no less than 48 years and 7
months among 11 prisoners, in sentences ranging
from 10 years to three months. After ordering
the Black Flat stickers-up 10 and seven years
apiece, his Honor proceeded to deal severely with
lesser offenders. With one man’s protestations
of innocence and assertions of his ability to prove
it if enabled to get his witnesses, the Judge was
so far impressed that he advised him to lay the
facts before the Inspector-General, but instead of
postponing sentence pending investigation he
ordered him to gaol for two years, with 24 days
solitary. To another, who pleaded for the clem-
ency usually shown to first offenders, his Honor
observed that he was a married man living in
adultery with a woman for whom he had
stolen the article, the subject of the charge.
The absurdity of a man living in sin asking for
leniency was too great for words, and he went to
gaol forthwith, to ponder over the painful fact
thathe got into trouble through stealing for his mis-
tress, and that had he been a respectable married
man he would probably have been released as a
first offender. But his Honor reached the height of
illogicality in his remarks while sentencing an old
man of 77 for indecent assault. Said Hood, J. :
“You have twice previously been convicted of
similar offences, in ’95 and ’9B. lam almost in-
clined to think these acts are due to some disease
of the brain. You are sentenced to two years'
imprisonment, and if you weie a younger man I
would have ordered a flogging. '’ Does Judge Hood
really approve of the Hogging of people whom
he is"“ almost inclined ” to regard as lunatics ?
Already Queensland is beginning to feel the teeth
of the private Railway Shindy kite. The rates
and charges on the Hrst section of the Mareeba-
Chillagoe railway have been fixed up between the
co. and the gang"that at present occupies the posi-
tion of the Government of Queensland. The
Mareeba to Chillagoe Railway Act of 1897 pro-
vides that :
The owners? may themselves from time to time fix' by
bv-laws the tolls,hares, rates and charges for the carriage
of goods, live stock, parcels and passengers, provided that
no such tolls, fares, rates or charges shall in any case ex-
ceed one and a-half times the amount payable in respect
of such goods, live stock, parcels or passengers under the
by-laws of the Commissioner in force at the date of the
passing of this Act,
At the date of the passing of this Act l Railway
rates will fall every few years as the country fills
up till they reach the nadir at which a working
profit can be made, but the co s charges can go
on serenely unaltered for ever, or till the line
ceases to be private property! Now the rates
have been fixed up, people find that they are
nearly three times the general rates charged on
the State railways, and the co., with its tongue in
its cheek, explains that they are only 1£ times the
rate charged on Cairns-Mareeba line, of which the
Mareeba-Chillagoe line is a continuation, at the
time of the passing of the Act. Just then the
rates on the Cairns-Mareeba line were special
rates, about 75 per cent, higher than the rates in
general force. This shameless swindle is per-
fectly correct and legal, apparently, and the
kanaka-ridden country finds itself saddled with
another old man of the octopus tribe, who pulls
with all his suckers at once. Private Enterprise,
indeed !

The Case of Dr. Bernstein, J.P.
DISMISSAL RECOMMENDED.
The report of Judge Heydon upon the case of Dr.
Ludwik Bernstein, J.P., who was stated to have, on Oct.
23, 1899, whilst a justice of the peace and holding other
offices under the Crown, at an hotel at Lismore, in the
presence of several persons, used expressions insulting to
the Queen, was last night submitted to the Leg. Assem-
bly. Judge Heydon found that Dr. Bernstein used the
words complained of, with full knowledge of their very
insultino- character, and with an offensive intention.
In mitigation of the offence certain matters were
urged for Dr. Bernstein, and the Judge found that he
had volunteered to go to the front (South Africa),
an act for which he deserved credit; and that he had,
during a residence of 22 years in Lismore, been an active,
public-spirited, and useful citizen, and elected by his fel-
low-citizens to many positions, including a surgeon-cap-
taincy in the volunteers. But a man who was generously
treated bv a community to which he came as a stranger,
and who took the oath of allegiance, came under pecu-
liar obligations of gratitude and consideration for the
feelings of those about him. “ Finding,” Judge Heydon
continues, “ that Dr. Bernstein did, after taking the
oath of allegiance, and while holding positions m the
service of the State, use language in the highest
degree insulting to the head of the State, and that
he used it wilfully, and with an insulting intention,
the question arises, what action should be taken ■ • • •
This matter has obtained great publicity, and has given
grave scandal. The feelings of the residents and the
magistracy of the district have been aroused. Dr. Bern-
stein, though at first in a letter to the Chief Secretary he
expressed regret for what he had done, never did so pub-
licly. On the contrary, he defended himself. ... It
appears to me impossible, under all the circumstances, to
treat the charge, now that it has been found to have been
fully proved, as a matter of small importance. With the
greatest regret, therefore, considering Dr. Bernstein s
many good qualities and his excellent record of public
work, I feel compelled to recommend that he be removed
from the service of the State, whose head he has wilfully
insulted, and dismissed from his appointments under the
Crown.” —S.M. Herald.
Good people all where'er ye be,
Arise and curse this late J.P.
If you for bitter words are lost
Just damn him —never mind the cost!
There’s a point where Scoundreldom
Run mad, Satanic may become.
That point is reached, that base decree,
By Dr. Bernstein— late J.P
His record —and the villain laughs
Is not composed of epitaphs.
For two-and-twenty years lie wrought
Amongst a people void of thought.
With pills and potions, all in vain,
He cleared the turgid local brain,
And made his patients bear witli ease
Their only kind of Height's Disease.
He brought through Birth’s mysterious gate
The embryonic magistrate.
He was a useful citizen,
Approved by all his fellow men.
No man more freely filled the trump
Of Fame about the Lismore pump.
No man on bridges was more free
And fresh and eloquent than he.
'Tis less than nothing, it appears,
This tale of two-and-twenty years.
He used one night in flippant in >od
Some words of black ingratitude.
He said our Great Immortal Queen
Was not as young as she had been,
He also said the Boers had
Some serious reasons to feel mad.
Judge Heydon sat upon the case,
And said it was a dire disgrace.
The crime so much did him appal
He felt hivisdf a criminal.
And he impluxWthat ermined elf —
The name of God was name of Guelph i
And said it was a trouble great
To him to sack a magistrate.
Yet right was right, and fact was fact,
And Dr. Bernstein must be sacked.
His Government appointments he
Would have to lose immediately.
Judge Heydon is too mild and good
To deal with Traitors as he should.
Could he no racks or thumbscrews get
Or find no noisome oubliette?
Can Lismore show no patriots keen
To rig a home-made guillotine ?
Is there no rope ? Is there no tar ?
Or have we grown particular ?
Is there no fire to but'a with glee
This fiend—at least in effigy?
Of loyalty in vain ye prate
Oil Lismore folk degenerate !
Had ye but felt as ye should feel
He would be broken on a wheel.
Judge Heydon even tried to spare
This monster with a record fair.
Good people all, where’er ye be,
Arise and curse this late J.P, .

of teachers in denominational schools as it has to
pay them indirectly by a, system of scholarship
subsidies. The proceeding was an eminently
crooked one and deserving of the strongest repro-

secure a favorable verdict from the electors let
them introduce an Amending Act; and, above
all, let them avoid this miserable juggling in
camera with the rightful law of the land.
