“ Robbery Under Arms ” revived at
Sydney Criterion on Saturday with the usual
amount of shooting, one man in his excitement
fatally wounding a tree, which fell to the earth,
turning up its root to the audience in a pathetic
manner. As usual, almost every act ends with
the bushrangers covering the policemen with
their revolvers. These latter, during a long course
of bushranger-hunting, evidently never remember
to go into the fray with their weapons to hand.
Several horses meander about the stage, or are
introduced to the audience as my dear old Rain-
bow, or something to that effect, whereupon the
audience cheers tremendously. For the rest
“Robbery Under Arms” continues to be the
good old story of the persecuted robbers, who,
when they were caught and prevented from rob-
bing any more, nobly repented, and resolved not
to rob any more. Alfred Dampier takes his old
part of the high-minded bushranger who never
Steals from the poor, probably because the poor
man hasn’t anything worth stealing, while J ohn
Bruce is natural as the lower-class respectable
type of forest robber. Miss Katherine Russell
is excellent as the bad woman, who has remorse
after she has done the mischief, while a good word
may be said for Howard Smith as Warn gal.
** * *
To-night (Thursday) “ Hedda Gabler,” by
that long-haired Polar-bear person, Henrik Ibsen,
is to make its first Sydney appearance at Her
Majesty’s. “Hedda” is announced for two nights
only, which, for the first production of a much-
advertised drama by the longest-haired dramatist
in Christendom, is something unusual. Evidently
Williamson has his own hidden opinion about the
Man with the Mane, and regards him as good for
a brief sprint, but devoid of staying power. Any-
how, Ibsen isn’t going to be allowed to accumu-
late on the premises. “School for Scandal”
starts on Saturday, and fills three nights and a
matinee, and “Queen Elizabeth” begins to
perish miserably on the floor on Wednesday. In
the meantime Floria Tosea is still addressing her
Plimmer on the cold battlements of Rome at day-
break, and urging his dead body to get up and fly
for its life, and Plimmer doesn’t get up worth
** # *
Miss Lottie Collins’ kangaroo-like bound-
ings continue to arouse enthusiasm at Sydney
Tivoli, though her most original performance, the
one most calculated to set her apart from the
ruck, is that of the “Coalman’s Wife.” Miss
Jessie Williams combines dancing and gymnastics
in the fashion that is approved of in these athletic
days, while Miss Tillie Rochester’s voice deserves
better than to be thrown away on the worn-
out coon song, wherein the coon’s mamma is con-
tinually requesting her honey babe to sleep.
The curious point about the Rickards show is that
when one great star breaks through the gloom
numerous smaller stars break through also, where-
as if these latter were judiciously saved for the
dull nights, the Tivoli would show a good
average, instead of passing through weeks of
dreariness as it frequently does. At present
under the good influence of Kangaroo Lottie, the
performance shines from eight till half-past ten,
except for one shouting lady who is dressed like a
bee and is likewise a fly in the ointment.
** * *
The above few remarks represent all the
show business there is in Sydney this week. The
Royal, Palace, and Lyceum are closed ; the man
with the strange collection of diseased heads and
the drunkard’s liver on a plate left some time ago;
and business is dull because some people are at
the Cup, and the rest are pretending to be there
but are really in concealment at Newtown.
*m * *
At Melb. Princess’s the course of grand
opera runs smooth, save for occasional jolts in
the performance. ' Last week the “Faust’’cast
was varied interestingly. One night Salvi, Miss
Lorraine and Miss Janson replaced the previous
Faust, Marguerite and Siebel, with the result that
the opera looked more important. Miss Cecilie
Lorraine is not a timid, little, shrinking Marguerite
of the appealsome pattern that Miss Coomber
presented. In point of fact, the bonnie blonde
lady is rather the sort of Margy who lets her hat
blow off at a picnic and sends three young men to
fish it out of the creek, but she sang much of the
music charmingly and put some nice bits of
color into the al fresco flirtation in the
second act. Salvi, as Faust, was an all-
round triumph compared with the other tenor.
Also, the Devil had an improvement forced upon
him on this occasion. He fell suddenly ill before
the curtain went up, and the substitute Devil in
the first act (Gilbert King) wore a beard, so when
the original fiend came along and took up the
running, he wore hair on his face for the first time,
and was all the wickeder and stronger for the
ohange. There’s a lot of evil suggestion in a
“goatee,” seemingly. During the week Madame
Slapoffski appeared as Leonora and Marguerite,
and the local press sang her praises somewhat too
sweetly. Madame Slapoffski has not too sweet a
voice herself. Moreover she drags the music in
her effort to put expression into her singing, some
of which expression is ultra-dramatic. But, all
things considered, the variations in the “Faust ”
cast were good for the opera season.
• • • •
“Carmen,” on Saturday, had pleasant
surprises in some places and disappointments in
others. Salvi, as Don Jose, proved himself a
really good actor. To be a good actor with a full
Italian tenor voice that doesn’t go wrong every
time the weather changes is to command the
respect of this country. Moreover, Salvi is not
much too plump for the character of a love-struck
sergeant. He looks equal to the task of winning
Carmen from his superior officer, and in all
respects he fills the bill. Miss Janson, the excellent
Azucena of “II Trovatore” lacks the first essential
for Carmen. She doesn’t give an even half-con-
vincing picture of the irresistible inan-killer. The
public, who look for a tuneful, fascinating girl,
rather than a solid contralto vocalist in this part,
will not be enthusiastic over Miss Janson. Tall
Max Eugene, as the Toreador, suggests the Lord
Mayor’s coachman masquerading in fancy dress.
An artful little beard or a moustache might modify
the suggestion considerably as regards his face,
but nothing could make such a heavy-weight bari-
tone look likely to earn renown by dodging
infuriated bulls in the arena. He’d be disem-
bowelled at his first bulky meeting with the
four-legged beef. Still, there is a tremendousness
about Max Eugene which would commend him to
the affections of a Carmen. Little Miss Lilian
Coomber is a likeable Michaela, and among the
minor characters whose acting in this opera is of
greater business importance than their singing,
the two smugglers are particularly well presented
by Gilbert King and our old friend Charles Leu-
mane. The chorus and the staging score heavily.
If there is any fault to find with the orchestra,
under the clever management of Slapoffski, it is
in the tone of some of the new brass instruments.
On Thursday of this week “ Lohengrin ” will be
produced, for the first time in Melbourne since
the era of manager Musgrove’s late uncle, W. S.
Lyster.
* * # *
J. C. Williamson has now fulfilled his
promise of a commanding frontage for Her
Majesty’s (Melb.). The old-time Alexandra
Theatre, where odours of fried fish used to steal
in gently at the end of the first act and fight for
supremacy with the perfume of steak and onions,
was made bright six months ago. On Saturday
the house was born again, with new entrance
lobbies and a fine suite of ante-rooms. The fish-
shops and steak-emporiums are gone for ever, and
all the comforts of an airy show-place have been
provided in their stead. Great is the goodness of
theatrical competition. One of these days, per-
haps, somebody will devote a few thousand
pounds to the glory of the ancient Royal, and will
grow rich by the enterprise.
* • * *
“ The Rose of Persia,” which is the re-
opening bill at Her Majesty’s, received its bap-
tism of praise in The Bulletin three months ago.
Arthur Sullivan has put some of his daintiest
music into the score; music that will still be worth
hearing when its freshness has departed. Not all
the airs are new in spirit. Reminders of
“ Mikado” crop up among the barbaric splendour
of the Persian potentate, but, then, nobody objects
to a mingling of Sullivan past with Sullivan
present, any more than they object to the Gil-
bertian character of Capt. Basil Hood’s libretto.
Hood is not Gilbert, but his imitation of Gilbert
on “Mikado” lines has more merit than the
average comic-opera story. “The Rose of
Persia ” has nothing much the matter with it as a
musical story. Its fault is a negative one. The
characters lack distinction. They are too like the
people who populate pantomimes.
* * • *
Lauri, as the husband of 25 wives and a
general benefactor of his kind, is the main joke in
the narrative. He gets drunk on bhang, and
cheeks the Sultan, who garbs him in a little brief
authority, and lets him run the kingdom in his
bhangful mind until the humor of the situation is
fairly exhausted. The other characters, including
Sultan Brownlow, are very subordinate to
Lauri. The men offer rather too many Persian
greetings to one another, and the ladies excite no
particular interest at the first time of seeing them,
anyhow. “ Rose of Persia ” owes its success to a
general effectiveness in the production—flowing
melody, fine dresses, strong chorus, and a first-
class ballet. The show is always good, and often
gorgeous.
* * * *
At the Royal they are winding up the
Anderson season with a wildly preposterous
drama of the “ Missing Will ” class. The one
novel circumstance in this deplorable horror is a
casual maniac who lost his reason through “ the
treacherie of a woman together with socialistic
tendencies,” it appears, and in the course of his
howling madness this unfortunate Republican
tries to decapitate the heroine with a toy guillo-
tine which works by steam. “ The World’s Ver-
dict ” is either the positively worst, or the worst
but one, drama on record at the Royal. Friday
will bring it to an unhonored end.
* # * *
On Saturday, the long-looked-for Bland
Holt will return to the Royal, with the full
strength of his forces. “The Absent-Minded
Beggar” is the first drama in Bland’s plan of cam-
paign, and, from casual notes of expectation that
are uttered by citizens in the street, tram and
railway carriage, The Bulletin anticipates a
huge welcome for the warlike fiction aforesaid.
** * *
A bare mention of the fact that Rickards’s
Bijou is open as usual will suffice for the present
week. The Derby and Cup are drawing nigh and
when Melbourne gets horse on its brain, the house
of Rickards turns money away every evening.
The other circus (Wirth’s) starts in Flinders-street
next Saturday (3rd) and sundry other things inci-
dental to Cup week are happening at various
places in the Melbourne district.
** * *
Fitzgeralds’ Circus, which drew an enor-
mous crowd on its opening night (Sat., Oct. 27th),
has pitched its tents this year on the soft green
grass of the Exhibition Oval. Rhodesia, the
light-fingered juggleress, deals deftly and daintily
with billiard-balls, &c., and looks especially
womanly , and domesticated twirling and tid-
dley-winking piles of plates. Madame An-
tonio, the sensational high-flyer who falls
backwards from the roof and somersaults
from giddy heights, is a living proof that
if a lady feels she could relish fire-brigade
practice her sex in itself need not bar her from
her goal. The Jandaschevskys’ “Matador”
dance seems rather slow and stately, considered
as an imitation of adventures with a goaded mad-
dened bull. But Clown Guillaume’s comic bull-
fight with trick dog “Black” is really funny.
“ Black ” disguised as a pigmy bull, with absurd
little horns, rushes between the toreadors’ legs,
upsets them like ninepins, rampages like an in-
sane billy-goat, and scuds from the ring ere
laughing spectators have seen enouarh of him.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Some notable remarks by Premier Seddon on
the second reading of the Maoriland Consols Bill
“ I look forward to the time, and I do not think it will
be very far hence, when the Government will be able to
use State paper for paying State claims. . . . We are
yearly sending out of the province a large sum to pay
interest. No doubt there is a large sum of money in the
province available, but there is no machinery to get at it.”
There is only one possible answer to that; the Govern-
ment should provide the machinery. In the last ten
years there has never been less than £2,500,000 in gold
lying idle in the Maoriland banks. That is the money
that is not in circulation. The country wants gold to
pay external liabilities, but this is not wanted to pay
external liabilities. It is the idle and unprofitable surplus.
For internal purposes £500,000 in gold and £2,000,000 in
guaranteed State notes would answer quite as well; the
notes would lie idly in the banks’ cellars quite as capably
as the gold does. And that £2,000,000 which is lying in
the banks quite uselessly is costing Maoriland £70,000
a year. It has cost £700,000 in the last 10
years; £1,400,000 in the last 20 years. All that
money has been paid for the mere satisfaction of knowing
that the gold is there, but never seeing it, nor using it,
nor handling it. For if the Maoriland Government
adopted the Canadian banking system, and compulsorily
replaced £2,000,000 of the gold reserves with State paper,
and made it compulsory for all banks to hold a fixed pro-
portion of their cash reserves in State paper, then it could
have paid off £2.000,000 of the public debt with the pro-
ceeds and saved £70,000 a year in interest. By not doing
so the existence of these unused reserves does cost Maori-
land, quite uselessly, £70,000 a year. It is a ridiculous
sum to waste.
• • * •

The National Bank has thus reserves amounting to
£66,737 (reserve fund, £55,000, and undivided profit,
£11,737). The earnings for last half-year are at the rate of
£3 12s. 3d. per cent, per annum on capital, or £3 9s. Bd.
per cent, per annum on the capital and reserve-fund.
The National Bank’s earnings, despite the recent exten-
sive writing down of capital and the relief obtained by
the repayment of the expensive 4* per cent, reconstructed
deposits, are still only moderate, and compare as follows
with those of its principal local rival, the Bank of Vic-
toria :

Earnings on capital .. ..£3 12/3 p.c. £3 17/4 p.c.
The National Bank has undoubtedly a splendid
business. Its deposits now amount to about £6,000,000
and the reserve of cash and liquid securities (£3,154,323)
is equal to over 40 per cent, on all liabilities to the public ;
which is something far beyond the average in the way of
solidity. Included in this sum is £736,000 worth of State
and municipal securities. But there is a great deal to do
yet in the way of rebuilding the lost reserves, and it
would probably have been better if the management had
started its dividend on ordinary shares at 21 per cent, and
left a wider margin with which to strengthen the
institution.

this basis, however, the United is a very strong and pro-
gressive institution, and the regularity with which it
earns its big dividend shows good management.

St. Joseph’s Investment and Building Society
(Sydney) issues its balance-sheet for year ended 30th Sep-
tember, 1900. This concern suspended in 1892, and in
1895 it made an arrangement with its depositors by which
they agreed to accept 3 per cent, interest. Deposits are
paid off by instalments as cash becomes available. St.
Joseph’s wrote off £42,880 of capital in 1895, reducing it to
£28,588, at which figure it now stands. The record of the
last three years is as follows :
Balance.
Sept., 1897 .. £377
Profits.

„ 1899 .. 2,617 „ „ .. 2,600
„ 1900 .. 2,985 „ „ .. 3,000
Balance .... 264
£9,364 £9,364
Fixed deposits (that top-heavy mass of deposits which
first brought St. Joseph’s to grief) were £285,000 in 1892 ;
£180,541 in 1895 ; £110,891 in 1898 ; £105,438 in 1899, and
they are now only £98,627. The reduction is therefore
very large and continuous.
For the last three years the income of St.
Joseph’s Investment and Building Society has shown the
following return on the paper value of its total assets —
interest-bearing and otherwise :
1898 .. ..£6 2 6 percent.
1899 .. .. 5 10 11 „
1900 .. .. 5 16 9
These are not very bad returns as things go nowadays,
and, unless there is some cause for despondency which
doesn’t appear in the accounts, it is difficult to under-
stand why the reports are written in quite such a de-
pressed frame of mind. The Langridge Building Society of
Melbourne, which realises about £4 15s. 6d. on the alleged
net value of its investments (the value after deduction of
the depreciation fund) has proudly resumed the payment
of dividends, and congratulates itself on being fairly over
its difficulties. Undoubtedly there is still depreciation to
be provided for, and the persistent writing down of
securities by the St. Joseph’s management is a wise and
most commendable proceeding. Still, if the alleged in-
come is all cash actually received, and not to any great
extent merely interest charged against advances without
any certainty that the said interest will be collected, the
institution should be reasonably near to the end of its
difficulties, provided no new slump sets in.

The Mutual Fire Insurance Co. of Tasmania,
now 26 years old, only commenced last year to publish
income and expenditure accounts, so it is only possible
to make a detailed comparison of its progress for two
years:—
Pi-nfifa Total Reserve Dividends and
at each Date. Dividend Tax.

The profit for 1900 as shown above includes a mysterious
and unexplained addition to the balance at credit of con-
tingency fund, which seems to have been growing un-
accountably in the night. The reserves include reserve
fund, £12,000 ; contingency fund, £3073; re-insurance
reserve, £2392; and undivided profit balance, £3821.
Paid-up capital is £37,500. The income from invest-
ments (£2902) is sufficient by itself to pay
a 7 per cent, dividend, which is a rather unusual
state of affairs, and the only visible bad feature in the
case is that the business, which was always of a small but
remarkably safe character, appears, while still remaining
safe, to be growing still smaller. The whole premium
income for year ended 30th September, 1900, was £4785,
so the profit nearly amounted to the whole premiums,
interest earnings being almost sufficient to cover losses
and expenses
* * * *
Westralia, on present appearances, will have to
find £5,000,000 within the next 15 or 18 months to keep
some big unfinished public works going, and to settle the
temporary loans with which Bigjohn staved off the evil
day. These short-dated loans were floated at high inte-
rest, because things were bad, and it seems likely that
they will fall due when things are worse. When Forrest
throws up the weary sponge in a few months, as he pro-
mises to do, he will leave a fearsome load for his suc-
cessor.

The Austral Otis Engineering Co. have just
completed a concentrating test on the Wilfley Table
installed at their South Melbourne works of a parcel o
crushed ore representing the second product from the
concentrating mill at the Conrad mine, owned by Mr. J.
Howell, which hitherto has all been stacked on the mm ■
waiting the advent of a machine that would profitably
treat it without re-crushing. The result of the Wilfle\
test is a high-grade concentrate, and tailingwaste show -
ing very little values, and the Otis Co. have despatched
the tables for erection on the mine.*

The Bill abolishing imprisonment for debt has
now passed both Houses of M. L. Parliament; only in case'
of contract-penalties or anything in the nature of fran
dulent debt can a debtor be “jugged” in future. Much
rejoicing of the Micawbers, and wailing and rending of
garments among the usurers.

On paper there should be something left for the share-
holders and no necessity for further calls, though book
values are very uncertain. The fact that the earnings
last year got almost within gunshot of the expenses is
encouraging, in a way. Things certainly look much
better than they did in ’9B, when the gross income was
£2 and working expenses £344. Two pounds is a depress-
ing sum for any company to earn in twelve months.
* * * *
Tooth and 00., Limited, brewers (Sydney),
showed a gross profit of £38,951 for the half-year ended
30th September, 1900, and after deducting £2500 for
depreciation of plant, the net profit was £36,451. The
usual 7 per cent, dividend absorbed £31,500, and £5OOO
was carried to a reserve for equalisation of dividends.
Tooth and Co.’s is one of the rare concerns which has no
mortgage, lien, overdraft, or debenture liability weighing
it down. The paid-up capital is £900,000, and total re-
serves are now £115,795, of which £99,349 is separately
invested in State and other securities.

Total reserves at June, 1895, were £5347 (reserve fund,
£5250 ; and undivided profit, £97). They are now £14,556
(reserve fund, £14,250, and undivided profit, £306), so
the increase in five years amounts to £9209, though
only £5459 of this is actual trading profit. The capital is
only £27,497, so reserves are very large in proportion.
But the Wellington Building and Investment Co. carries
a very heavy load of deposits (£91,641, or nearly 2) times
the shareholders’ funds); and, as it has £119,034 invested
in mortgages, it might be greatly embarrassed if bad
times fell on Wellington, and heavy withdrawals set in.
A curious feature is a steadily-growing item among the
agse ts—“ Investment by Company, £8652.” For all the
information supplied this amount might be State securities,
or dredging shares, or Tattersall’s sweep-tickets, or almost
anything. ,
“War God”: (1) Personally, writer wouldn’t
care to touch it. (2) Debentures are a written engage-
ment by a co. to pay certain amounts at a certain time,
with interest (payable yearly or half-yearly) at a certain
rate. They are a debt secured on the paid and callable
capital and on all the assets.
r * * * *
Considering its size and the value of its output
White Cliffs opal field is the most unscientifically-worked
district in N.S.W. Most of the best country was taken
up under mineral-lease. The lessees let small blocks on
tribute. A tributor merely sinks a pot hole and gouges
out in various directions at different levels reputed to be
opal-bearing. There is no timbering either of shafts
(except in very rare cases) or of drives. Fortunately it is
remarkably good standing ground, and so accidents have
been very rare ; but, as in most soft country, the work-
ings don’t improve with age, and subsidences are bound
to be numerous in the future. The first on a big scale has
just occurred ; luckily nobody was underneath at the time.
But “Accident at White Cliffs” is going to be a com-
moner newspaper heading than it has been hitherto.
* * * *
After a fearful slump in the opal market, last-
ing six months or more, White Cliffs reports a better
demand again for the gem. *
R. N. Thomas, manager of the Junction North,
leaves the mine at the end of the year, or earlier. It is a
good while since the public had confidence in either mine
or stock, and this change will not tend to improve matters
in this respect.
* * * *
One after the other the B.H. Prop., which in
the dull days pegged out most of the abandoned shows
round Broken Hill, lets out the most promising blocks to
speculators for flotation. The Victoria Cross co., having
been put on its feet after a big struggle, the White Lead
will now be tried. The question is whether the B.H.
lode continues even after the outcrop disappears ; if it
does—and there is certainly ore in irregular bodies on the
flat country beyond the outcrop—the White Lead should
get it.
b * • • •
Silver has passed the half-crown and lead is a
rattling price ; and that is probably why there is more
dissatisfaction among the Broken Hill mining em-
ployes than for years past. The men at the smelting
works in S.A. are grumbling most; over 600 of them have
asked the B.H. Prop, for an all-round rise of a shilling a
shift. But they have no organisation to back them. Port
Pirie, where most of the smelting is done, was never an
Eden ; but since the furnaces were built there it has
become about the most unwholesome and uncomfortable
spot to live in in all Australia. South Australia wasn’t
happy till it got the smelting business ; and now it has
got it it permits workmen and people to he poisoned with-
out an attempt at the regulation upon which, for some
years before the furnaces were shifted from Broken Hill,
N.S.W. Health Department insisted.
» « * *
It will not surprise readers of a recent par.
printed here to learn that the zinc works at Broken
Hill have stopped. Reasons given are the big rise
in ocean freights (all the product had to be shipped
to Europe to be smelted, &c.) and the bigger rise in the
price of coke —and these refractory ores use up a great
lot of fuel. The only surprising thing about the official
announcement is that the Australian Metal Co., which
was always understood to be simply an offshoot from the
German co. dealing in sulphides, sets out that it has been
“ managing the works for a foreign syndicate. It is
claimed that, though the process already adopted has
been “ a great technical success of late, two new mag-
netic separators, the co.’s own property, are to be further
experimented with. , , *
Block 14 shuts down one of its five smelting
furnaces at Port Adelaide, and explains that its auction is
due to the rise in the coke-market, and the lately in-
creased rail-freights. Block 14 is a low grade mine ;
moreover, it has led the way in experiments on refrac-
tory sulphides and mill products. The shutting-down of
a furnace does not mean the reduction of profits by a
fifth; it only means that a quantity of stuff "'hjcri for-
merly just paid exs. will no longer be treated. This ore
as a rule requires more labor than the less refractory stuff,
hence more workmen are affected by the stopping of the
treatment of this particular ore than of the richest and
best ore in the mine. The work-people are, therefore,
more interested than the shareholders are in reducing
freight charges low enough to allow the stuff to be
worked without loss. The S.A. Govt, and the Laborites
who keep it in office see, however, that the great bulk of
the B.H. ore carried can very well afford to pay the in-
creased freight; and so they naturally hesitate to approve
of an all-round reduction. A sliding scale is badly
needed.
* * * *
B.H. Junction shareholders have not yet paid
the full penalty for rooting out the rich sulphides so fast
four years ago that they had not time to fill up the holes
they made. Every part of the main workings seems to
have been so weakened by the collapse which followed
that local “ slides ” are constantly occurring. The latest,
in mid-October, is the most serious for 12 or 18 months.
And it comes, too, just when the co. thought it was
firmly on its feet again. t
Though the B.H. Prop, has been silenced by
concessions, other B.H. co’s will continue-the fight against
B H. Water Supplv monopoly. The Sulphide Corpora-
tion, South, and Block 14 are openly hostile, and have
undertaken to guarantee a certain minimum consumption
from anv Govt, reservoir at 3s. 6d. per 1000 gal. ihe
total amount guaranteed in this way would more than
pay estimated working expenses and interest on esti-
mated capital cost of the Umberumberka scheme. It is
doubtful, though, whether the latter is big enough ; the
Works Committee is now inquiring. Broken Hill mining
now depends absolutely on ore-concentration ; ore-con-
centration depends absolutely on water ; and for water
B.H. depends absolutely on the one fast-silting-up dam
across a single creek, which runs for an hour or two only
after heavy rain. Nothing is surer, therefore, than that
a second scheme must come. Tired of waiting for the
people to agitate for it, the mining co’s are now engineer-
ing the movement for a Govt, supply.
s * * * *
The sulphide problem solved once more. No
doubt about it this time. Says Sydney!). T: “ Mr. R. J.
Horne of Gulf Creek, via Barraba, N.S.W., writes . I
have thoroughly solved the zinc problem, the new process
extracting the zinc in 15min. and leaving the other metals
as a residuum. The patent, which has to be registered
throughout the world by myself and partner, Mr. W.
Trewenack, of Gulf Creek, has been sold to a wealthy
Corporation for a large sum. It will completely revolu-
tionise the process at present in use, being original, cheap
and simple—the great desideratum of modern metal-
lurgy.’ ” If Trewenack endorses this yarn there may be
something in it. + *
Girilambone : Some more tall newspaper talk
and managerial gas about the Broker’s Joy. ai y
assays give an average of 5 per cent, copper. On Friday
the black ore lode was struck, and gave nearly 10 per-
cent. copper. Between the black ore zone and some other
zone there is a 13ft. 6in. body, the whole of which will
average from 5 to 6 per cent, copper.’ The barren
winze has been found to be sunk under the ore, and the
manager is of opinion that the body of sulphide ore is the
same as that at the back of the winze, and they will have
150 ft. of hacks in consequence. The S.E. drive has gone
74ft in ore, and the face looks as well as ever. Samples
taken from the trucks gave an average of 5 per cent,
copper.” It is long since the furnaces did any smelting,
and, unfortunately, newspapers’ and ma - . -ors reports
produce no copper. Seeing that the plant h is been shut
down for about a year it would be a fair thing for . fc! ' e
Girilambone management to shut up until it is re-started.
* * * *
Turon Dredge —the favorite of the Sabbath
tipster. “ It is from No. 1 dredge, however, that the big
return is anticipated and between this and Christmas we
hope to find the present record of 750 z. lowered more
than once.” Yes—and it hasn’t been long about it. It
was lowered this last week to 20oz. for 113 hours work,
the total output for the two dredges for the week being
only 580 z. —“ There is nothing in the mining list offering
such inducements to purchasers as Turon shares at about
30s. and were it not for the peculiar stagnation of the
share market just now these shares would stand neaier
their true value of £4 or £5.” There are only 48,000
shares in the Turon Co. At £5 the valuation then would
he little less than a quarter of a million, and the enor-
mous interest that 58oz. of gold per week will pay on a
quarter of a million of money, deducting a fair thing for
expenses and wear and tear, is quite clear. It would
work out something less than 1 per cent., which is of
course far more than ample for any dredging investment.
Milburn Creek (*N.S.W.)t The Melbourne
crowd decided last week to wind up. Danvers Powers,
writing to S.M. Herald, contradicts the statement that
the ore-bodies are cut out. “As a matter of fact ore
was showing in every face where the workings were in
the ore tunnel —viz., at the bottom of the shaft, bottom
of two winzes, and at the ends of No. 1,2, 3 and 4 levels.
It is only in those workings hopelessly off the lode where
there is no ore showing.” Well, the co. has spent £lO,OOO
in plant alone, and a great deal besides in unwatering,
opening-out and prospecting this mine. For a time pros-
pects were so good that they were almost certain of exer-
cising the option, and is it likely, after spending so much,
that everything would be abandoned for the sake of
£lO,OOO, 6 the price of the mine? The Melb. crowd in
question knows too much for that—the reputable Mr.
Danvers Power to the contrary notwithstanding. ‘ Ore
was showing in every face ” is not quite definite enough.
* * * # *
Cobar : Eldorado mine bagging ore worth from
Goz to 20oz. gold and 70oz. silver to the ton. The Drys-
dale mine we are told, has struck some more wonderfully
rich ore. At present it is impossible to say what quan-
tity there is of it, hut appearances point to there being a
considerable body. That in the Eldorado is making
towards the Drysdale mine.” A few weeks ago, it is said,
it was the other way about. These two mines must not
he confounded with those in the immediate vicinity of
flohnr They are over 20 miles away, on a distinct line of
country, and distinguished by entirely different charac-
teristics Five-sixths of the Drysdale gold -has been got
from a pipe of ore from 10ft. to 15ft. long and very narrow,
which has gone down almost vertically to 300 ft. There is
no prospect of this particular pipe going into the adjoining
mine, which has signs of one of its own Outside of
this pipe, in the Drysdale as well as in the Eldorado,
bunches and bodies of lower-grade ore have been milled,
but they are erratic in value a 3 well as in their course.
A winze is going down below the bottom level in the
Drysdale mine on the rich pipe, and as this is done
before the shaft is sunk, it is practically an underhand
stope robbing the mine of the rich ore before the shaft
gets down to it. The two mines should amalgamate,
and expend at least £lO,OOO in a new shaft and plant.
By that means the two perhaps would become one per-
manent div.-paying mine, but as sure as the present
gutting-out policy is followed so sure will Drysdale meet
another bad time, and the shares of each mine will fall
to a less number of pence than they are now shillings.

Gibraltar Consolidated, N.S.W. —Re-construc-
tion effected in London. New capital over-subscribed.
Chairman Hoffnung said the stopes had not yielded
enough ore for the battery’s capacity, nor had quality
improved. Three months’ production—39lo tons, 2188 oz.;
value, £9933. Mining costs, £7900 in addition to capital
expenditure, that of July and Aug. alone amounting to
£2130. It was a mistake to put up so big a crushing-
plant to deal with so small a vein.
* * * •
Grassy Gully, N.S.W. —Said that the battery is
doing 50 per cent, better work. “ No improvement in
the yield of gold, however, is looked for, as the material
now being put through includes a lot of dirt which was
taken out of the side of the hill when making a pad-
dock.” ’Twas ever thus. No such excuses when the mill
started.
» * * *
Associated : “When first cut, the ore body at
600 ft. in the crosscut connecting the two main shafts
gave values of over an ounce to the ton. Latest assays
show only about half an ounce, although at the same
time the ore carries a tremendous quantity of silver.”
A few days before it was a “ very high percentage ” of
silver. But lOOozs. to the ton would not be a tremendous
quantity, nor would 1 per cent., which would be several
hundred ounces to the ton. Some more changes in con-
templation. Latest is that Hewitson will stay in London
as managing-director, and that Liveing will take the
mine’s general management. “ Development work in
other parts of the mine proceeding as .usual,
but the men have all been taken out of the
stopes in consequence of the treatment - plant
being unable to cope with the amount of stuff sent to
surface; in fact the sulphide plant has been behaving
very badly for a long time past, and its thorough over-
hauling is the only means by which the Associated will
ever be able to make ends meet.” Thus endeth the story
of the magnificent sulphide plant. Bad enough, Heaven
only knows, but it is riot half as bad as the mine, which
has been gutted of its good ore. Spent enormous sums in
development work without finding any more, and practic-
ally wasted a quarter of a million in a plant which at last
is thus described.
* • * •
Lake View Consols.—“ The rumor of cutting
the lode at 700 ft. level proves to be without foundation.”
Time something was definitely known as to the value of
this mine below 400 ft. The local general impression is
that the 400 ft. level was none too good, the 500 poor, the
600 unpayable, and the hope of getting anything good
before the lode goes into the Golden Link is not very
bright. .
* * * *
Golden Link.—“ The ore body at 930 ft. level
continues to improve, the face at present carrying values
well over an ounce. The shaft is now down 959 ft., and in
very broken country. In fact, it has been broken from
800 ft. down.” That'is one of the many troubles of the
Golden Link. It has always been in broken country. For
the last six months the Eclipse, a lease outside of the
Golden Mile, has been the shareholders’ great consolation.

Fremantle averaged lOoz. 16dwt. per ton, value £28,210 ;
profit, £2s,lss—the difference being freight and treat-
ment charges. Probably nothing allowed for mining. A
big mine, if it can keep going like that, which it un-
doubtedly won’t. In the face of this magnificent return,
shares have fallen 10s., and that means a drop of £175,000.
♦ • * *
Brown Hill (Kalgoorlie): The British Aus-
tralasian having boomed these shares when they were
over £8 to go much higher, and finding them at about
half the price they were a few months before, now wants
to know why the directors remain silent; while one H.
Marshall writes to the press to inform the public “ that,
as a matter of fact, this mine is played out, and that he
knows that the reef has suddenly disappeared.” If a lie,
the B.A. wants to know what sort of punishment could
be meted out to the man who made the statement, and
adds that, if true, it is clearly the duty of the directors to
be frank with the shareholders. No punishment sug-
gested for the directors. Oh, no ! They have been long
aware of the fact that the mine was rapidly exhausting
its oxidised ore. The payable oxidised ore remaining in
the propertv could he accurately measured a year ago ;
this was well known to every workman, and must surely
have been known to the management. It has also been
well known that the sulphide zone is treacherous and
precarious—so well known that thousands of scrip were
oversold six months ago, and a number of “ bears ” have
been ruined by their temerity.
The Brownhill management so concealed the
condition of the mine from the public and so much
money has been spent in bringing about a “bear
squeeze ” that shares went to £lO instead of going to £5,
and as much as 3s. 6d. per share a fortnight has been paid
as carrying-over rates. What this business cost the co.
will probablv never be known, but some day it will be
found out what it has paid in acquiring absolutely worth-
less blocks. One, the True Blue, was worked by an Eng-
lish co. until it was regarded as a duffer. Shares were
sold down to a few shillings. Then it was suddenly
acquired by the Brownhill co., payment in shares, most of
which the nominees of a well-known mining firm had the
pleasure of receiving. The Princess Louise, another side-
on block, was not worth prospecting and is never likely
to be. The Federal is also off the line. Some day, when
the public are too tired to listen and most of the victims
of this fake are dead, the business will be cleaned up and
the easy way in which fortunes fell into the lap of some
people will be made known.
* * ¥ •
Hannan’s Eureka, W.A. : “The prospects of
this mine are still considered good, but the funds are
low, and the directors are applying for exemption, as the
time is not considered opportune for further call-making.”
Then why not shut up the darned mine ? Like many
another in its vicinity, it should never have been opened,
but as that cannot be helped, the next best thing is to
close it.
* * * *
“Moses Moss, ” of London Truth, is a most
lamentable tipster. A few more of Moses’ recent mis-
takes. Five weeks or so ago he commenced his “ Golden
Westralian ” article : “ There is no doubt now that the
improvement in the W.A. market has come to stay.” Yet
within ten days the collapse occurred, and stocks gener-
ally have been from 20 to 30 per cent, lower ever since.
He predicts great things for the Diehl process. “ Under
these circumstances it is not surprising that Lake View
Consols have risen to 14J. It is expected moreover that
they will rise at least two points within the next few
day's.” Instead of that they fell £2 10s. a share within a
few days and are about that much lower now. “ Brown-
hill shares have, curiously enough, experienced a relapse
and are down to £7 16s. Yet another dividend of 7s. 6d.
per share has been declared.” Curiously enough Brown-
hill shares have since fallen another £3 a share. Two
weeks before they fell to £7 they were £8 12s. 6d.,
and then Moses 'strongly advised his readers to buy
them as they were going to £l2 within a few
wee ks—instead of which they stand under 90s. to-day.-
“ Peak Hills have been at a steady market for £4 55., arid
there are whispers of a coming advance in these.” The
public will do well to keep off M.M.’s whispers. Take this
one as a sample: “There are indications that we sb".!l
shortly see a considerable improvement in some of the
low-priced shares; amongst these I hear that Paringr.s
are likely to go ahead, and they have already hardened to
Bb. 3d. per 10s. share, and should go considerably
higher. ” Of course, they have gone lower, and they are
not worth as many pence as they are shillings. Moses
Moss should soon reach the public estimation of a certain
Sabbath tipster.

Collie Coal, W.A.: Two or three new seams
have been found in the deeper ground of the Wallsend
colliery-one 17ft. thick. “ The discovery is regarded as
of immense importance to the whole field. Undoubtedly
—provided the coal is of a different character to that
worked, a good domestic and steaming coal, but contain-
ing too much moisture and sulphur for shipping pur-
poses. The W.A. Wallsend colliery alone will soon be in
a position to supply more coal than the present consump-
tion of W.A., but, if a good shipping coal is found, there
will be plenty room for other collieries. The latest addi-
tion is the Collie Coal Trust, floated in London with the
inadequate working capital of
Lake View and Boulder Junction, in applying
for six months’ exemption, admits having spent £50,000
in prospecting, and found
North Lyell.—J. H. Curie, the Economist’s
able mining commissioner, thinks this Co. has made a
grave economic mistake in erecting its own smelters.
The Mt. Lyell Co. is crying out for silicious ore for fluxing
and it would pay that co. to treat N. Lyell ore for less
than N. Lvell will inevitably spend in treating its own.
Mr. Curie remarks that the only pyritic ore body of the
field, other than that of the parent mine, is South Lyell.
which may prove, from one cause or another, to be useless
He might have added that S. Lyell is also mighty poor in
copper and its permanency is far from established. In
June, ’99 the N. Lyell directors reported that there wer<
in sight a million tons of ore of various grades, worth,
with copper at £5O per ton, £5,726,387. Curie figures >ll
out, after a year’s further development, at
tons, valued, with copper at £75 per ton, at £2,921,00(1.
The financial aspect : Issued capital, £465,00. (,
but before smelting can start there will be
a debt, including debentures, of nearly £350,000.
and it seems probable that the directors will decide
to liquidate this by issuing 100,000 new shares,
bringing the capital up to £565,000. With an output 01
6000 tons per month he estimates that the yearly prow
will not be more than £245,000, that with copper at £1 • <
per ton, and of this not more than £lBO,OOO would be
available for division. He therefore thinks the shares
are high enough at £3, but would be worth £5 if a
working arrangement with the parent mine wen-
effected. That should have been brought about year-
ago. Both co.s must have been aware of the position.
It seems to be a case of biting noses to spite faces. Mean-
while the N. Lyell people have been spending hundred-
of thousands—in steamers for which they have no use,
and which they sold when the fact became evident to
them ; in a railway for which there would be no use it
the mines were worked in unison ; in smelters, which
mav be of no use because of the likelihood of the ore
being more cheaply treated in another way ; and in other
directions too numerous to enumerate. The question is,
is it too late now ? Worked together, half a million, of
money would have been saved, and more than a million
added to the value of these two mines.

SOCIETY
Perth Morning Herald recently men-
tioned in quite a casual way that a ticket-of-leave
man had been fined 10s. for being out after 10
p.m. It is a strange-smelling item, like the whiff
of sonje animal that died and wasn’t buried in the
year One, when a favorable wind blows out of
B.C. and sends the fiavor of its bones down the
centuries. In Westralia the old bondman is still
supposed to advertise himself as such by flying
out of the theatre at 9.45 p.m., or by dropping his
liquor or his poker-hand and sprinting for the
door, so as to get home and conceal himself in his
stifling apartment by 10. To go to bed early over
there doesn’t necessarily imply that you are a man
of regular habits; it may rather arouse suspicion
THE RECALCITRANCE OF PEGASUS.
The N.S.W. Distressed Jockeys’ Fund now amounts to £5000, but there is no fund for distressed
poets. Herewith you see the luckless bards striving to qualify.
that you daren’t be out late. What a satirical
picture an artist could draw of Westralian
society; a glittering ball-room, a clock registering
9.55 ; and the doorway choked with evening-dress
gentlemen who had a misfortune in earlier days,
and so have to be home by 10. It mightn’t be
strictly accurate, but it is a picture which should
command a good price in W. A. Somebody would
surely want badly to burn it.

The Bulletin had something to say about
the Boer whiskers, teeth, &c., sent to Australia as
trophies from the seat of war, but a trooper (name
printed in the local Y.D.L. paper) of the first
Tasmanian Contingent caps all by writing (North
West Post, Tas., 18/10/’00) to his mother telling
her that he saw a Boer gunner blown to pieces
with a lyddite shell, and, he continues, “I have
some of his clothes and a little lump of his flesh
(italics Bulletin’s) as a keepsake, and will bring
them home with me if all goes well.” Home,
sweet home ! Let us hope this gentle Raspberry-
lander will keep it nice and fresh so that the
family will be able to sample it when he arrives.
•Come to think of it, potted-Boer sandwichei
would have sold like hot cakes in various Aus-
tralian cities on Mafeking-drunk night.
* • * •
The Bulletin has seen in print many
unspeakably-liideous narrations addressed by Aus-
tralian soldiers to their mothers. Under other
circumstances the elaborate brutality of the tales
in question might prompt speculation as to what
sort of female ghouls these swaddies imagined
their mothers to be. But the fact that Mother is
often so proud of her gorilla-minded boy that she
joyfully hands his blood-stained screed to the
printer goes far to prove that Private Gnawbones
from Skull Hollow knows his dear mother right
down to the ground.

A Hobart advt. says a certain entertain-
ment is “under the patronage of H.E. the Ad.”
Chief Justice Madden, at Melb., got the title of
“ Acting ’’-Governor changed to Lieutenant-
Governor. But to suggest that a man is an adver-
tising or advertisement Governor is as insulting
as to say he’s an ac-tor-Governor. By the way,
Madden was the first stop-gap Australian Gover-
nor to pose as the real article. Chief Justice
Stawell never did more than sign official papers.
At Hobart, “ Johnny ” Dodds is unwisely taking
pattern by Madden. The Colonial Office never
meant that the locum-tenens should be more than
what it officially calls him—“ the officer adminis-
tering the Government.” At Sydney, Chief Jus-
tice Darley puts on no frills, and sensibly sticks
to the letter and spirit of the office.

The perplexity of the N.S.W. Govt, over
the question of how it can, with a decent demo-
cratic face, provender, during the Federal celebra-
tions, the wealthy and political pushes at the ex-
pense of the general revenue, was much relieved
during the week. A member whose constituents
don’t know much about him leaned over a bench
and asked the Premier would he, as part of the
ceremonies, give a feed to the old parties in Sydney
Benevolent Homes. Premier Bill thought he
found salvation. It is said he kicked See under
the table, and motioned to O’Sullivan to keep his
mouth shut. “ Certainly I will,” said Long Bill-
ominous name ! —“certainly. The poor must have
their rejoicings as well as others.” The meaning
of this is that Sydney’s wealthy lower orders and
general political pushes will gorge themselves on
the best brands of everything at the public ex-
pense, and that to cover this gluttonous robbery
of the people some hundreds of old people in insti-
tutions will get a blow-out coming to about tup-
pence-ha’penny a head. Is there no one in Par-
liament to get up and burst this raid on public
confidence—this idea of feeding the derelicts as
a mere foil against those who object to the enter-
tainment of “ ladies and gentlemen ” at Treasury
expense ?

It has been published in London that Aus-
tralia will only be absolutely happy if the Yorks
are permitted to bring their b.ibv with them.
“The dear little fellow, in the sailor suit, with
whose photo, we are already familiar,” is the par-
ticular kid that Australia is supposed to be yearn-
ing for. Salisbury, Chamberlain, and the Queen
are understood to deeply regret that it can’t be
done, it being considered too big a gamble to
allow two heirs to the Throne loose on “ a long
and perhaps perilous journey.” The Bulletin’s
idea is that, if England had horse-sense, it would
seize the present opportunity to send us the whole
Royal family, root and branch, and “ for keeps.”

Is it the federation of Australia that is to
be celebrated next year, or the subversion of the
S’African Republic ? It would really seem that
the Australian people are to be asked to work
themselves into a lather of enthusiasm to the
honor and glory of a gaudy and greedy militarism
under the pretence of signalising the peaceful
birth of a new nation with spontaneous rejoicings.
Gorgeous warriors are to be imported from all
over the Empire to decorate the occasion. A
thousand assorted British soldiery, including
Indian niggers, all in their most beautiful clothes,
are on order, the returned contingenters are to be
paraded freely, and when the “juke” lands the
country will swarm with swashbucklers and mili-
tary dudes, to be trotted out by special request.
Far better let the Commonwealth come in peace-
fully on its own than disgrace the event with a
display of mock patriotism, utterly foreign to the
spirit of the moment. The Commonwealth cele-
brations are, it would appear, to be merely another
big advt. for John Bull and co.

Sydney D.T. has now got a scheme for
divorcing the control of the metropolitan trams
from that of the Railways, on the ground that the
three Railway Commissioners have their hands
full without the bother of the fast-growing tram-
way system. The idea is that a single Commis-
sioner for Irani ways he appointed. This sugges-
tion is much more rational than the Telegraph’s
previous tram-scheme, about which the less said
the better. By the way, what sort of a Tramway
Commissioner would President Carey make ?

The Catholic Press aforesaid recently
gave its readers something pretty to look
at ph otograp 1 1 s in three postures of the
mutilated remains of one of the victims of the
Cninese outbreak taken after the unfortunate
creature had had his eyes burnt out, his non-vital
parts riddled with bullets, and other sections of
him cut off and roasted while the main portion of
him was still alive. The Press accompanies
these fascinating items with the remark
that this is the first time that the aspect
of a martyr after martyrdom has been preserved
by photography. Thank heaven that the inven-
tion of the art was delayed till the ages when
martyrs were scarce !

Hon. John Hughes’s speech at the second
reading of N.S. W.’s new Money-lending Bill tem-
porarily galvanised into life the “House of
Fossils-” It co sisted mainly of a series of
scathing charges against Sydney’s chief usurers.
One by one they were named, and particular
instances of their greedy practices related. The
height of denunciatory eloquence was reached,
and the most cogent proofs of need for the Bill
advanced, when Mr. Hughes dilated on the wily
ways and exorbitant charges of a certain Gentile
“worse than a Jew.” If the same enthusiasm:
prevails in the Lower House the safe passage of"
the Bill is assured. There was but one of the-
fossils who tried to keep up the appearance of the-
mummified, and even he failed. The shape of his
proboscis portrayed an inborn antipathy to public
discussion of the subject, and though* he buried
his head in The Bulletin, even that journal failed
to engage his attention during the speech.

Premier Seddon’s Jingo cablegrams are
likely to make a big hole in next surplus. Appa-
rently Richard of Kumara grows garrulous with
age, and to be garrulous by cable is expensive. -
The shriek which he sent to Roberts whep.Jhat',
condensed grenadier was appointed commander-
in-chief must have run to many pounds. It r Hfc.
eluded such costly remarks as—“ Your life-lopgg,
successful, and distinguished services to the em,Y
pire fully entitled you to position, and Imperial/
authorities have done well in placing British army
under your charge.” Roberts replied briefly. He
hasn’t a whole Treasury to use forpurposesof diffuse
cablegramming, and probably he curses with a
curse that would be good to hear when he has to
waste time and ink and cash in responding to the
dull platitudes of people he never met, and whom
he could see run over by a tram-car with perfect
indifference.

The Backwash has got into the columns of
the Jingo papers now. Perth Morning Herald
prints this from its correspondent with the
Imperial Bushmen, a well-known and reputable
Westralian journalist:
“ ADVT. ADVT.-ALL ADVT ! ”
I have seen Mafeking, talked with its people, and have
come to the conclusion that the siege was perhaps the
most “ over-hoomed” incident of the war. “ Advt. Advt.
—All Advt.!” is the comment mostly heard from visitors to
the town since the relief. The harrowing tales of the
privations suffered by the garrison that have been circu-
lated may be put down as mostly overdrawn rubbish.
When 1 heard them laughing at the description of the
trials in the outside newspapers, I remarked, “ But,
surely, that report about eating horseflesh wasn’t a de-
liberate lie ?—and when a garrison gets down to that,
things must be at a low "ebb.” “Oh, yes,” replied a
trooper, “ we had a go at the horses ; but, bless you, there
were herds of cattle grazing on the flat here all the time ! ”
“ Then why, in the name of all that’s foolish, did you do
it?” I asked, naturally. “Orders,” he replied, with a
charmingly expressive shrug. As a matter of fact, about
the only article they really felt the want of badly was
good flour, and that was only towards the end. It is an
indisputable fact that one of the most daring attacks on a
Boer position from the town was the result of a bet made
across a rather lively supper-table at 12 p.m. I, like all
other visitors, was keenly disappointed in what I saw and
heard in Mafeking, having expected so much—and here
one of my idols has come down with a crash.
and the Leg. Ass.’s tomcat, was anxiously awaited.
Alas ! it came not. The Sleath Comedy Co. had
failed to find a quorum, and the commitment to
prison and torment of the refractory G.O.C. —who
had refused to disclose to Mr. Richard Sleath, as
chairman of a Royal Commission, the condition of
N.S. Wales’ armament —had to stand over
indefinitely. As a matter of fact and constitu-
tional law, while Sleath has a perfect right to hand
over a recalcitrant witness to the Sergeant-at-
Arms, that luckless man would at once find him-
self burdened with a white elephant. He has no
place of confinement provided for the reception of
his “prisoners” : he could neither provide them
with food nor accommodation; he could not deliver
them to any prison, for the Governor of a gaol
cannot take any warrant of commitment save
from a judge or magistrate, and if he did the
friends of the “prisoner” could get a writ of
Habeas Corpus and have him out in less that 24
hours; finally, a nice little action for false
imprisonment, with substantial damages, would
knock the mythical gilt off the illusionary ginger-
bread.
* * * *
To a constable who omitted to give, in his
evidence, certain conversations telling in favor of
an accused, Judge Hood (Vic.) recently: “It is
an outrageous thing for any constable to give only
such parts of a conversation as might tell against
a prisoner and keep back parts that might tell in
his favor.” Which is a farcical observation for
any Judge to make while he is himself part of a
system that spends its money and employs all its
ample forces to con vict an accused, while render-
ing to him not the slightest assistance to defend
himself —unless the paltry £l5 allowed for legal
assistance in murder cases can be dignified by
calling it a “ Grown defence allowance.”

“Pedagogue”: The school-boys in our
smellful Sydney suburb have two new games
which are responsible for much wagging o b riday
afternoons. One is a kind of barbaric golf played
with crooked sticks and “language. Ihe other
is called “Blacks and Bobbies.” The blacks
(burnt-cork-’uns) start out from a scrubby allot-
ment called “ Breelong ; ” stick up school kiddies;
take tucker from them ; camp ; boil the billy;
and all the rest of it. The trouble is that as only
two can be Breelong blacks at a time, there is
fierce competition for the “star-parts.” I have
had to administer corporal punishment for the
actual looting of lunch-baskets. Oouldn t stand
so much “ realism ” as that.
... *
The three individuals who salted the Glen-
fine No. 1 mine, and thereby deprived a few
people of a little money in a Stock Exchange
flutter, were sentenced by the Victorian courts
to six and 12 months “jug” respectively. And
the Age reports that the public is surprised at the
leniency of the sentence. What in thunder is it
surprised about ? Only a few days ago a Mel-
bourne “push” nearly killed a man with horrid
brutality, and, allowing for medical expenses, loss
of wages while ill, and his permanent deteriora-
tion, it probably did him more actual cash
damage, quite apart from personal suffering, than
all the loss created by the Glenfine salting case.
And a brute-loving magistrate fixed the penalty
at 10s. fine. While Victoria hasn’t energy enough
to drag that magistrate off the Bench and roll him
in the gutter what has it to be surprised about ?
From a mere property point of view the sentence
on the Pitfield salters was atrociously severe as
compared with the other case. Can’t the Vic.
Bench begin to comprehend that a man’s head and
ribs are his property as much as a man’s mining
scrip ?

Incidentally cropped-up again in
N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, the other
evening, the fact that the alcohol
supplied to the gaol-hospitals
in the State is always in the
form of “white spirit.” The
avowed object of furnishing
the stimulant in as nauseous a
form as possible involves the
preposterous assumption that a
prisoner’s illness should be made
a part of his punishment. A patient
should surely be considered simply as
a patient, and with no reference to his-
tory, or personal character, or any other
circumstance whatever.

* » * *
John LongstafFa portrait of Chief-Justice
Darley is a notable piece of work. Artists were
curious, to see whether Longstaff could make a
life-size, full-length, six-foot figure clothed in
bright scarlet robes trimmed with white ermine,
look alive. The white fur was dead white, and
the red pure vermillion —more insistent and glar-
ing than the red of a postman’s coat. Yet, above
six feet of this trying color, Longstaff has painted
a head that is brilliantly alive, and the tone of
the flesh does not suffer at all from the
blaze of red beneath it. It has been
done, too, without subduing the red by
shadows —the hood is painted in as strong a light
as the head. In a straightforward, manly way
the artist has done a daring and difficult thing.
The figure is noble and dignified in spite of robes
that, in less adroit hands, would have made it
squat and dumpy. The painting of the face, with
the skilfully-treated shadows from the brow half
down the eyes, does full justice to one of the
handsomest men of his time despite a Chief
Justice’s wig—certainly not an enhancement to a
naturally-fine presence.
* * * *
Sydney had another attack of Sunday-
Brunkerism last Day of Rest. The Lyne Govern-
ment’s conscience was smitten about the evils of
the Sabbath banana, and it indignantly closed up
the fruit-shops, just as Brunker did. Brunker’s
conscience lasted for one week only, and it isn’t
likely that Lyne’s will last much longer. The
same Sunday that the Lyne Govt, decided that
poor people must not be allowed to sell fruit in
order to eke out their meagre livelihood, because
the Saboath was holy 7, various members of the
Lyne Govt, travelled by train to Orange, and
thereby knocked the holy Sabbath into 49 pieces.
And they weren’t driven to do this wrong by the
necessity of making a living; their Sabbath-
breaking was pure luxury based on a desire to
gorge at a banquet. Therefore the Lyne Govern-
ment had better crawl down in haste from its un-
tenable Sunday-banana attitude, lest it be too like

When the plague-scare was on in Sydney,
a certain business man had a big clearing-out of
tilings in his office, with the result that lie came
across a heap of stamps that he had previously
collected and then forgotten. He took the lot
round to Smyth and Nicolle, stamp-collectors, and
received £l5O for his bundle.
* * * *
Certain assertions concerning alleged
practices by N.S.W. attorneys as a body, made
by Mr. L. F. Heydon, solicitor, in an affidavit
submitted to Mr. Justice A. H. Simpson last
week, have made the minor branch of the pro-
fession sit up violently. And The Bulletin
doesn’t wonder. It is now for the N.S.W. Law
Institute to afford the Full Court a chance of pro-
nouncing judgment on the whole matter at issue—
incidentally, perhaps, giving Mr. Heydon an
opportunity of acquainting the public with the
names of the leading legal firms who are in the
habit of taking commission from other attorneys
under such circumstances as those set forth in
Mr. Justice Simpson’s judgment.

Jimmy Governor, one of the outlawed
Breelong half-caste-aboriginal murderers, who had
five teeth shot out of his mouth by a rifle-bullet
some time ago, has been caught after more than
three months’ chase —when be was weak from
hunger and exhaustion and tired of the hunt. A
party of seven civilians, headed by one John
Wallace, surrounded his camp at night and cap-
tured him at dawn after filling up his back and
legs with shot. “The police,” says Jimmy,
“could not run down a poddy-calf.” Joe Gover-
nor is still at large. Jimmy was caught near
Wingham, on N.S.W. northern coast, more than
200 miles from the scene of the Mawbey murders.
He says he had been living on oranges and honey
for a fortnight, and was thinking of giving himself
up. And according to Jimmy, it was his white
wife who was the woman at the bottom of his
bushranging, “ and like another Helen, fired
another Troy.” They were splitting timber and
digging postholes for Mawbey, and at night con-
versation turned from hard yacker to the easy life
of bushranging. Says Jimmy (it is a quaint yarn):
“Jacky Underwood, Joe, myself, and Mrs. Governor
were talking. ‘Well,’ I said to them, ‘if I was a bush-
ranger I suppose I would take some catching.’ Jack;,
Underwood said. ‘ Oh, no, Jimmy, you could not be a
rider through the scrub like me. You would have to fol-
low.’ Joe said, ‘ I’m as good as any of you.’ I said to
them, ‘ I don’t crack myself about being any good, but.
perhaps, f could do as well as any of you before I was
married.’ ‘ Well,’ Underwood said, ‘ What do vou say if
we go bushranging?’ I said, ‘I don’t care.’ Then
Underwood said, ‘ I don’t care either. I shot a black-
fellow in Queensland. I don’t like Joe getting shot. You
had better come with me.’
“The missis wanted a fortune dropped on her. She
wanted us to rob people of money, and leave it at Jim
Watson’s corner fence, 2£ miles from Gulgong. Her
brother Willy was to go there and get it when it was all
over.
“ This talk occurred two or three days before the Maw-
bey business. Then I broke up our plates and saucers
and everything in my own camp and was to go next day.
but didn’t go.
“ My missis, Jacky Underwood, and Joe laughed at the
idea of bushrangintr. They said to me, ‘You are not
game to go.’ I said, ‘ That is forcing me to go.’ Joe and
Jacky Underwood went away to Dubbo next day, and
did not come back for eight or nine days. They brought
Jacky Porter back and the boy, my nephew.
“ Well, during that time they had been talking about
bushranging every day. I had never read any of Dead-
wood Dick’s novels or any books about bushranging till
we came out. That night my wife and me had a word or
two about cooking and something *br another about the
camp.
“ With that I said, ‘ I suppose I am in this world alone
with no one to care for me. I thought you was my wife.'
She said, ‘Go to the devil.’ Everything I said to her she
said, ‘ Pooh, that’s nothing ! Pooh, that’s nothing.’
“ With that me and Underwood cleared out. I thought
I might as well die, so the Mawbey murders were com-
mitted. *
