A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, October 30, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
Nothing happens in Sydney at present. All
the people who make things happen are helping
to wear down the lawn at Flemington. The
moths are being chased out of his Ex.’s port-
manteau, the Premier has set all the eggs for the
hatching of Commonwealth festivities, and the
committees are now brooding upon them. May
one be festive by Government order? On such a
date ye shall begin a burst of joy. On a certain
date you shall cease bursting with joy. The city
is for the present given over to flower-shows and
tae amateur actor. The wife of the Chief Justice
opened the annual rose show at the Town Hall
Workmen on scaffolds in the gallery made what
s.io said inaudible with the vigorous hammering.
A rose show is a good place to see a list of famous
and notorious names. There was a wonderful
Pompadour rose, and as champion the Marechal
JN tel was at the head of the review.
Ihe Voice that breathed o’er Eden spoke from
the bridal bouquets, and its advice was, “Do —
that you may carry a posy like this.” It would
h s a pity to abolish the bridal bouquet for the
fashion just set by a fashionable English bride,
tone carried the wedding-ring on a white satin
cushion. When you remember that, on the
authority of historian Buckle, the wedding-ring is
merely a symbolic survival of the last link of the
chain with which savage man dragged his wild
bride to his lair, you will realise that carrying the
posy is not so squaw-like a proceeding as bearing
the wedding-ring.
Doesn’t the decoration of the table at which
you are dining affect your mental attitude ? One
of the four competitive tables, the prize-taker,
as bedecked with pale pink carnations, Japanese
maple, feathery ferns, and pink satin ribbon.
I he garnishee scored in not using a scrap of white
in her garnishings, so they showed up well against
the white background of tablecloth. Not that I
eared much for the sash-ribbon, all the same. It
would surely catch in some man’s coat-sleeve, and,
its ends were hitched to a tall vase in the centre
of the table, there would be trouble.
Yes, your dinner-mate at this table ought to be
named Guy—pray pronounce it as in French. He
would be ironic, elegant, and wickedly witty, with
exactly three drops of best Rhine violet on the
upper part of his shirt-cuff.
You would be gay, cynical, epigrammatic.
Y our gown should be tender green, and he should
say you reminded him of a Chopin etude and
entreat you to eat Angels on Horseback. You
would try one and remark that you never dined
in white since you noticed that women who did
seemed related to the table-cloth. You are sick
of the winding-sheet-like table-cloth. Why don’t
we have green ones on which the flowers would
look at home ?
Then there was another table decorated with
jonquils, cornflowers, and a frank slab of mirror-
glass. At that you would be a little ungramma-
tical, but human and healthy, and talk of golf,
home-made millinery, billy teas, and eat roast
mutton.
Why don’t women who enter for these table-
decorating contests remember that ornamentation
is merely the means to an end, and that end is
the comfort of the diners? Some of them all but
forgot that anyone was to feed from off the table,
and that their food was not to be flowers and
ferns.
The matrimonial-agency persons who celebrate
marriages are to the clergy as foxes, the little
foxes that gnaw the vines. And Govt, is going to
legislate and muzzle the foxes. It’s very hard on
the agencies. They have been kind to the young
lady with a domestic, loving disposition who
desired to meet the tall, dark gent, with a view to
marriage, and have married her most happily to
the short, fair, bald man who, until he met this
young lady, yearned only for the middle-aged
widow with means. But the agency fees are
lower than the clergy’s. That’s where the ring
pinches. Government ought to simplify matters
by making the civil marriage a necessity and the
clerical optional, as in France.
What room there is for an “Hotel Matri-
monial,” where persons desiring to meet affinities
might go to stay ; a place containing a chapel and
clergyman’s quarters and a chronic wedding
breakfast! There was, or is, such a desideratum
in Sydney, only it didn’t desiderate far enough.
It lacked benefit of clergy and chapel. A well-
known Melbourne hotel in Spring-street earned
the name of the “Matchbox” some years ago
because of the number of affinities that made each
other’s acquaintance under its roof.
Concerning the “ Matchbox.” A lot of persons
will recall the case of a maiden lady of a very cer-
tain age who went to live there and presently
met and married a fellow boarder—a squatter,
rich, 70 years old, and a widower. His infuriated
daughters, all mothers of families, calmed down
wonderfully when they saw the bride. “An old,
old maid,” they told each other; “No danger,
and she’ll look after Pa well, for he was getting
very trying.” Within a year little Bobbie was
born. Pa made great changes in his will, then
died. Photograph : an interesting young rich
widow holding her infant son Madonna-wise.
Will Australian men have enough ego to shed
the unnecessary coat during Dog-Star weather, or
will they wait for the Governor-General to give
them a lead ? If our present Ex. had set the
fashion of shirt-waists lie would be gratefully re-
membered in Australia when Mafeking and the
Australian contingents are forgotten. In New
York men of fashion have discarded the coat
during hot weather, and no one is now surprised
to see men in “ shirt-waists ” at theatres, in draw-
ing-rooms, and at dinner-tables. The shirt-waist is
the assertion of a common-sense fashion, and it is
infinitely more picturesque, artistic, and cleaner-
looking than the coat. The only time Sarah
Grand s heroine in the “Heavenly Twins ” sighs
because George isn’t really her hubby is when she
. llm coatless whilst hanging pictures. The
S nrt Waist ” man is, she says, the most becom-
lng.y-dressed man. Which is an easily-under-
standable assertion when you come to remember
u o T?i X ? e^ fi£ ure ( as illustrated on the occasion
by Phil May) cut by that fine, straight man, Mr.
-*-° m , now N.S.W. Minister for Lands,
when he wore, on Melb. Cup lawn, some years
a,go, the typical smart Queensland bushman’s
shirt-waist costume.
At the recent Sydney C. of E. diocesan festival,
after the “Hallelujah Chorus,” the plate went
round, and the Governor scored a point by ex-
horting the audience not to follow the example of
Spurgeon’s rich friend, who sang lustily “ I give
my love, my life, my all,” while he fumbled his
pocket for a threepenny-bit!
The matter of a lady-teacher’s right to wear a
hat before her class seems to have been the root
of that little rumpus in Brisbane Grammar
School lately, which resulted in the suspension
of the lady who had the temerity to sport a
chapeau while on duty. The suspended one
alleges (in the Press) that the hat was “an
elaborate affair, ’’ and a removal of the article
would have entailed much subsequent trouble
when it had to be put on again. Surely a
head-mistress should be cognisant of these facts,
and let down the offender in so vital a matter as
lightly as possible. _ Naturally, the sterner sex is
all a-grin at the episode.
Sydney Newsboys’Picnic, at Clontarf, was an
exhibition of plain - and - fancy gluttony. For
hours before the steamer started the boys surged
about the Government wharf 500 strong, hungry
and joyous. There were scores of sham-fights in
progress. About 150 heads were in chancery, and
the chancellors seemed to be pummelling severely,
for love. Only this kind of boy and ownerless
dogs can play violently for three parts of the day
on an empty stomach, and sleep soundly all
night, still unfed. Only a pure newsboy can
smoke _ the fifteenth part of a cigarette and
be envied by the boy who does not own that regret
of tobacco and memory of tissue-paper. Bland
Holt’s cake was as big as a bicycle-wheel, pink,
and topped by a ragged boy playing a tin whistle.
It was heavy, not in the digestive, but in the
monetary sense, as it was thickly sown with three-
penny bits. It is a great thing to be able to take
the heaviness out of a cake and barter it for cigar-
ettes, or a pork pie.
Seventy-two guineas each was what the Conrad
Martens pictures in Hon. W. A. Long’s collection
fetched. Mr. Long bought them for half as much
years ago. Nerli’s “Cosette” brought eleven
guineas. She goes to Maoriland.
Many persons must have got a shock this week
when they opened an envelope manifestly covering
a card of invitation, and were faced by the words,
“ Thou Fool! ” That is the name of Rev. George
Walters’ new play produced this week. It has
nothing of old Judea in it, but much of fashion-
able drawing-rooms, concert-halls, and young
men’s rooms at college.
“Old Campaigner” MTvor is worth hearing
on the war that was. He has a lot of good stories
and a fine way of telling them. Years ago Mr.
MTvor was a peaceful citizen of Wyoming ; then
he began to fight Indians under Generals Crook
and Custer, yet his scalp is intact.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The MacEacharn turned on plenty of palms,
Chinese lanterns and champagne for his ball at
the Town Hall on Thursday night, so that every-
one voted the last official kick of Mayor Mac. a
really noble performance. To save all jealousy
and heart-burnings among the Naicest, he selected
pleasant, homely Mrs. Downes, wife of the mili-
tary commandant, to assist in the hand-shaking
which some of the staid sticklers religiously per-
form, though Toorak flung its nod and chirpy
smile from a debonair distance.
“ Looking from the gallery at the opening set of
lancers one was struck by the display of bald
heads and bare shoulders. One bald head was
hidden by a tight little Turkish cap, which made
the wearer, Dr. C. Ryan, the envy of all his
shiny-pated brethren. Lieut. Col. Robertson was
a mountainous figure in the set. The incoming
Mayor—Cr. Gillot —was dwarfed beside him. The
military Triton, by the way, danced with the fair
Miss Ruby Madden, who looked plump and
pretty (after her Sydney trip) in an innocent
frilled frock with baby bunches of pale blue
chiffon. A similar decoration drew attention to
her well-dressed head.
“S’Rupert’s hop is fixed for Friday next (9th)
at Prahran Town Hall. Everyone wonders who
will do the feminine honors of the evening.
“Nellie Stewart proved a draw at that charity
fete at the Mars-Buckley mansion (Sat., Oct. 27).
“ At the first glimpse of the favorite in wine silk
dust-cloak, tucked from top to toe, sister women
seemed rather pleased to detect something a wee
bit hard, artificial and mature, in Nellie’s aspect.
But one and all confessed her charm when she
tripped in to sing, in simple white muslin with a
few lace-insertion yees on her skirt, pale-blue
waist-ribbon tied at the side, regular nuggets of
turquoise dangling from her neck-chain, and
white crinoline hat, all pink roses, and pale-blue
satin bow.
“When she began to warble ‘Margot’ (‘La
Cigale ’), she-hearers perked up again and
whispered ‘ What a dear little chirp ! ’ However,
such winning ways, bright locks, dainty attitudes
and sweet smiles sauced the song that the per-
former soon had her audience on toast. Seen at
her best, the Stewart —with eyes blue as ever, arch
dimples, sunny hair and engaging smile—is still
rich in looks, talent and magnetism, though her face
is less soft, her fingers more massive, and her voice
dryer than of old. Of course, Parisian plumage
and artistic make-up are sharp arrows in Nellie’s
quiver. On Saturday, when she arrived at the
fete, she and her party went to ‘take off their
cloaks,’ and spent so long over it that the amateur
concert-promoters, goaded by an audience raven-
ing for its lions, grew anxious. A flurried lady
suggested to colleague Nevill that ‘ p’raps the
artists were changing their costumes.’ ‘Surely
they’re not going to sing in costume ! ’ panted her
confidant—inwardly conjuring up a vision of
tights, &c., while he threw an apprehensive glance
at Mrs. Gov. Madden, who sat in the front row,
pale and preoccupied.
“When the stars (human) did appear, the ladies’
skirts and sleeves and men’s curls were the same
length as other people’s, so I s’pose they’d been
‘reposing themselves,’ as Society’s pet French
tenor probably surmised. The good Boffard !
He’s been there himself. And doesn’t he look as
pretty as a picture in platform war-paint ?
“Other operatic performers were Avery Bel-
vura, said to be a Canadian, a tall fair, clean-
shaped, high-nosed young man. Arens (recently
h aust) has a broad, flat, good-humored Slav face.
He accompanied himself on the Russian instru-
ment, a small music machine, a melodeon to look
at, and a wheezy baby-organ to listen to.
“Lawrence Mooney (Valentine in ‘ Faust ’) is a
big, burly, dark bhoy, the image of a priest.
Cecihe Lorraine, a pretty woman, whose figure
doesn’t promise to remain so youthful as Nellie
Stewart’s, wore white with pale-blue baby-ribbon
threading her lace-tucker.
“The local Chaucer celebration was not more
than might have been expected. Chaucer himself
wrote that ‘ the grettest clerkes been noght wisest
men,’ and Profs. Morrow and Tucker, though they
may be ‘ great clerkes, ’ are neither very wise nor
very interesting. The affair was a social field day
for University graduates, however. The number
of women wearing academic hoods gave one the
impression that there are a great number of highly-
schooled, if not well-educated, women in
Melbourne. Moreover, a pretty woman looks
especially charming in hood, gown and trencher,
more so if she dresses up to the colors of the
hood. Scholarship certainly doesn’t seem to have
destroyed the good looks of the Melbourne girl
grads. John Madden gabbled fluently about
Chaucer without conveying the idea that he had
ever read a line of the poet’s works. He would
gabble on Confucius, Lucretius, Colley Cibber, or
the poet Burns just as fluently.
“ A cluster of Roses of Persia brightened the
Princess d.c. on ‘ Trovatore’s ’ last evening. Our
only baronet (at the Opera every night) left his
box for a bit and squatted on the steps beside the
just-arrived charmers. A fat, little, old ladies’
man, in large checks, who had been intermittently
slumbering, woke up with a start and tried to
weep on S’Rupert’s shirt-front in a paroxysm of
ecstacy over those ‘ ’squisite gels ! ’ Sheridan,
Lauri, and Bracy, with the respective wives of
their bosom, looked grave as deacons.
“ Tenor Salvi’s attractions grow on she-hearers,
but he and soprano Lorraine apparently hunt in
couples, and there’s a haunting dread afloat that
they 11 presently turn out to be a signed and
sealed married pair.
‘‘Opera-goers interested in Mdme. Slapoffski’s
‘ Trovatore ’ innings found her Leonora, though
waistless, rather winsome to look at, in deep-pink,
with jewelled-net Medici collar. She was resource-
ful and at home on the boards, and her voice
sounded trained to a hair. Nevertheless scribe’s
heart _is _ ‘ true to her Poll,’ otherwise Cecile
Lorraine in the same part. The latter’s soprano
is fresh and artless and her matronly figure topped
by a comparatively youthful face. The grace of
La Slapp’s stage deportment is dislocated every
two minutes by the way she kicks her train, in
clear-the-deck-prepare-for-action style.
King-Hedley, the Australian actor engaged by
Beerholm Tree for the part of King Herod’s
physician, is to have as a colleague beautiful
Maud Jeffries, Wilson Barrett’s ex-leading lady
now billed for the role of ‘ Marianne.’ otherwise
Mrs. Herod. Mummer Hedley, a big, non-push-
ful fifty-vear-old, is still so much in love with his
art that he revels in the smallest impersonation
and even cherishes tender memories of that burnt-
cork Patagonian character which once fell to his
share in a cronk play at Melb. Royal.
“The marriage of a medico (not long divorced)
and a fair and lovely Hebe happened in a R.C.
church t other day. The lady played heroine in a
sensational romance a few years ago, when she
was engaged to a certain bachelor. The gentle-
man did not fulfil his engagement of marriage
but paid handsome damages for his breach of con-
tract, married a girl in his own set, and burnt his
boats behind him. People had forgotten all about
the Hebe and the romance, and the cheque for
heart-salve, till the appearance of the marriage
announcement the other day.
“A shocking tragedy caused the postponement
of a wedding (out Camberwell way) a few weeks
ago. The day before the event the brother of the
bride-elect (an only daughter) mysteriously disap-
peared. Inquiries failing to trace his where-
abouts, a search was made all over Melbourne
Up to 12 o’clock on the eve of the wedding the
friends of the family maintained a feverish hunt
and at last had to give up in despair. The wedding
was postponed, all the invited guests being informed
of the reason. About five days afterwards the
announcement of the brother’s death appeared in
the papers, but nothing has since been ascertained
of the manner in which it occurred. The rumor
of his body having been found in the Yarra can
have no foundation, since the papers gave no
account of an inquiry or an inquest, or, in fact
anything relating to the sudden and mysterious
end. As the bride-elect fancied herself the
innocent cause of his death she has put off her
wedding indefinitely, and is given up to unutter-
able grief.
. “The Melb. voter is a much brighter humorist
than he was 10 years ago. Resurrections of
Matthew Davies, James Munro, Mirams, and
other good marks for satire have developed
a turn for ready comment in ‘A Voice.’ The
person who interrupts the candidate is almost
invariably A Voice. He was at Duncan
Gillies’ meeting the other night, and when the oily
little old bachelor ventured to think that ‘ women
have higher duties to perform than going to the
polls/ A Voice wanted to know why Duncan
hadn’t given one of ’em a chance to do her duty.
Duncan Gillies as an upholder of the Higher
Woman is certainly an impressive sight.
“ As the Preacher remarks —‘All is vanity’ in
this world. I’ve just heard a touching story of
the late curate of one of Melbourne’s toniest
Anglican churches. Years ago he threw in his
little lot with the holy bachelor brotherhood,
who bound themselves not to look upon the
Woman when she is red; nevertheless, lie even-
tually got engaged to one of the daughters of a
suburban family. It was rather a shock to him,
no doubt, when the elderly father of the lady
sprung a comparatively youthful stepmother upon
the family, but the affianced shepherd was not
too upset to face the altar. Meanwhile he had
been appointed to an up-country living, and, after
the honeymoon, he brought his bride back to
the parental domicile, preparatory to bearing her
off to the vineyard.
“ Well, with a view to making a favorable first
impression on his expectant parishioners, the gay
ex-curate bought a brilliant outfit for the journey;
clerical ‘ togs ’ of the smartest cut consistent with
godliness, expensive hat, boots, and so forth.
The luggage had already been sent away]
when the happy pair started to follow it!
They made an early start, and this was.
lucky, for, as the departing masher was walk-
ing down the gravel path with his bride,
turning round at intervals to wave last adieux to
her sisters, he fell backwards into the fountain
basin. His plight, when his better half fished him
out of the ornamental waters, was simply awful.
Wet to the skin, new clothes painted green with
slime, tears in his eyes, and on his head a tadpole
or two. Everything, of couse, had to betaken off,
except his broad-rimmed hat, which lay wrecked
at the bottom of the fatal fountain basin, and the
trouble now was to get other garments and fix
him up nice and dry in time to catch the train. A
parlormaid' rushed round to the house of a neigh-
bouring curate, and breathlessly borrowed an old
suit, a battered hat, pair of broken boots, and
some underwear—everything but a shirt. Within
20 minutes of his calamity the poor cleric was
smuggled off to the station in a cab, the wildest
caricature of his former self. The shabby bor-
rowed suit was six sizes too big for him, the hat
came down to his ears, and the coat had to be
pinned across his neck to conceal the loss of his
linen. In this deplorable condition the shirtless
shepherd went to meet the parishioners whom he
had hoped to dazzle.
“The Hopetouns apparently have still just the
same neat little family as of yore, two boys
Victor etc., etc., aged 15 or so, and ‘Charles
Melbourne,’ somewhere about eight. The
younger, don’t forget, is a feat’- jr in our cap as
shown by his name. Let us hope that Australian
air won’t affect the Hopetoun household in the
same way as it did before, for we all know if the
wretched parents attempt to call a future little
stranger (male or female) anything but ‘ Sydney,’
the very Widow of Windsor would smart for their
temerity.
“Pa Hopetoun already trains his chicks in the
way they should go to popularity, pleasantness
and peace. Son and heir, it is printed, made his
maiden speech by way of farewell to the Hope-
toun tenantry. Young Hopeful said that future
summer holidays would see him (the speaker) and
little brother rolling home from far Australia to
Bonnie Scotland for ‘father doesn’t want us to
grow up strangers to our own home.’
“ Pity Hopey didn’t give just one of his popu-
larity pills to poor young Beauchamp, ere the
latter went forth to carry his N.S.W. cross. A
nun, over from Sydney side, says that one at least
of the Orange rows fanned up round his Ex. was
the result of a misunderstanding. Throughout a
certain inland trip, says my informant, he gave
next to nothing except a solitary £5O donation to
a convent. Hence the fuss ! But the Mother
Superior’s sister had nursed, or was nursing, a
wounded Beauchamp in the Transvaal, and letters
had come from the ministering sister to her Aus-
tralian relative, giving interesting particulars re-
garding her charge. The anxious enquirer got
little satisfaction out of his visit, for, much to his
annoyance, a bevy of local magnates had been
invited to meet him, while he had come prepared’
but to hear of ‘ unhappy, far-off things and
battles,’ only too recent, from the lips of a gentle
recluse.

The old difficulty of insuring valuables against
fire and burglars has come up again. It is, however,
becoming an acknowledged fact that it is impossible to
arrange a satisfactory insurance. The Sydney Safe De-
posit has overcome the difficulty by offering to secure to
insurers the articles themselves in their fire and burglar
proof vaults, and thus, by the payment of a comparatively
small premium in the way of rent, avoiding all possibility
of loss. The system is most interesting, and the manage-'
ment invite a" call for inspection. The Safe Deposit is
situated at the back of Paling’s, off George-street.*

old-age pensioners—Mere Ngatahuhu Purdy, who
died recently at Auckland, aged 103. She had a
grown-up son at the time of Heke’s war, and he
and her third husband were drowned 30 years
ago.

Apropos the Duke of York, Melbourne
Public Library has a curious new book entitled
“ George the Sixth.” It is a reprint of a work
written in 1763, prophesying what England would
be in 1900. The author made out that George liJ.
died in 1810, then George IV. and George V.
almost filled out the century. George VI. is m
full bloom in 1900, smashing the French and
Russians both by land and sea There is no
glimpse of railways, steam, ironclads, electricity,
or any of the modern improvements.

Ex-Premier John Downer, Q.C., although
now somewhat of a political back uujnber in S. A..,
has definitely made up his mind to stand for the
Federal Parliament. He lately married a young
and handsome Sydney lady and his slumbering
ambitions are re-awakening. His chances of elec-
tion to the Senate are moderate, and if he gets in
his personal friendships with i'cderal leaders will
probably win him a Judgeship or something
equally good.
Lasl week, S.M. Herald reported that
two N.S.W. solicitors had been called upon to
“show cause,” etc. They were described by the
Herald as (say) “ Mr. P. and Mr. Me.” Now,
if the Herald believes in keeping the names
“ dark,” why even quote the surname initial ? It
is manifestly unfair to other solicitors whose sur-
name initials might be identical with either of
those of the cited two.
Henry Small, of Ryde (Sydney), who died
the other day at 04, made a very early demise for
a member of a singularly long-lived family. His
father —a native of N.S.W.—lived to 95, and his
aunt, the late Mrs. Oakes—said to have been the
first-born wnite Australian —was 93 at death. Of
eight surviving brothers and sisters, the eldest is
over 80 and the youngest 57. The earliest age of
death amongst five of the previous generation, on
the paternal side, was 85.

The imperishable Randolph Bedford is
having another shot at Vic. politics, and is now
Liberal candidate for Bourke West, trying his
darnedest to grab the seat from that mossy fixture
Staughton. Bedford’s darnedest is a thing which
it is impossible to overlook, and Bourke West is
having the liveliest shaking up it has experienced
for 18 years. Evidently David Syme has adopted
Bedford, as the Age is now manifesting quite a
benevolent interest in the young man.
Kingston says he can’t sleep at night for
fear the atmosphere of S.A. Leg. Council will get
into his bones and make him a hardened Tory
The ex-Premier was 50 on Oct. 22, and three days
before his birthday he had assisted in getting an
Early-Closing Bill through the Council for the
first time in its history. It will be impossible to
secure Household Suffrage this session, however,
and Kingston lias shortly to choose between
Federal office and remaining in local politics until
his cherished measure becomes law. For Aus-
tralia’s sake it is to be hoped he will decide for
Federal politics.
That ready debater and well-read man,
Jos. Syrnes, ex-Wesleyan cleric • and longtime
Melbourne’s boss agnostic, erstwhile a valued
associate of Bradlaugh, talks of giving up his
Saper, the Liberator, and returning to England.
Reason—no money. The root cause no doubt is
that young Australia doesn’t care a rap about
higher questions than horse-racing, cricket and
football. While the oldsters are mainly conserva-
tive in the matter of religion, the young ’uns,
though in reality freethinkers, give no attention to
such things. From a clerical point of view, the
young Australian is worse than an agnostic ; he is
an utter indifferentist.
Hall Caine, during his last visit to the
U.S., received a letter from a death-condemned
murderer piteously requesting a visit,, and when
Caine went the condamne said that, during his
imprisonment, he had been permitted to read
“ The Christian,” and it had done him so much
good that he thought he could die happy if he
could get a few words of comfort from the author.
The novelist was much affected, prayed with the
assassin, and kissed him on the forehead before
leaving. But the very next day, goes on the story,
two illustrations of the scene—founded on snap-
shots—appeared in a N.Y. daily, the editor of
which had offered the prisoner lOOOdol. for his
family if he could succeed in obtaining the inter-
view.
“Tommy” Taylor, the juven-
ile-looking and blatant cold-tea mem-
ber for Christchurch (M.L.), is the
antithesis of the ancient Taylor who
preceded him in the representation
of that constituency. The old un
was responsible for the wildest spon-
taneous laugh ever heard in M.L.
Parliament. W. P. Reeves referred
to Tuvlor and Fysh as “ Arcades
arnbo.” Taylor took this as being
some insulting, nigger-minstrel allu-
sion, and indignantly protested
against being called an “ Arcady
Sambo” by any jumped-up young
jackanapes. He and Reeves were,
however, firm friends, and explana-
tion patched matters up. Taylor and
Alfred Saunders (the Nestor of the
M.L. House) were a picturesque old
couple,'but Taylor lacked the flowing
mane and massive head of Saunders.
Not' generally known in Australia
that Saunders has given to the poul-
try world, in his “ Our Domestic
Birds,” the most practical common-
’ sense book on the chookie ever com-
piled. The old man has been an
enthusiastic, breeder for over 60 years,
and took the first fowls into Nelson
province. His book had a big sale,
and bigger reviews in England. He
reared iiis family as simply as lie
reared his flocks, and a tine lot they are. feix-
feet-two Bant is editor of Lyttelton Times, and
was at one time a most fearless and solid sporting
writer.
“Miles" : Roberts will not and, indeed,
cannot fill the office of Commander-in-Chief. His
health is bad. Recently he was in bed for a
fortnight. He can hardly sit a horse, through the
effects of an operation. He will just snatch the
honor, and retire early next year. But for the
last change of Ministry, Buller would be Com-
mander-in-Chief now. Rosebery was on the point
of making him such when turned out of office.
Buller has been unlucky in two other ways —first
through Kitchener’s rise, second through the war.
Alex. Byrne, who died in Melbourne re-
cently, aged 78, was for 40 years crier of Melb.
Insolvency Court Court. Thirty-five years ago,
when Higinbotham was Attorney-General, he
got an anonymous letter, stating that, contrary to
the rules of the service, Byrne kept a public-
house, in which he had a dummy. Higinbotham
sent for Byrne and handed him the letter. “ I
don’t ask you for a reply,” said he. “In justice
to you, I show you the letter” —and then he took
it and put it in the fire. Some other Ministers
would have secretly given it to a detective to
inquire about. But “Honest George” wasn’t
made that way.
Ex-councillor James Garten, who lately
died at Jolimont, Melb., aged 75, was a remark-
able athlete when a young man. For instance, in
the ’fifties, when he kept the big hotel on the
beach at the corner of Beach-street and Railway-
place, he swam across Hobson’s Bay from Sand-
ridge to YV illiamstown. About the same period
he, for a wager, jumped out of one Cask into
another in a series of ten or twelve, without
touc! ing the cask with his hands. Garton was a
humorist as well as an athlete. Most of his life
ie wore a full black beard —which, like Butters’,
never got grey. One day, acting on a sudden fad,
he turned into the barber’s and had it shorn ; and
when he was clean-shaven —for the first time since
he had had a beard —the mother that bore him
wouldn’t have known him. He was sure the wife
of his bosom wouldn’t. Straight home he went
and rang the bell. Mrs. Garton happened to open
the door, whereupon her lord assumed a German-
Hebrew voice, and demanded to see Mr. Garton.
Mrs. G. said he wasn’t at home. The stranger
said it was all nonsense, and lie must see Mr.
Garton. Mrs. G. told him he mustn’t be insult-
ing, and repeated that her husband was in town.
But the smooth-faced Hebrew gentleman became
abusive, and it was only when Mrs. G. sent for a
constable that Garton disclosed himself. Mrs.
Garton died not long since.
Printed that Sydney’s old friend, John
Alex. Dowie, at present of Chicago, “is now worth
£1,000,000 ; is at the head of a church he founded,
which has 50,000 members ; is the proprietor of
several hospitals, the president of a college and of
a bank, owns a faith-healing hotel, and the
greatest city of religion planned since the Mor-
mons set one up on the shores of Salt Lake. He
started lately in Pullman cars, and supported by
a large retinue, for the Holy Hand. Three flats
have been engaged for him at the Hotel Cecil,
London, and no end of money for Mt. Zion (the
town lie is building) is to be collected in Europe.
He is said to have travelled in state like an Eastern
potentate. He has appointed a time when he will
stand on the Mount of Olives and a time when he
will enter Jerusalem. He controls millions of
dollars and thousands of minds In his tabernacle
in Chicago a few Sundays ago the entire congrega-
tion stood up and said they would vote for what-
ever Presidential candidate their leader selected.
He has a larger personal following than any other
man in Chicago. There has been a ten years’
crusade against him and his works, but it has
totally failed. Dr. Dowie had the training of a
Congregational minister. He calls himself “the
fighter of the devil.” He also fights secret
societies, the use of tobacco and alcohol, the medi-
cal profession, the press, and the habit of eating
pork. He fights them all in his own way. When
he is not busy with them he is working with his
faith-healing, or planning and carrying out big
business enterprises. Zionites give up tobacco,
drugs, liquor, and secret societies on joining the
sect, and present the doctor with 10 per cent, of
their earnings. One cannot help reflecting this is
nice for—Dr. Dowie.”

Aboriginalities.
“Bullfrog”: What timea the Darling
pirate has ! Aa a rule, he works at some riverside
shed during shearing; then he starts down-stream,
for Renmark or Mildura fruit-harvest. On the
way he fishes, or shoots wild duck, for which all
the stations will exchange rations, so that if he
favors Mildura, he arrives with a fair stock of
tucker. By the time fruit-picking starts there is
quite a fleet of boats, so an old man stops to look
after them, and shift the mooring pegs as the
river falls; the rest go out on the settlement to
work. It is April by the time the raisin-grapes
are off; then the boats are hauled out and painted,
fishing-lines and ammunition renewed, books
swapped, and away the pirates go, without a
care on their long pull up the Darling. Four
months till shearing, but they camp a week here
there, where there is a patch of ’possums, or
rabbits are extra plentiful. They wait near a
station or woodpile till a hawking-steamer comes,
and then they sell the skins, and so dn till shear-
ing.
* • * *
This is told as a fact authenticated by
several persons in the West. A certain bush-
ranger was betrayed to the police by a man who
had previously befriended him, and had been be-
friended by the latter. Result, the highwayman
was riddled with bullets by police while going,
with a bridle on his shoulder, to catch his chest-
nut horse early in the morning. A son of the
betrayer born subsequently, and now living, has
on his back a growth of chestnut hair in the shape
of a bridle, and on his body marks corresponding
in number and location to the bullet-wounds in-
flicted on the bushranger.

“ Weoro ” : Never tried any tinned
“bull” ( “_A.Gr.” B. 8/9/’00) but have had ex-
S>nence with fresh ram, when camped on the
arling in ’92 waiting for shearing. They were
killing a lot of aged rams, and we tried the flesh
all ways, but could not get rid of the musky smell
that was worse than the taste, till an old sun-
downer put a couple of legs in his tucker-bag and
buried them in cold moist ground for 24 hours.
The meat was then all right. This philosopher
had noticed that his dog would never eat the flesh
fresh.

When anybody who knows a little about
the matter wants to show what still can be done
at sheep-raising and wool-growing in the droughty
salt-bush and mulga country of Centralia, one
particular station is nearly always pointed to. The
proper thing to do when that station’s name is
mentioned is to say : “ See what brains and long
study and experience can do.” But The Bulle-
tin has found a copy of the agreement this
station asks some of its employes, including
all the rouseabouts at shearing-time, to sign ;
which accounts for some of the profits. The
men (plenty of “rousers” are married far-
mers with families) agree to work for 3s. 6d. a
day, “ and proportionately for every portion of a
day” (there is often a lot of waste time at a
shearing-shed); rations to be supplied at cost,
plus carriage, plus 10 per cent. ; if workmen
leave voluntarily, or through sickness, 12s. 6d. a

The miners at Tarcoola (S.A.) held a race-
meeting last month. There are about 500 people
on the field, of whom some 150 are teetotallers,
but the others consumed the contents of 90 cases
of whisky (1080 bottles), the result being that no
work was done by anybody for a week. The
Mines Minister, in speaking of Tarcoola and its
prospects, cited this whirling' orgie as a proof of
the prosperity of the district.

At a recent meeting of a Southern N.S. W.
Pastoral and Agricultural Society, a member pro-
posed that the annual show be postponed from
February to March, as the former month was too
hot for delicate women and pigs. True bill !
* » * *
Some day, when the Royal Commission
has finished inquiring into the Western (N.S. W.)
pastoral industry, Govt, might do worse than
appoint another to investigate the condition of
the men engaged in that industry. . That Com-
mission ought to have special instructions to
study the sanitary accommodation at shear-
ing sheds; also whether at some stations the
men are not plundered through exorbitant over-
charges for stores and rations, and whether they
are not practically compelled to patronise the
station store by means of a heavy commission
which any travelling hawker has to pay to the
station on his sales.
* * * *
S. Australia’s Gov. Tennyson startled an
Adelaide audience t’other day by protesting
against the use phosphorus in rabbit des-
truction.. “Surely,” he said, “there is some
drug which will kill them in less than two or three
days, and without inflicting excruciating pains.”

* • * *
“ Wiljar ” : Used to water about 1000
sheep daily at the small dam of a cocky. Once
through the panels—half-a-mile from tank —they
always made off at a mad pace in strings till they
reached the water, into which they instantly
plunged, literally covering it. Yet I never had
one drowned, though I nave seen old ewes so
blown-out with water —which most of them
quaffed in the swimming process—that they reeled
about after coming out. By the way, on a big
Vic. run, a few years ago, some 1500 sheep were
drowned in this way in one act.
* • • *
“Snell” : Forest-thinning is a pleasant
game in the summer, according to a man
off a relief-Job who enlightened me in a
railway-carriage t’other day. “It isn’t
hard work,” he said, “ but generally the
snakes are a bit tryin’ to a nervous
man. I was workin’ with a gang near
St. Arnaud last summer, an’ one
afternoon my mate, a feller named
Joe, had taken up a’ armful o’
sticks an’ bark, when suddenly
he fetches a yell, pointin’ with
his sticks towards my feet.
‘Jump! jump!’ sez he—‘a
snake!’ Just then I spots a
snake nipped between two twigs
in his armful, wrigglin’ like fury.
‘ A snake ! a snake ! ’ he yells.
‘Well,’ sez I, ‘you blanky
fool, why don’t you drop him ? ’ ‘ It’s
you,’ sez he. ‘ It’s you ! ’ sez I. We
jumped together. I was standin’ on
the neck of a daisy ’bout four foot
long all the time.’’

Association:—
Black women in the Northern Territory had very insuf-
ficient protection by law from the brutality of some of the
lawless white men there. The illicit trading in liquor
done with the blacks there ought to be stopped, for it was
ruining them body and soul. Blacks were even now being
carried away from their tribes by white men into virtual
slavery.

was rammed down Australian throats from nume-
rous pulpits.
A “RUM” EXPERIENCE.
INQUISITIVE STRANGER (to “ Deaf
Ben,” one of the “old hands”
of Green Wattle Gully): “ Your friend says
he’s had fifty years’ experience of the district.
What’s yours?”
BEN: “Eh?”
STRANGER (louder): “ What’s yours?”
BEN (promptly): “ Mine’s rum.”
A patriarchal resident of Tamworth,
N.S.W., tells what looks like a complicated fiction
about the first criminal trial held in that centre :
It was in the depth of winter, and the jury were locked
up in a room with smashed windows and gaping cracks,
through which an icy wind swept. The only refreshment
supplied by the Court was cold water, and there were no
seats of any kind. The nearest publican, on learning of
their discomfort, came across with empty gin-cases, and
passed them through the window. Also, a bottle of the
best rum. They could not agree while the rum lasted,
and when the end of that came they began to shiver once
more. So they jumped on the gin cases and made a fire,
but their fuel soon gave out. The Judge left word that
as soon as they arrived at a decision they were to be
taken over to the hotel. This was done. He then ordered
the jury to be handcuffed in twos to a bullock-chain, and
then marched them up and down the road till the morn-
ing came, when the decision was announced.
» * * *
Matters pastoral in Maoriland rapidly
approach a crisis. For years the fame of the
province’s frozen lamb has been so
great that a large portion of the an-
nual increase of the flocks has been
killed and sent ’Ome. Ewe lambs
have been specially in demand. The
result might have been foreseen. The
country is now almost denuded of
breeding ewes. Stock has gone up
enormously ; freezing works are closing
down. Three years at least will be
necessary to put the balance straight;
and during that three years Argentina
will have things practically its own way.

“Shellback”: In Central Queens-
land I once saw a woman of German ex-
traction—after vainly trying to make a
young puppy drink milk from a tin plate
—place, the animal to her breast saying—
“ This is the best way.” Pup seemed used
to the operation and was quite at home
robbing the baby, which was on its
mother’s knee. Then picking up another
puppy, the lady observed—-“ This one is
the worst, he is always in a hurry.” She
was right; the little beast nearly choked
itself. Told us the pups were a nuisance
at night as they often squealed for at-
tendance, and that she kept them in her
room so that she could administer to their
wants. There was no habitation within
miles and I suppose solitude had made
the woman callous. But what sort the
husband 1
“ H. J. Mac.” : Be Australian aborigines’
burial customs (“Pelican,” B. l/9/’OO)
I remember when 1 was a lad, in the valley of Burra-
gorang, seeing the natives swathing an 8-year old boy’s
corpse, in tea-tree bark and binding it tightly with stringy
bark until it was compressed out of all human resem-
blance. The body was placed upon a bark platform
upheld by 10ft. poles, and was allowed to remain there for
about 5 weeks. It was then taken with much yell and
“yabber” and deposited in the hollow of a gum-tree,
about 30ft. above the ground ; the darkies afterwards shift-
ing camp about 6 miles away. The tree was about 65 chains
from our house, and after standing the horrible stench for
about 3 weeks my father set fire to the tree one evening
about dusk ; with the result that in less than two hours
the house was surrounded by angry blacks, who
tried to set fire to it in turn, and my father had
to shoot three of them before assistance arrived.
The same tribe of aborigines often carried their children’s
corpses about with them for years, though at other times
they buried their dead forthwith, always making three
graves parallel with each other and placing the cadaver
in the middle one. Some oddly-shaped white stones were
laid upon each of the graves, and changed every morning
for four weeks. The object of this proceeding and of the
three graves now to puzzle the Evil Spirit who was ex-
pected to come cruising around during the night after
the corpse’s spirit; and was expected to sink two duffers,
the Ist and 3rd, and then clear out disgusted at his dis-
appointment. I may add that I consider Mr. H. Stock-
dale egregiously wrong in everything he has contributed
to The Bulletin on this subject, and that it is well-known
that no hard-and-fast ceremonial rule exists in connection
with the burial of the dead of any given tribe—especially
in N.S W. and Vic.
“Canip”: Was recently coach-travelling
on a road rough as the path of the virtuous. My
own trouble was to keep my head
from shooting through the roof and
the rest of my body from making
spasmodic excursions in various
directions, but my companion’s
solicitude was all for a cockatoo in
a cage. “You seem to think a
lot of that bird,” I remarked.
“An’ so I orter,” was the re-
\ ply. “’E’s earned me two
\ ’undred quid this year.”
Learned then that the bird’s
owner was a disciple of
Ali Baba, and carted
“cocky” round to coun-
try racecourses. Cocky’s
biz. was to draw sweep
tickets out of a box ; mar-
I bles, &c. “’Ow’d I teach
’im? Well, I uster tie a
string round ’is neck, set
’im on a table, and dror ’im
along by the string to a box
as ’ad tickets in it. Then I
says, ‘ Cocky, pull a ticket,’
grabs ’im by the ’ead,
shoves it into the box, opens
his beak, sticks a ticket in it,
’olds it tight shut, and drors
’im back by the string to other
end of table afore I lets go ’is
’ead, so ’e can drop the card.
Oh, it was a long time afore ’e’d
do it on ’is own. Sell ’im ? Not
fer fifty lovely quid ! ”

Yon Kotze ” makes some disparage-
ments about Townsville:—
Townsville mainly consists of a flaring streak of damna-
tion baptised Flinders-street; a large rock covered with
soap advts. and yclept Castle Hill by the benighted
natives, and an overpowering smell. The latter emanates
from Ross River, a lagoon which, I believe, in that respect
“lays over” any other navigable water in the world.
Unfortunately the smell does not confine itself between
the mudbanks, and as the common variety of deodoriser
is powerless, the Municipal Council has to let out contracts
for the removal of the overflow at so much a yard. Lately,
on account of the plague scare, the Health Board has
advised the employment of a bucket-dredge.
Townsville’s crowning glory is her harbor. Envious
outsiders are in the habit of alleging various drawbacks to
the accommodation therein. They maintain the basin is
too shallow. But as there have been already £500,000
sunk in that same basin, and not a penny of it protrudes
above the surface, the vileness of the aspersion appears
obvious. These evil-minded folk quote the case of a
woman who tried to drown herself at high tide, but
failed on account of an inability to keep her head long

Till lately one of the most prominent citizens was one
i u a *’ oa b w t>ose chief characteristics were a thirst for
alcohol and a habit of never paying for same. He would
plant his forelegs on the bar and joyfully empty any
bottle available, from beer to pot. cyanide. Once a bush
drunk went on a howling spree with Bill. That drunk
was a very famous drunk indeed and never before had
been beaten in the contest of long beers. They kept at
it from 10 a.m. till 5 p.m., though sad to say Bill never
shouted once. Then the bush-drunk, foreseeing defeat,
insulted his mate and refused further refreshment. With
one wild swoop Billy hit his companion where most of
the beer had accumulated, and, majestically stalking over
the fallen remains, put up his hoofs on the counter again,
and demanded more beer with both horns and great fury.
William, the Townsville goat, has never yet met enough
liquor to make him really incapable ; ‘2Bs. 6d. worth of
alcohol has been supplied him in one day, and then, being
put out at closing time, he promptly tried the side door.

• * * *
Out of about 50 men on the Midnight
Lead at the Feather only four (Italians) were
doing legitimate work. The rest devoted their
time mostly to two-up, and occasionally some of
them would stroll over to the Italians’ “dump” and
earnestly encourage the guileless foreigners in
their labors. Then, satisfied that the blanky
Dagoes were proving the ground for them, the
exhorters would rejoin the “school.” One morn-
ing, however, the Italians were discovered sending
down ore-bags which were evidently filled and
kept below, and also washing samples, promptly
discontinuing when anyone approached. Then in
all haste picks and shovels were seized, windlasses
rigged, and the crowd set feverishly to work to
strike the gutter. Hopes increased when at
night the Italians removed their windlass so that
no stranger could get down the shaft. Things
looked rosy until the fourth day, -when it was
found that the Italians had vanished during the
night, and a hurried examination of their claim
proved it a rank duffer, without a sign of wash.
Then the curses, of a badly-“had” crowd of
diggers filled the air, and they sorrowfully col-
lected their tools and left; the mournful row of
dumps bearing witness to their useless activity.
A few days later one of the disappointed met one
of the Italians in the street, and after luridly ex-
pressing his opinion of the trick, was answered
with a sweet smile—“Yel, you do nozzing. You
come to ze dump an’ you pick up ze rock, an’ you
say, ‘ Ha! ee’s zees bottom. Bettare go leetle
deeper.’ Zen you go ’way an’ you play ze two-up.
Ver ’ Bene ! —ve do ze vork an’ve find nozzing an’
zen ve zink you bettare find ze bottom too. And
you have found ’im. Bon ’ giorno, signore .”
Bishop White, of
Carpentaria, told in Bris-
bane lately of his meet-
ing in the Far West a
horse-team driven by a
woman whose husband
was dead. A boy of 14
and a girl of 12 were
helping the team along.
After leaving them, the
Bishop overtook some
bullock-drivers, who in-
vited him to dinner. The
subject of conversation
during the meal was the
little girl’s power of
blasphemy —at which
the bullockies professed
themselves horrified.
The little girl (they
averred) could beat them
easily. They wouldn’t
have minded anyone
talking to bullocks that
way, but it was simply
disgraceful to address
that sort of language to
horses.

A Boundary Rider’s Hardships.
“Although I have lived so long in this
district,” said Mr. Henry Gray, of George-street,
Singleton, to a special reporter who, having heard
of his remarkable story had the curiosity to seek
him out, “it is only during the last two years that
Singleton has been my home. For 21 years
previous I was a boundary rider, often camping
out and suffering such exposure to wet and cola
that Rheumatism came on, causing excruciating
pain in the joints and muscles ; the attacks were
so bad that for nine weeks I was bed-ridden —
powerless to do anything, and when I rose from
bed I had to resort to crutches. In cold weather
the pain was greatly aggravated, and my flesh
swelled and became so tender that I could not
bear it to be touched, My hands and arms
trembled so that I could not control them, I
staggered when walking, and was as miserable as
possible. I tried many remedies, liniments, em-
brocations, electric belts, and faithfully followed
the doctor’s orders, but with no beneficial result.
Two years ago I read a pamphlet concerning Dr.
Williams’ rink Pills and commenced them.
After using a few boxes the excruciating pains
disappeared. Since then I have had no return of
my ailments. I have regained my old strength
and activity, and am a wonderful example of the
curative properties of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
If other sufferers give these pills a honest trial
they will not be disappointed.”
The real cause of rheumatism is disordered
blood, often caused by irregular meals, exposure
and hardships, as in Mr. Gray’s case. Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills make rich, red blood and in-
vigorate the system, so that rheumatism is dis-
pelled. By their tonic action on the blood and
nerves they also cure anaemia, debility, liver

THE objects of the Company are to work, under timber licenses direct from the Crown, the practically unlimited virgin
resources of a remarkably rich extent of forest lands in New South Wales, situated between Coff’s Harbour and the
T* q w NeW . England Plateau, and bordering on the celebrated Don Dorrigo range of country, and to acquire other areas in
N.S.W., and trade in other timbers as may from time to time be deemed expedient; also to acquire from the present lessee
special leases of 100 acres and 20 acres respectively for the purpose of tramway construction, mill and residence sites • also tn
acqmre from the vendor 3043 acres of dense forest land as freehold, all rights of access and right of tramway construction and
all the privileges in-connection therewith ; and further to acquire from such other person or persons as may be here,"ter found
necessary a right of way over any lands which it may be found expedient to cross in connection with the above-mentioned
tramway or otherwise in connection with the Company’s operations, the area of which may be said generally to embrace the
watersheds of the Orara, the Nymboida, the Bobo, and their numerous affluents. It il also to lnd run
specially-designed steamers. r r 1 uu

POLITICAL
POINTS
One who ought to know prophesies that
when Rarotonga is made part of Maoriland it will
become the champion smuggling head-quarters of
the Pacific.
The stonewall of Q. Laborites will not be
altogether in vain. Their bold front will proba-
bly cause some of the other syndicate railways to
be dropped. There are five to be considered.
In the Vic. general election proceedings,
baby-Minister Willie Watt gave himself away
beautifully by declaring his hostility to the tote,
but his cordial approval of the A.N.A. art-union !
“ When a legislator looks upon his seat,
not as a trust, but as a property, it is time to get
rid of him ” says Melb. Aim us, reciting the evils
of payment of members. - But tie Argus does
not really mean that Vic. Upper House should be
got rid of.
The N.S.W’. Leg. Council’s rejection of
the Mines Eight Hours Bill lias touched coal,
silver and gold miners alike on a tender spot, and
has set them clamoring for a popular Convention
to frame a State Constitution as offering the
Bpeediest means of reforming the Council.
A majority of members of S.A. Assembly
have now about made up their minds that indi-
vidual risks attaching to a reduction in the per-
sonnel of the Chamber are too great to be airily
taken. So they endeavor in a discreet way to
avoid coming to any decision on the subject.
The irregularities in the administration of
Lord Howe Island grow graver. Mr. Frank
Farnell, as visiting-magistrate, has issued a set of
regulations for what must be called unfortunate
political stepchildren. He has also provided that
a lady is to be appointed to teach the local school
“and to impart religion on Sunday.” Is this
N.S.W. law? Can it be condoned by N.S.W.
law ? What does N.S.W. Parliament intend to
do?
IN PUSH SOCIETY.—REFLECTED GLORY.
C ITIZENESS: “My first bloke was lung f’r
corpsin’ a trap.”
CITIZEN : “ Yah, yer skitin’ !”
The Maoriland Jingo Government is run-
ning riot with a vengeance. The latest awfulness
is the spending of £12,000 on 140,000 medals to be
distributed among the school-children of the pro-
vince “in commemoration of the successful issue
of the war. One would almost think Britain- had
never won a battle before she triumphed over the
little nation whose women she is now punishing
(vide cable) for being found under arms.
Some Federal certainties, so far as S.A. is
concerned, are that President Baker, ex-Premiers
Playford and Downer, with Symon, Q.C., will be
among the six members for the Senate, and that
three of the seven seats in the House of Repre-
sentatives will be filled by Kingston, Holder, and
Glynn.
No Victorian may sib in the two Legisla-
tures, yet ex-Attorney-General I. A. Isaacs in-
sists, in opposition to the will of his constituents
(who wish to support him as a candidate for the
House of Representatives), in contesting his old
seat for Bogong in the State Assembly. A bird
in the hand is worth two in the bush, says Isaacs.
From the pen of a duly-qualified solicitor
of the Supreme Court of Tasmania :
ELECTORAL ACT. (Schedule 4.)
Summons.

Premier Seddon’s extraordinary assertion
that the Cook Islands (1700 miles off) “ are geo-
graphically an integral part of M.L.,” recalls the
wonderful dictum of the British press wire-pullers
who wanted, some years ago, to “ confer the bless-
ing of civilisation ” upon Morocco, and, in sup-
port of that intended “grab,” argued that
Morocco was “the natural granary of. Great
Britain.”
Q. Speaker Morgan ruled that the various
syndicate concessions now being engineered
through the Assembly were public Bills, yet in
the committee stage one of the Ministers, on re-
ceipt of any amendment, used openly to consult
the syndicators sitting in the lobby. And the
latter scornfully declined anything tending to
preserve the rights of the taxpayers of poor
Boodleland.
The Callide private railway Bill, just run
through Queensland Parliament, received a Par-
liamentary lick which makes it the only measure
of its kind in the world. Cowley moved that the
co. allow all members of the Australasian Parlia-
ments to travel on the line free, and the motion
was carried on the voices. In England, in France,
in the U.S., wherever there are private railway
lines M’s.P. may or may not travel free. The co.
extends the courtesy, or doesn’t, just as it pleases.
But Queensland Parliamentarians when asked to
sanction a private line stand straight up and ask,
“ Where do we politicians come in?” A Licens-
ing Bench might just as properly put in as a con-
dition of a hotel license that the members of the
Bench were to have free drinks.
Seddon has taken in hand and passed a
Bill dealing with M.L, Press Association mono-
poly. The exorbitant fee of £5OO
admittance to the big show, and the
subsequent charges, quite prohibit
Labor organs from getting a start,
and as no capitalist cares to sup-
port the present Govt., Seddon has
Been without press-help in all his
Labor arid Democratic legislation.
At first, and up to lately, any pro-
posed legislation affecting Capital
was the signal for a bitter and. per-
sonal attack upon the Ministry at the
hands of the Tory press, but after last
general election the Fatman papers,
realising that the public would have
none of their nominees, accorded
Richard a patronising and half-
hearted support. Seddon has now
taken steps to smash the newspaper
“ring,” and the long-suffering
Democratic public will now have a
chance of seeing the other side of the
question in print.
S.M. Herald, in its leading
article of 18th Oct., laid it down that
the very slow increase in the popula-
tion of France is one of the dread-
ful consequences of Protection.
Wherefore it would naturally follow
that the tremendous decrease in the
population of Ireland is one of the
awful consequences of Freetrade.
The Irish population has diminished
almost one-half since Protection was
abolished. Another curious circum-
stance : From 1810 to 1840 (30 years,
during which Britain was intensely
Protectionist), Britain’s population
increased at the rate of 434 per 1000;
i.e., where there were 1000 people in
1810 there were 1434 in 1840. Then,
in 30 years of Freetrade, from 1860
to 1890, it increased only by 305 per
1000. When Britain was Protection-
ist in 1820 it gained 171 new in-
habitants where France gained 47
—surplus in favor of Protectionist
Britain, 124. In 1890, when Britain
was Freetrade, it gained 109 new
inhabitants where France gained 37
—surplus in favor of Freetrade
Britain only 72. Apparently in this
matter, as in many others, Freetrade has struck
Britain in the eye with a dirty left, and it isn’t
quite the country it was.
Foster, Mines Minister in the late Vic.
Govt., told his constituents, t’other night, that he
“ was not a candidate for the Federal Parlia-
ment, but while Gippsland East stood by him he
would stand by Gippsland East.” What pitiful
provincial claptrap ! And, by the way, if Gipps-
land East ceases to stand by its Foster, the other
fellow will stand by it. Every country district
can depend upon the bystanding of the bloke wot
it put into Parliament. No occasion for E. Gipps-
land to go in dread of losing its precious Foster.

IKEY (who is learning his tables): “Vat’s twice
two, fader?”
AARONS: “Fife.”
IKEY : “ You’re wrong, fader, it’s four.”
AARONS: “Ah, my boy, I said fife because I
thought you'd beat vie down.”
S.A. Register now speaks of the obstruc-
tive Council majority and of Solomon’s gang in
the Assembly as “the Liberal party,” while it
has christened Kingston and all who vote on his
side in politics as “Socialists.” Nor does the
editor appear to see the humor of the situation,
which is the outcome of the Tory Register's desire
to retain its boast of being “ the organ of Liberal
thought in South Australia.”
Cabled that Rhodes is trying to induce
Australians to settle in S’Africa. Extract:—
The Capetown correspondent of the Daily Mail is
authorised by Mr. Rhodes to state the following : —“ I
am opposed to the introduction of Chinese labour. I
deem that every other means should first he exhausted.
We are now trying to get labour from Abyssinia.”
Abyssinian labor is black, and if other means are
exhausted, then follows Chow! For this have
Australians been shedding their blood in
S’Africa !
Guffaw Peacock was returned for his dis-
trict unopposed, and, in gratitude, distributed
among local charities the money he was going to
devote to electioneering expenses. The Bulletin
scents bribery. It is worse to bribe a whole con-
stituency with a charity gift than to fill one
elector full of sheoak. Peacock’s act may be
meant merely as a thanksgiving, but all the same
it’s a big, fat bribe to do this particular smart
Aleck the same kindness next trip.
Under the old Westralian electoral law a
newcomer had to live in the State 12 months
before he could get a vote, but that violent
reformer, Bigjohn Forrest, did away with all that.
His present law provides that you may get a vote
after six months residence, but can’t use it till
six months after you have nominally got it.
People are so broken up with gratitude at this
generous concession that some of them are going
to present Bigjohn with the first dead dog they
find in the street.
S.A. Premier Holder has gob hold of an
excellent idea. If “vested interests” are the
bugbear in connection with the reform of the
liquor traffic, why not, he asks, refuse to establish
“ vested interests ” in new towns—especially min-
ing towns ! So he proposes to establish a pub. or
two on the Gothenburg plan at the new northern
goldfields. Already the province has, at Ren-
mark, a successful Gothenburg hotel, owned by
the community and managed by a committee in
the community’s interest.
Dear Bulletin.— N.S. W. civil-servants
are once again trying to get a refund of the
cash they sank in the Superannuation Fund.
They are, of course, the younger ones who have
wisely withdrawn from the affair. Those of
55 years and upwards will naturally stick to
it like glue. By the way, why don’t some
speculating “ shent-per-shenters ” buy up all the
chances of those who would (at a ruinous dis-
count) sell their claims ? Suggestion gratis. Was
told by a C.S. the other day that he would jump
at the chance of half cash. Can anybody say
whether this unrighteously annexed cash will ever
be refunded ? —J.G.
Dear Bulletin. —As an old contributor
to your family journal, and an enthusiastic snorter
at the Melb. Land Boom crowd, I crave your
sympathy. One night last week—time 10.45 p.m.
—I found three men on the verandah outside the
front door at my place. The spokesman of the
party apologised for disturbing the household at
that ungodly hour, but said his business was im-
portant. And then the brazen nuisance explained
that he was canvassing on behalf of “the Hon.
James Munro ! ” The blow was too staggering. I
didn’t invite those shameless myrmidons of Jimmy
into the house, and go for them with fire-irons. I
merely cursed them on the verandah, and banged
the door. Now I see the full magnitude of the
lost opportunity and curse my unreadiness. I’ve
missed the patriotic chance of dying on the
scaffold for the murder of at least one of those
three canvassers who were apparently unarmed
and unsuspicious.—F.
It seems to be forgotten by
many parties that under Queens-
land’s now Criminal Code the
conductor of any newspaper iii
that territory defaming foreign
princes is liable to two years’ im-
prisonment !
B. B. O’Conor, M.L.A., is
quite right in his contention that
in the interests of the pure ad-
ministration of justice N.S.W.
police magistrates should have
their salaries paid under statute,
as in the case of the Supreme
Court Judges, who are indepen-
dent of the caprice of M’s.P.
and of the. Ministry of the day.
The N.S.W. public has often seen
a police - magistrate sent from
Sydney by a Govt, to hear semi-
political cases in a distant part
of the province, and has frequently
felt in its heart that that P.M. would
not have been selected to deal with
those, particular matters unless his views,
concerning them had been known to the
Minister. The fact constitutes a blistering
scandal, but one impossible to resent. The P.M.
often has in his hands quite as much power as (and
in some cases even more than) a Judge, and he
should be made as independent as a Judge.
Rev. Handel Jones, the new president of'
Vic. Congregational Union, makes no bones about
his opposition to Bible-reading in State schools.
The Message, he says, distinctly infers that it
shall be interpreted only by duly-qualified par-
sons, and the ordinary State-school teacher,
not being consecrated to the work, has no right to
take it on. It was just the same, concluded the.
new president, as if Paul had gone to Caesar and
said, “I)o my Master’s work—l am too lazy to do.
it myself.” Whatever the Rev. Jones’s logic
amounts to he has done a signal service to the
community, for many Congregationalists are
bound to be influenced by their leader.
Letters addressed to quack doctors at
G.P.O. private boxes, Sydney, are read by the-
Postal Department before being given to the-
addressees. “ I say this,” says Postmaster-General.
Crick, “so that people in the country will know
their letters are read in the office.” But the-
country people ought to have been previously
notified to that effect. At least the Govt.
Gazette should have contained such a notice.
Mr. Crick makes a mistake which, in a better
organised country, would cause him the loss of his
office. At the same time, it will be in the public-
interest if all letters addressed to quack doctors-
are opened in the post and read oy the police.
Perth Morning Herald (brother Alick’s
paper) goes with a loud yell for Revenue-Tariffism.
—which it calls Free trade. It says the Federal
Government “ must levy duties only on such goods-
as Australia cannot produce.” On that basis the
whisky, beer, wine, brandy, tobacco, cigars, sugar,
cloth, silk, and almost all other profitable duties
must come off, and in the practical absence of a
Customs revenue an income-tax of from 2s. 6d. in
the £ upwards is visible on the near horizon. If the
all-wool Freetrader would only try his own doc-
trine for, say, five years—no Australian State has
practised Freetrade, or even pure Revenue-Tariff-
ism, within the memory of any but the oldest and
most decayed inhabitant —and find out what it is
like, it would be an interesting experiment.
The workmen’s homes inaugurated by the
M‘Lean Govt, will be rare prizes for the men
lucky enough to pull them off. Blocks of an acre-
and-a-half, within a few yards of the Brunswick
2d. tram and not three miles from Melb. G.P.0.,
are to be handed over for payments extending
over 31 years. The Govt, also intends to build
houses for the tenants, and the offer practically*
amounts to a chance to obtain a six-roomed brick
house, standing in an acre-and-a-half of good,
high ground, for 7s. 6d. a week, with the cer-
tainty of its becoming the tenant’s property at the
end of 31 years. To prevent booming, it is pro-
vided that no tenant may sell out under seven
years without permission. That “without per-
mission ” is a big mistake, and will lead to much
jobbery and political interference of M.’sP.

Mr. Perott of the Commonwealth Lumber
and Shipping Co. Ltd., draws the attention of the
public to the prospectus of his co. and says that
the N.S.W. people have only to digest this
prospectus in order to comprehend what riches
they have in timber. “Alex. Kethel at first
pooh-poohed the idea of the forest being so rich —
said it was a fairy tale; but after going through
the forest personally he returned with the air of a
man who has looked for wood and found gold.
Walker, the well-known manager of the Kauri
Timber Co., is just as enthusiastic as Kethel; and
nobody can upset the figures we publish in our
prospectus. Every detail has been worked out
and sifted bit by bit by our Board of Directors
for the past six months. What we say in that
prospectus is true.”*

Three Hambourgs are rumored to be coming out to
Australia next year. Mark Hambourg, the pianist, who
didn’t go mad seemingly) Jakoff the violinist, and a third
and younger brother, a ’cellist of wondrous genius. But
the third Hambourg sounds rather like a paragraphist’s
invention.
Melbourne is to have only two pantomimes this Xmas,
not three, as suggested in last week’s Bulletin. There
will be a Nellie Stewart panto at the Princess’s, and a
Rickards panto. By the way, a rather absurd report has
been published concerning Irene Franklin and the Xmas
show at the Bijou. Said that the auburn Yankee damsel
with the remarkable twang would appear as principal
girl to Billie Barlow’s principal boy. Fact is, the girl
part has been given to Noni Rickards, and if Miss Nom
plays it as well as she played Babbie in “ Little Minister ”
she will get through with honors.
One hundred and seventy girls turned up to fill 18
vacancies in Musgrove’s Opera Co. in Melbourne. Still
the stage strikes more than lightning !
The same Sydney paper which reports the shooting and
capture of bushranging Jimmy Governor reports that a
packed house gave Alfred Dampier’s revival of “ Robbery
Under Arms ” a cordial reception at the Criterion Theatre.
Australia likes the highway-robbery business. See how
popular it was to volunteer for the Transvaal!
“ Melb.” : The good old Queue ! This city takes it like
a lamb. You remember the observations of the Manager
in the Prologue to Goethe’s “ Faust.” How he delights
to see the mob pushing and fighting at the pay-boxes, as
at a baker’s shop for bread in time of famine. O’Cal-
laghan—not the man of “ His Last Legs,” but the Melb.
Police „Super., and aspirant for Chomley’s boots—has
abolished these fierce pleasures.
Ed. Bulletin. —For the “ true-inwardness ” of the
tricks with which the “ Georgia Magnet” is now “ para-
lysing ” Sydney, vide Pearson’s Magazine, Jan. ’99,
wherein will be found a complete explanation of how such
physical “ fakes ” are worked. —Nem.
Fitzgeralds’ had bad luck with their tents in Melbourne.
Love, who had gone ahead to prepare the way, planted
the huge tent beautifully on the Exhibition oval,
when a whirling willy-willy came along and whipped the
lot up by the roots, enveloping the Exhibition dome in a
canvas overcoat, and strewing all Carlton with fragments
of the firm’s dry goods. On Wednesday it looked as if the
Fitzses would have to house their show under umbrellas
for the first night’s performance. “ But Love will find a
way.”
One of the improvements Geo. Musgrove will introduce
to Australia(says The Bulletin London correspondent) will
be the sixpence-in-the-slot opera-glass, such a success in
English theatres, &c. A natty nickel case is fixed to
backs of stall-seats and holds an aluminium glass, which a
“sprat” places at your disposal “for to see the plye”
and to understand the understandings of the ballet.
Charles Cartwright’s Cromwell (writes Tiie Bulletin’s
London correspondent)has been remarkably well received.
0.0. gets a little punishment from one or two critics, but
plenty of praise from the majority. And Miss Edith
Cartwright has nice things said of her. Strange coin-
cidence ! —John Morley, a relative of C.C.’s, is publishing
a biographical study of Oliver Cromwell next month.
Musgrove’s managerial policy re Madame Slapoffski
doesn’t look a little bit clever. Madame came out to
Australia, unannounced, with her husband who has the
drilling of the opera chorus. A week or two after her
arrival the names of the Musgroveian principals were
solemnly declared, and hers was not amongst them.
Then came the boss with the co., and the boss told half-
a-dozen different interviewers that neither of his three
prima donnas was older than 24. No suggestion of a pro-
bability of adding Madame Slap, to the list. “II Trova-
tore ” was produced with five of the new people on the
bill. Laudatory notices. “Faust” followed with an-
other four new people in the cast, and the notices were
less laudatory. The average quality of the co., judging
from the “ Faust ” performance, didn’t seem as high as
had been expected. Then came the news that Madame
Slap,, who is certainly somewhat older than 24, would
appear as a prima-donna. The public, or some of them,
would naturally look at this last announcement in the
light of the “Faust” notices, and take it as an admis-
sion of weakness in the company, although they can see
no immediate necessity for another principal soprano.
What Musgrove chiefly wants is a second tenor of Salvi’s
calibre, and a contralto to balance Agnes Jansen.
BjThere is one class that laments the sudden elevation of
the old Alexandra into a fashionable house, alias Her
Majesty’s—it is composed of those poor players and
poverty-pointers who only got a casual look-in at the
metropolis, and for whom the Aleck offered the one pos-
sible opening. The wandering co., accustomed to cow-
houses and barns, has no longer a chance of dissipating
its little savings in a city splurge.
Musgrove’s production of “ Carmen ” is only the third
that Melbourne has seen in 12 years. In Oct., ’BB, Simon-
sen staged Bizet’s opera at the old Opera House, with
Louise ’ Lablache in the name part, Pimazzoni as the
Toreador, and Flora Graupner as Michaela. In April, ’92,
Marie Halton played Carmen at the Princess’s, Flora
Graupner (with diminished voice) as Michaela again,
Charles Ryley for Toreador, and Willie Walshe (now in
England) as the tenor, Don Jose. Florence Young and
Violet Varley took minor parts in that show, also
Leumane, Howard Vernon, and Alan Morris, the latter a
capital baritone singer who had got few opportunities,
because Ryley was the popular candidate.
George Crichton Miln (writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent) is now thoroughly devoted to literature
and journalism, and no longer oscillates the ceiling suites
with his thunderous bass. He has done remarkably well,
his signed articles having appeared in the new and extra-
ordinariiy-successful Express, the Daily Mail’s red-hot
rival. His wife —Miss Louise Jordan, as she is remem-
bered “down under”—is, I grieve to say, a martyr to
nerves, and is unlikely to act again, though with her pen
she also has done very well.
“ The greatest of elephant shows,” writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent, “is now demoralising the
stage supports of the Palace Theatre. A big American
negro, Eph. Thompson, brings on three of the 4 two-
tailed’beasts, and after preliminaries Eph. falls ill on the
stage. Immediately an elephant carries on a bed and
pulls the coverlet back with its trunk, elephants 2 and 3
pick the prostrate one up —one getting hold of his feet,
t’other a breast-belt, and dump him into bed. No. 1 l.f.
then picks up a safety match in its trunk and strikes it
on a large board—prepared surface —lights candle, drops
match on ground and puts its dainty Cinderella on it.
Meanwhile No. 2 l.f. goes to 4 property ’ telephone, rings
up, gets a reply, takes off and holds a pan-like receiver to
its cellar-flap ear, and then opening a mouth like a
butcher’s shop bellows into transmitter and replaces
receiver. No. 3 l.f. then proceeds to 4 property ’ chemist’s
door, rings bell, takes bottle of medicine, doses invalid
with it, and finishes remainder. No. 1 l.f. then puts out
candle and proceeds to rock the bed with no gentle
motion. Patient soon recovers, and is assisted to rise,
&c. Also fine bit of thrill when Eph. lies down and
makes an l.f. walk across his body, which it accomplishes
with extraordinary reluctance and gingerliness. A woman
dances, and two I.f.’s with skirts clumsily raise alternate
forelegs, finally pulling up their skirts with their trunks,
displaying frilled trouserettes.”
According to war correspondents’ accounts,
in the terrible advance to Pekin the Japs, proved
themselves the best marching men, the Russians
were second best, the British a moderate third,
the Indian troops came fourth, and the U.S.
infantry, lame and crippled and broken-down,
was the worst of the lot. In the last hours of the
march the remnant of the American infantry was
only induced to stagger on by the constant appeals
of the officers to keep going for the honour of the
flag, and not let the Japs, and Russians take
Pekin while the star-spangled banner fainted in
the dust 20 miles behind.


A decent Adelaide citizen faced the police-
court, the other day, on a charge of not having
had his child vaccinated. Defendant produced
the boy in court, and showed on his arm an erup-
tion which still remained from a previous applica-
tion of lymph over eight years before, and which
numerous Adelaide doctors had failed to cure.
Despite this the man was fined £2 in all, in-
dignantly refused to pay the fine, and went to
gaol.
Stated in evidence in an assault and battery
case between two highly-placed Vic. public ser-
vants, t’other day, that after defendant had
grasped complainant—a vegetable pathologist and
lecturer—by the shoulders and run him vigorously
out of his class-room into the grounds outside, the
latter arose in a state of mild excitement, aggra-
vated by gravel-rash, and returned to class,
observing, “ Students, after this digression we
will resume the lecture.” “ Digression ”is good.
S’Malkum M‘Eacharn is taking up the
“Greater Melbourne” talk again, and says he
means to go on with it. But why did he incon-
tinently drop it a year ago, when matters were
coming to a head ? Simply because some of the
old fossils on the City Council said they wouldn’t
vote for his re-election as Mayor if he didn’t—and
if he hadn’t been re-elected lie wouldn’t have got
liis title. Is that the sort of man to be trusted
with the leadership of any popular movement ?
