Pastor Eitchett writes that “T)e Wet is the
only Boer general who has gained a bright repu-
tation.” That is, Joubert, Cronje, Ollivier, and
De la Rev have not done anything to brighten
their reputation. Which is indeed rough on
Buller and Co.
Ex-Sydneyite tells things about Seddon's
country:—
Maoriland Education Boards and School Committees
are smellful institutions. A personal or other canvas of
any member is supposed to put an applicant out of court.
Yet, no candidate ever yet got appointed to a M.L. school
who relied on his (or her) merits alone ! Political patron-
ace, in fact, is rampant in the land of King Richard.
When the Govt. Insurance office was started, vacancies
were filled on the basis of suitability alone, but now the
insittution is flooded with a set that holds no other claim
to merit than the relationship or friendship of somebody
of importance. Maoriland is, in view of the Plague, being
cleansed of material filth, and now is the opportunity for
some great political scavenger to arise and shed daylight
on the policy that hitherto has sailed under that mud-
bespattered flag—democracy.
“Alia” to The Bulletin:
Several lines of railway have been recently recommended
lor construction by N.S.W. Public WorksComnutteewith
a , lr oviso for tbe betterment principle to be enforced in
properties benefited by their construction. But why
should this proviso only be enforced on these new lines,
most of which are of the pioneer type, through sparsely
settled country, where possible benefit to property-holders
will not be great, owing to distance from markets, &o. If
the betterment principle be good, why not pass an Act
making it applv to all railway lines in the State? Espe-
Sallv on these constructed through large estates, ori-
ginal! v Or >wn grants, whose owners contribute more to
? tbnr, otherwise bv blocking closer setcle-
fos n sof revSftue than otherwise by blocking closer settle-
ment This might easily be effected by increasing the
land-tax on a defined area within a workable distance
each side of the railway lines, graduating it according to
Quality of land and area held, in an increasing ratio.
Some past Government shave shown a desire to retain the
unearned increment to the Crown by reserving land from
sale on the surveyed routes of several proposed lines.
Why the betterment principle has not previously been
enforced maybe accounted for by the fact that on more
than one line worked at a loss large areas were held for
speculative purposes by Ms. P.-some of whom bit their
fingers.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Melbourne Cup this year promises to be a more
than usually great event for the gentlemen who lay the
odds. At time of writing one might get different “ good
things ” from ten different authorities, meaning sound
judges of racing whose opinion is worth considering.
And this apart from numerous fancies and suggestions of
outsiders “ likely to run a good race,” and so forth. The
bookmakers, who see everything and hear everything in
the Turfy world, and are practised sifters of evidence—
even the bookies have about ten different tips to give this
year, for although the Children of Israel never believe
very hard in any particular horse, they have their likes
and dislikes the same as humbler mortals.
* * *
If there were no influenza at Flemington the
present scribe might have attempted a long' and serious
discourse on the Cup chances, notwithstanding the open
appearance of the race. The influ., which has already
gripped Seahorse and Finland, will probably get hold
of some more cattle before the big race comes off.
Then why go into details concerning favorites which,
since the outbreak at Hickenbotham’s stable, are in con-
siderable danger of being knocked out before next Tues-
day ? Nothing in journalism is sadder than a careful
analysis of the “ form ” of a handicap gee-gee that has
just been scratched. And all sorts of scratchings may
happen before next Tuesday.
• * • •
Three times within the last five years the Cup
has gone to a three-year-old. Auraria, in 1895, ran only
third for the Derby, whereas Newhaven and Merriwee
won their blue-ribbons before annexing the richer stake.
It is not safe to talk against this season’s three-year-olds
until they have shown how they can gallop in the Derby.
An easy win for Malster on Saturday would make him
favorite for the Cup, perhaps, but he would have to show
a marked superiority to Kinglike and Barbarossa, who are
much better handicapped for the two-mile race. There
is little encouragement to tip the Derby-winner for the
Cup this year. Malster may be a very fine colt at his best,
but he is certainly delicate, and probably bad-tempered.
Anyhow, he comes from the same dam as Carbinier (the
Millars’), which is a point against him. Kinglike is not
the ideal Derby horse in appearance. Barbarossa is a
grand one to look at, and a most attractive galloper,
except for his habit of “ hanging.” At Randwick he
“ hung out,” but at Flemington, where they race t’other
way round, the deep-chested son of Medallion “ hangs
in,” like Dewey last year. The fault may not spoil his
chances, or it may get him into trouble. Anyhow, pre-
sent scribe likes Malster, Barbarossa, and Kinglike for the
Derby in that order, and whilst giving them all a chance
for the Cup, is inclined to prefer Barbarossa to the others
over the two miles, without expecting him to win the
Cup outright.
Take the following as candidates with undeni-
able chances. La Carabine, Tarquin, Merriwee, George
Frederick, War God, Lancaster, Fleet Admiral, Severity',
Maltster, Barbarossa, Kinglike, Clean Sweep and Lan-
caster. Perhaps the best of them will be beaten by one
or other of the next lot: Mora, Wait-a-Bit, Gauleon, Con-
trast, Horace, or Baromeo—not to mention twenty
others. Present scribe’s fancy leans to the thirteen first
named, and as they can’t all finish in front of one another
he takes four to beat the field—
1— War God
2 George Frederick
3 La Carabine
4 Barbarossa

Albion Park Syndicate, of Breakfast Creek, near
Brisbane, has paid fractions of a shilling on their tote
divs. since the outcry in the press. Judging by results the
proprietors should have a nice nest-egg from the past
seven years’ racing.
* * * *
Canterbury (M.L.) breaks out into a red rash of
festivity to-day, Nov. Ist; when its Exhibition (covering
3 acres of earth) is to be opened with much pomp. On
3rd, sth, 7th, and 10th the C.J.C. holds its Jubilee
Metrop. Meeting, the advertised stakes amounting to
£lO,OOO in all. This is the largest sum ever offered by a
racing club at any one meeting in M.L., and the best
advt. the tote has scored thus far.
* * * *
A few days ago at Mt. Gambier (S. A.) a pro-
fessional horse-breaker (Skuthorpe, of Moree, N.S.W.)
accomplished a leap that has never been attempted since
Adam Lindsay Gordon did it 40 years ago. Skuthorpe,
riding a hunter called Wallace, had a run of 24ft. on a
macadamised road at a jump over a gutter and a three-
rail fence, 4ft. 3in. high, alighting on a strip of ground
10ft. wide, from which there is a precipitous fall of 250 ft.
to the Blue Lake. The leap, which was witnessed by
hundreds of spectators, amidst great excitement, was
managed with perfect success. The horse was stopped in
his stride as he touched ground, the rider being merely
unseated by the shock. The slightest blunder meant
certain death. What a mad freak !
* * • *
M.L. bruiser, Otto Cribb, hastily made up his
mind last week and left for Yankeeland per Mariposa on
Wednesday, he having only got back from Melb. previous
day. Mick Dunn and Dan O’Connell were waiting for
promised matches—latter with backing—but Cribb
couldn’t be induced to stay. He will find the opposition
in the U.S. a lot stiffer than here, and will be lucky if he
doesn’t hump up against something too hot early. He is,
scientifically, not a top-notcher—he’s merely a good,
strong, spunky rough ’un, and there are plenty of his sort
there. Besides, though the money offered" may look
bigger, it won’t pan out as well as that earned in Austra-
lia. Over yonder there are too many to cut it up—the
necessary manager’s whack being bulkier than the pug’s
as a rule.
• * * V
The matching of old rivals, Tim Hegarty and
Jack M’Gowan, for £lOO aside is the latest pug sensation.
Condition—26 rounds, with as many more as referee
may deem desirable to justify a decision, which means a
knock out; so, bar accidents, there’s sure to be a winner
this time. Australian light-weight supremacy is in-
volved, as M‘Gowan won that by beating Peter Murphy,
whose claim stood out. Cribb would have owned the
title had he stayed here, through M‘G. refusing to meet
him at the lOst. limit.
“Old Timer”: Saw champion bantam Bert
Weekes at work on ebon-hued Peter Edwards (“ Young
Peter Jackson”), in Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney), Saturday
night. The black’s case was one of palpable fright and
quit. He tried to be counted out twice, but referee
McDonald wouldn’Phave it, and warned him that there’d
be no loser’s end if he didn’t stand up. ’Twas no good—
Peter’d, sooner lie down than be thumped, and the thing
was stopped. Edwards was “ buttons ”at old York-street
(Sydney) S.A,G. Club 10 years ago, and learned the game
in a good school, but wasn’t built for it. Writer remem-
bers Peter having ’em on with Griffo for a spar one day.
Griffo accidentally thumped a bit hard once, and knocked
all zest out of the black, who took off the gloves and
blubbered. Why they called him “ Young Peter Jack-
son ”is a poser. The only resemblance is that he’s black.
Jackson hails from Santa Cruz, while Edwards is ft
Mauritian.

North Sydney A.A.C.’s initial sports meeting
on Saturday attracted a big crowd, and several competi-
tors from Vic. and Q. Best performance was credited to
Bananaland heel-and-toe man, A. B. Wilcox, who covered
ft mile in the very speedy time of 6.50, and under rules
recently adopted by Australasian A.A.U.
* * * •
Old man hammer-thrower M. Fitzpatrick was
lately gathered to the fold of Amateurism (which is very
sick just now) and made his first bow under the newly
acquired status at North Sydney A. A. C’s. sports
Saturday. From scratch (in a 9ft. circle) he threw the
161 b. hammer 109 ft. 3in. Second man Lyon (with 18ft.
allowance) couldn’t get nearer than 106 ft. Holder of
Amateur premiership—though entered and getting a good
start —didn’t toe the mark.

Thirteenth amateur sculling premiership of
Victoria over Lower Yarra (2-j- miles) on Saturday was won
easily by previous holder, 0. "Donald (Albert Park), with
P. C. Ivens (Mercantile) second, and C. E. Macnamara
(Mercantile) third. Time, 19.7. Result was confidently
anticipated. This is Donald’s third successive win.

Big Australasian bowling tourney promoted by
M. 0.0. (Melb.) begins on Monday next. Contest for
individual supremacy of the continent has attracted 128
entrants from M.L., Tas., Vic., and N.S.W.; hence right
of winner to the title cannot be questioned. N.S.W.
team to meet Victorians in usual annual match leaves
Sydney on Friday, next week.
« • • •
Fast-bowler Jones gets £llO a year from S. A.
C.A. as a retainer to play cricket for the wheat State, and
the agreement expires this month. It is expected that
Jones will be offered about half that sum in future, and
whether he will accept depends upon what offers come
from other States. Jones is supposed to have the refusal
of a Melb. billet.
• * • *
Dates for the inter-State cricket matches have
so far, barring the first contest between Vie. and S.A.
next week, not been agreed on. Why don’t the different
associations meet early in every season to fix programme ?
* * * *
The ill-managed and garrulous League of Vic.
Wheelmen, which wasn’t fit to manage an ice-cream cart,
practically received its knock-out on Saturday evening,
when all the racing cyclists who had promised to support
it sent unconditional entries to Melb. Bicycle Olub, in-
curring the threatened disqualification of the League,
whatever that may be worth. The sport will be conducted
far better by the M.8.C., and if the League has any sense
it will quietly collapse and cut up its assets among its
survivors. By the way, its assets are only £2OO, and
“ out of this ” £3OO is due to a fund for injured cyclists.
* * * *
Crack Yankee cyclists—Lawson, Chapman,
Vaughan, and Bowles—reached Sydney per Moana in
quest of biz. Lawson and Chapman are front rankers.
Former set up new figures for a half-mile competition
(55 2-5) just ere leaving for Australia. Best ever done
here was Gordon’s 56. Chapman has been among first-
flighters for years. Vaughan, too, comes well endorsed.
Said that U.S. top-notchers Cooper and M'Farland will
arrive later. These are bracketed with Major Taylor as
the speediest men on t’other side.
Top-notch English sprint cyclist, Jack Green,
is aboard incoming P. and O. liner Britannia, and will
land at Melb. He is fresh from Paris Exhibition races,
where he shaped well, but didn’t get away with much
loot. In semi-finals of the Grand Prix Green went under
to Frenchman Bourillon, after a severe struggle, only
won by inches. Green gave Australia a taste of his
quality once before, when he stayed two years.
♦»‘ * *
“Plugger” Bill Martin got a great reception
on Adelaide Oval, t’other Saturday. The crowd cheered
and clapped, and Bill, sleeker and apparently less aggres-
sive than of yore, seemed to be shaking hands with every-
body. In his heat for the big race he was' well to the
front when the bell went, but then he was hustled clean
out of it. Everyone appeared to bump him, and he fell
back to the ruck. A sigh went up from the gallery for
the Bilmartin of other days, who would have strewn the
grass with the bleeding carcases of such a rabble. Then
Bill, twirling a piece of string between his thumbs, was
looking for somebody. It was the judges or referee.
What occurred the crowd could not tell, for Bill was
smiling blandly and shook hands some more.
* * * *
Champion Roberts was fairly done on the last
day of his match with Weiss. The figures speak for
themselves. The question of luck one way or other is
not worth discussing. Soon after Weiss had passed the
19,000 mark Roberts pulled himself together for a supreme
effort, and scored—this was on the Thursday—lsB6 points
to Weiss’s 909, but it was noticeable that towards the end
of the evening he missed easy shots. It was Weiss who
had the balls in play when time was called. Scores,
20,131 to 19,420. On the last day, therefore, Roberts was
required to make 1580 before Weiss could put in 896. He
had got within 711 points of the long start man, but he
was finally beaten by more than 800. In short, his nerve
was not equal to the strain.
* * * •
A Maoriland owner who dreamt that he rode
his ’chaser over five gorse hedges, and then jumped over a
cliff, found the mare dead at the bottom of a precipice
next morning.
* * * *
That plucky little amateur pedaller, W. Dowd,
outpaced all objectors in the 5-miles Amateur Austra-
lasian Championship at the Brisbane trophy meet, and
scored another victory for the silver ribbon Massey-Harris
wheel.*
Duncan Gillies, the shiny, red gentleman
of Vic. politics, asked as a candidate what he
thinks is a fair wage for a workman, always re-
plies: “As much as he can get.” Frank Madden,
the man who has the appearance of being badly
built of bluestone, has caught up the cry and it is
echoed by Tory candidates who want to dodge
questions about the Factories Act. But Gillies
gives his case away. The workers are entitled to
as much as they can get—by Factory Acts, Mini-
mum Wage Acts, Protective tariffs, and any other
means. And being entitled to as much as they
can get they are also entitled to elect men who
will help them to get it—not men like Gillies and
Madden.
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
that the harvest prospects are still lading slightly. In
some of the western districts hot winds and lack of rain
have so scorched up the crop that it will have to be cut
for hay. Fortunately, in other districts, especially the
Riverina, the crops continue to flourish, and
unless a cold snap or a plague of locusts call
in, the 1900-1 season will still be a record one.
In Victoria and South Australia prospects are fairly
satisfactory, but the yield per acre is expected to be
less than our own. In the market wheat is still stagnant.
Freights continue to be as murderous as Jimmy Governor,
and as scarce as Joe, and there is an easier tone in Mark
Lane ; so shippers are doing nothing, while millers con-
tinue to buy only on a falling market. One or two large
lines have changed hands for dispatch to the other side,
but the figures paid are below what growers care to part
for. Some optimistic people declare that January next
will see an improvement, but the fact that freights will
probably be about as high and that the new grain will be
pouring into the chief seaports of the continent rather
indicates that the previous season’s grain will be utterly
squelched by that of the present harvest. Meanwhile
quotations may be given : Prime 2s. Bd., F.A.Q. 2s. 7d. to
2s. 7id. ; punched and chickwheat 2s. 2d. to 2s. 4d. per
bushel.
The action of the Queensland Government in the
matter of the late James Tyson’s estate, savors
very much of highway robbery. It not only
wants to charge succession-duty on Tyson’s
property in Queensland, but it demands nearly
£70,000 duty on his property in other States. The
basis of the claim is that the wandering millionaire,
who had homes and property in three States, and
roved in his grey suit over a wide expanse of
territory, was “domiciled” in Queensland. Of
course, if the Government wins its action it will
recover the money out of that part of the estate
which is within its reach. And as the N.S.W.
and Victorian properties must pay succession
duties in these States, a great part of the estate
will thus be taxed twice. In fact there seems no
reason why the N.S.W. and Victorian Govern-
ments should not also find that Tyson was more
or less domiciled within their territories, arid like-
wise charge succession-duty on the whole 'estate,
in which case it would all be taxed three times.
Any Parliament has power to tax to any extent it
pleases property within its territory, but when it
claims the right to tax property not in its territory
it seems very like bushranging without violence.
Apparently if a rich man had property scattered
over the seven States of Australasia, and divided
his time among them pretty equally, it might be
decided after his death that he was domiciled in
all of them, and then when the whole seven had
levied succession-duty on his estate there might
be no estate left.
Alleged that when the N.S.W. China
contingent arrived in Chow-land and was in-
spected by British officers, its band greeted them
with that joyful tune from the “Belle of New
York ” —“ Of course you can never be like us, but
be as like us as you can manage to be.”
George Rolfe, of the Cauliflower Inn,
Botany-road, Waterloo (Sydney), has just died,
aged 80 years. He had held the licence continu-
ously since 1850.
The A.N.A. organ. Advance Australia,
publishes a wild diatribe against The Bulle-
tin :
That unique example of presumption and arrogance—
the Sydney Bulletin— is again vilifying the A.N.A. That
newspaper, the predominant feature of which, next to
its diatribes of abuse, is its consistent inconsistency, is
envious of the great success of the A.N.A., and jealous
lest the recent development of our organisation in
N.S.W. and the inevitable foothold which our association
must secure in every centre of population throughout
the Commonwealth, may relegate that newspaper to its
proper place. It is not so overwhelmed with the sense of
its own importance as not to recognise that once the
A.N.A. is federated and possessed of one common policy
that it will be a power in the land. Nor
is it so purblind to its own interests as
to fail to appreciate that article of our faith
which requires the suppression or incineration of all ob-
scene literature and indecent advertisements. To effect
that purpose will be part of our national work ; indeed,
the Victorian section of the A.N.A. has already started to
do it.
That pro-Boer quasi-low-comic newspaper, with charac-
teristic audacity and unblushing impertinence, claims
amongst its aspirations that it is “ an Australian national
newspaper.” The experiences of the past twelve months
have proved that it by no means reflects Australian
national opinion. Tub Bulletin is “an Australian
national newspaper” merely by assumption of title. We
do not hesitate to charge it with being a nefarious medium
for the spreading of the germs of mental disease amongst
the youth of our nation.
The Bulletin, it may be remembered, pointed
out that the A.N. A.’s medical benefit scheme was
a gross and shameful case of sweating the medi-
cal profession. And the nearest the A.N.A. can
get to proving that it doesn’t sweat the medical
profession is to yell “ Pro-Boer !” What a lot of
dog-meanness is successfully explained away
nowadays by merely saying that somebody else is
“Pro-Boe r 1”
THE BIRD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Give War God one chance,” I heard
A voice in dreamland say.
'Twas like the chirping of a bird
When brightly dawns the day—
The early bird that catches worms
And gobbles them with glee ;
It must have flown from Flemington
To chirp those words to me—
“ Give War God one more chance.”
“ There’s Tarquin, Chief, La Carabine ”
I muttered, dozing still—
“ The grass upon the Flat is green,
And likewise on the Hill.”
“ This world is rich in verdant things,
But flats look often blue,”
The bird observed ; then spread its wings,
Repeating as it flew—
“ Give War God one more chance."
A hint to turn some verses off,
A “ tip ” to put in rhyme.
And if it fails ? Then readers scoff,
Forgetful of the time
When Mentor did the blessed trick.
A winning “ tip” looks well,
But he who doth a loser pick
Is d d to blackest h 1.
“ Give War God one more chance.”
The line is twisted in my brain,
I cannot get it out,
I wish the bird would come again
To ease me of a doubt.
Will War God take the prize outright,
Or be a runner-up,
Or finish with the “ second flight ”
Whilst Malster wins the Cup ?
“ Give War God one more chance.”
Big Georgy Fred’s a noble steed,
A son of Carbine, too,
i;d in.the straight he’ll hold a lead,
Provided he gets through.
Severity’s a cert., they say,
And Mora, she can go ;
Young Barbarossa, built to stay,
Must surely have a show.
“ Give War God one more chance.”
Black Merriwee, a year agone,
Was speedy, stout and kind ;
Men tell me, in an undertone—
“ He’ll not be far behind.”
Fleet Admiral, a luckless nag,
Might start in winning whim ;
The bookie with the leather bag
Would loudly' welcome him.
“ Give War God one more chance.”
They’ll serve up Kinglike pretty warm :
Perchance he’ll teach us soon
He wasn’t running up to “ form ”
The other afternoon
There’s Lancaster, with handy weight—
A “ monte ” 1 it is said ;
And Clean Sweep I must gravely state,
Can romp ’ome on ’is ’ead.
“ Give War God one more chance.”
Heigho 1 Give War God one more chance.
His claim is fairly strong ;
He will not lead the field a dance
He’s loth to come along ;
But still he’s there when others drop
Behind the foremost few.
I’ll back the old ’un win and “ shop,”
Instead of something new.
Give War God one more chance.
That dreamland bird with “ tip ” absurd,
I wait for it in vain,
To bring to me one final word
And make its meaning plain.
Each night I place a feast of crumbs
Beside my little bed,
But, seeing that it never comes,
Let’s hope my bird is dead.
“Give War God one more chance.”
EDMUND FISHER.

Jemmy Governor is to be sent to Sydney
for trial at Darlinghurst. In 1863, N.S.W. Govt,
issued instructions to all country benches to com-
mit all cases of bushranging to a special commis-
sion, presided over by Alfred Stephen, at Darling-
hurst, and that course was followed for years. It,
at all events, made Sydney the head show spot.

Milton Rathbun, of New York, recently
undertook a long fast, for the second time, to re-
duce his weight, but such heroic methods are not
likely to be adopted by many. Moderation in
diet, however, has long been recommended in
conjunction with certain mineral waters, the use
of which is strongly advised by medical authori- i
ties. For instance, the British “ Medical Jour- |
nal ” goes so far as to say that the efficiency of
Apenta, a Hungarian aperient water,_ for the sys-
tematic treatment of obesity, is clinically estab-
lished. Professor Gerhardt, Berlin, says that
Apenta produces a reduction of fat in the body
without detriment, as the general health of the
patient suffers in no wise, the cure running its
course in a satisfactory manner.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
Premier John Forrest addressing the
Westralian Assembly re the Perth Ice Co. that
very remarkable concern which is charged (or
some of its officials are) with wholesale and retail
frauds on the Railway Department (Adelaide
Observer report 20/10/1900):
Instead of blaming the Directors, if people are as
honest-minded as thev ought to be, they will sympathise
with them, because not only have their names been
bandied about the country in a way they ought not to be,
but they have lost their property ; and shareholders hold-
ing shares worth £2 have sold them for 10s. —they were
so disgusted with what had taken place. I sold my
shares for £2 after the matter came to light, as I was run
after for them, and did not wish to be mixed up with the
Company.
If that is a true report —well, it looks as if John
Forrest, who surely ought to have had a better
chance than some people to know the real size of
the exposure and disaster that were coming, sold
out from the noblest motives and escaped the
worst of the slump. Also that someone else, who
perhaps hadn’t such good information, is now in
the hole that he climbed out of, for there is talk
of a call, or perhaps more than one, to make good
the suras that the Ice Co, didn’t pay to the Rail-
way Department.

A Fact That Looks Queer.
Here is a fact, which, on the face of it, looks
queer The more we multiply destructive guns
and other slaughtering machines, the less is the
percentage of men killed in battle.
Caesar thought if at least 50 per cent of the
participants were not killed outright in one of his
engagements, he had stumbled upon a meeting of
the Universal Peace Society.
In Napoleon’s wars the killed in battle averaged
25 per cent., in the American Civil War 20 per
cent., and in our little South African debate, it
has been about seven or eight per cent.
At this rate, in a few years more, the only
really safe lace for a timid man will be a big
battle ; and the insurance rates on the lives of
military gentlemen in active practice will be
lower than the rates on tract distributors or hotel
porters.
The reason for this will be mentioned, if I don’t
forget it, later on in the present writing. Mean-
while, a cognate topic comes up —the question
why disease does more damage than gunpowder,
cold steel, or dynamite.
For over twenty years Mrs. Eliza Maybrey was
one of the wounded in the great army that never
handles lyddite shells or Krag-Jorgensen rifles.
She says she constantly retched and spit up
phlegm. Associated with this disabling trouble
there was a hacking cough which never let up day
or night. She says she often had to fairly fight
for her breath, and was so exhausted with the
struggle that she could hardly get about the
house ; and that her stomach was so weak as to
render eating almost useless, considered as a
means of renewing strength.
| |“I took,” says the lady, “so many medicines
during my long contest with my complaint, that
I pretty much lost faith in drugs. Then a neigh-
bour told me what good Mother Seigel’s Syrup
had done her, and I began taking it on her word,
and was quickly relieved. My symptoms got
milder, and in the course of a rew weeks I ate and
digested anything I wanted, and grew strong and
well. I know many in this district who have been
cured by Mother Seigel’s Syrup.”—Mrs. Eliza
Maybrey, Earlswood, Shirenewton, near Chep-
stow, April sth, 1899.
Seated with the rest of us around a campfire,
another soldier of the legion says “Up to the
early part of last year I had always enjoyed the
blessing of good health. Then I fell by the way-
side. I could not make out what ailed me. I had
no strength, and food did me no good. My flesh
wasted away until my clothing hung on me as,
it might be, on a wire figure. When I was
worrying over my miserable condition, T read of
what Mother Seigel’s Syrup had done for others
in the same sad plight, and got a bottle from
Price’s Drug Stores. In a week I was better, and
was soon completely cured.”—Mrs. Mary Bagley,
29a, Lower Trinity-street, West Bromwich, near
Birmingham, Sept. Bth, 1899.
“For many years,” say another —a man this
time—“l suffered from indigestion. I felt low,
weak, and heavy. My appetite was poor, and
after eating I had pain in the chest. I was often
troubled with wind on the stomach, which rose
into my throat and half choked me. I had much
pain at the heart, and palpitation when I walked
of worked. Finally, I was obliged to give up my
work. Doctors and medicines failing, I tried
Mother Seigel’s Syrup, which helped me right out
of hand ; and in a brief time it cured me. lam
now strong and well as before the disease attacked
me.” —J. W. Scott, Postmaster, Shirenewton,
near Chepstow, Mon., April sth, 1899.
The reason why the casualties decrease in
modem battles, notwithstanding the fearful
weapons of destruction used, is this:—Armies
fight at long ranges, and take every advantage of
shelter. On the day when Alexander slew 100,000
Persians between sun and sun, the armies fought
face to face, and chopped one another into bits
with swords. Let the Peace people not fret;
military science will presently rob war of all its
terrors, and battlefields will be popular with
parties out on picnics.
But as for the war against disease, that will be
conducted on the old principles with Mother
Seigel’s Syrup whipping the enemy in every
conflict.
In the row between Sleath, M.L.A., chairman
of N.S.W. Military Inquiry Committee, who puts
his feet on the table, and Major-Gen. French
who refuses to answer certain questions put to
him by the man with his feet on the table, there
is much to be said for the General. Answering
Sleath’s questions is publishing the weakness of
the Cornstalk defences to all the world, which
means doing possible serious harm. And it isn’t
likely to produce any corresponding good, for the
non-military committee and the non-military
.Parliament won’t understand the gravity of the
case if French explains it. The information will
be mostly wasted on them, and it won’t be wasted
by any means on the outside world. Probably if
the whole lamentable story was told at length in
the papers it would make so little impression on
a public that didn’t comprehend the details of
the case that it wouldn’t result in the provision of
an extra man or an extra gun. And if outsiders
didn’t know the whole story before, they certainly
would know all about it after that pu-blic ex-
planation.
It would really appear that nobody save
the N.S.W. Govt, is responsible for that dreary
eyesore, Devonshire-st. cemetery, Sydney. The
place is supposed to be governed by trustees, but
the last batch of trustees are all dead, and as they
pegged out one by one nobody remembered to
appoint any successors. The place owes 20 years’
municipal rates, but there is nobody to collect
them from. So the tombstones totter at their
own sweet will, and the ungoverned bones run
riot over the premises. When a city is ruled by
43 councils and multitudes of boards, trusts,
commissions, &c., some body or other is always
liable to die out unnoticed, and leave something
without care or guidance, and until the smell of
that something becomes too apparent, nobody
notices that an institution is missing.

Nominal Capital, £25,000, in 50,000 Shares of 10s. each, divided as follows:

PROVISIONAL DIRECTORS :
Dr H V C HINDER, and Messrs. T. H. MARTIN, J. S. HUTCHINSON, K. S.
' HUNGERFORD, and W. S. JACOB.

SECRETARY :
ALFRED H. ROSS, 12 Post-Office Chambers, Pitt-street, Sydney.
The Company has acquired an area of about 214 acres on the Buckland River, Victoria, in the
Parishes of Porepunkah, Wandihgong, and Buckland, and situated on the site of the famous Oxley
Diggings, and will work same by means of Bucket Dredges.
The property has been thoroughly prospected by Mr. K. S. Hungerford and Mr. H, R. Butter-
worth, M.E., A' 0.5. M., who has had many successful years experience in gold dredging m all its
branches in New Zealand.
The full reports of these .gentlemen may be had on application to the secretary, Mr. Alfred H
Ross at 12 Post Office-chambers, Pitt-street, Sydney, and by referring to these it will be seen that
the special features of the property, as shown in Mr. Hungerford s report, are as follow :
1. A large percentage of the leases is virgin ground, it having been too deep and wet to treat in
2 That even in the worked ground only the bottom wash has been treated.
3. That the gold found on the Buckland is coarse, and easily saved, and, moreover, is worth from
£4 2s. to £4 3s. per ounce. » ~ ~
4 That the top wash, which represents 12 to 15 feet of gravel, contains throughout highly-pay able
5 That headings and tailings, which lie on the surface in thousands of tons, prospected by him,
contained an average value of 12.7 grains per cubic yard. .
6. That ten Chinamen, working adjoining ground, were, at the time of Mr. Hungerford s inspec-
tion, obtaining 250 z. of coarse gold fortnightly. .
7 That the rock bottom is fairly even, and boulders are conspicuous by their absence.
8 That tliG ground on the whole of the flats on the leas©s can b© easily dredged, and there is no
9. That the water supply is plentiful, and firewood at hand unlimited.
Then by referring to Mr. Butterworth’s report, it will be seen that the most important features
if Mr. Hungerford’s report are corroborated, and from an engineering point of view.
Mr. Butterworth points out: —
1 That the whole of the property is. eminently suited for bucket dredging.
2. That there are no shallow rock bars and no large stones. , , ,
3. That practically there is no overburden, and, consequently, the dredges will have no dead and
4 That well within the mark to assume that there are 7,782,400 cubic yards of material
‘ capable of being dredged, and the value of this, taking the contents to be only 6 grains per
yard, would be £399,360, which would mean an annual income of more than the capital value
of the Company for 15 years to come.
It is also important to note that the rental of dredging ground in Victoria is only 2s. 6d. per acre
oer annum as against 20s. per acre per annum in New South Wales, and that gold from the claim is
worth £1 2s. per oz., as against the average of £3 17s. per oz. m New South Wales.
The Comoany has already acquired the machinery for the Dredge from the Australian Gold-
Dredging Co., Ltd., 10,000 feet of timber is at the claim, and Mr. Butterworth some time past started
his men at work building t ie pontoon.
The Dredge when finished, will have a working capacity of at least 8000 loads per week, and
when in commission will be one of the most up-to-date Dredges in the Colonies.
As obtaining machinery when required has been the stumbling block hitherto in the way of quick
Iredge-buildingf and as the machinery of this dredge is ready to be sent to the claim, it is safe to say
-hat gold winning will be in progress in March next year.
Any further information relative to the Company will be gladly supplied at the registered office,
12 Post-Office Chambers, Pitt-street, Sydney.
Pious Professor Harper, of Melbourne,
lecturing on the question “ Must a preacher prac-
tise all that he preaches?” says the parson must
not “keep down to the level of his own ethical
attainment.” Which may mean that the parson
need not trouble himself greatly to act up to his
published principles. “ Indeed,” says the Prof.>
“I counsel even a certain recklessness in this
matter” —that is in the excess of precept —“ for
whatever happens the ideal must be preached.”
Writes “Yon Kotze ” :
The Bulletin lately remarked on the relative value of
cleaning the streets and deodorising the language used
therein, which impels me to draw attention to the fact
that, despite the boasted spiritualism of our religion, we
still seem to value the body above the soul in our conven-
tions. The average well-meaning zealot will not hesitate
to publicly ask you, in a sepulchral voice, whether you
are prepared to meet your God ; or what you think of
Death, or Satan, or the sixth’chapter of Revelations ; nay,
he glories in what he is pleased to call “probing your
faith.” Vet, granted afly conventions at all, to a thought-
ful and rea'ly civilised being a naked soul in a public
place (within the meaning of the Act) must appear far
more indecent than a naked body. The benevolent prose-
lytiser thinks nothing of asking you to bare your heart to
him, in the presence of a sombrelv appreciative audience.
But if you ask the same benevolent party, for the sake of
an amateurish drawing-room experiment in pathology, to
publicly take off his clothes, the consequences would be
too horrible to contemplate. Yet Mrs. Grundy considers
herself a deeply pious lady, who places the soul far above
mere clay. Also, she claims the respectability that treats
all it meets according to precedence. There’s something
wrong somewhere —or is our boasted civilisation only a
very thin sort of varnish ?
Sydney advt.: —
HLAIEVOYANOE. —Mrs. O’Donoghue, daily.
O Seance To-night, 6d. Mr. O’Donoghue away all
this week. 2078 Brown-st.
The M‘Lean Government’s policy of re-
purchasing large estates in Victoria for purposes
of closer settlement has grieved the House of lucri
Landlords, which solemnly recorded, in its debate
on the proposed Whitfield purchase, that the
policy of settling the people on the land was
having an unsettling effect on the people. -this
is surely the Council’s prize insanity up to date.
When 'a man buys a farm he generally settles
down there for life. Supplying farms is fixing
the people on the soil. And the idea that a man
is more unsettled after he has got a farm and
settled down than before, when he was wandering
around looking for a farm, is a wondrous inspira-
tion on the part of the 48 Rich Dodderers.
The report of the officers of H.M.S. Mil-
dura, recently returned from a New Hebrides
cruise, shows plainly how things are drifting in
that group. In all cases of disputed land-owner-
ship the French trader “undoubtedly proves his
claim,” and “we are slowly but surely being
pushed out.” Besides, the French trader can
pursue his business unhampered by restrictions
that applv to his British or Australian brother.
The latter is forbidden to sell arms, ammunition,
or spirits to the native, whilst Johnny Crapaud
may deal in these articles at his own sweet will.
Unless the restriction be applied all round, the
Dual Control of the group exists solely for the
benefit of one party to the arrangement. I he
sooner Australia takes a leading hand m the
administration of this and the Solomon group,
the better for those of her people who have in-
vested labour and capital in them.
Go and preach to the people of saintliness still,
Forgive nothing of weakness, no sin in the herd,
But partake of the pleasures of life if you will,
For the parson need never be man of his “ Word ! ’

Actuary:
JAMES PULLAR, F.F.A.
Consulting Actuary
ROBERT THOMSON, F.I.A.
Report by the Directors i
connection with the Fifth Actuarial investigation of the
Affairs of the Society.
PTIHE Directors have the pleasure to present to the Members their Report in
connection with the Actuarial Investigation for the Quinquennium ended 31st
December, 1899, together with the Report of the Actuary and of the
Consulting Actuary to the Society.
In accordance with the Reports of the Actuaries, the Directors have satisfaction
in announcing that the surplus at the end of the Quinquennium amounted to
£278,879 14s. 4d.
Of this sum the Board has decided to add £67,969 to the Investment
Fluctuation Fund, bringing it up to £102,776 Os. 3d.
The Board has also decided to specially reserve a further sum of £35 000 to
bring the valuation to an Hm 3 J per cent, basis.
The amount of £175,910 14s- 4d. is thus left for allotment and distribution.
This, together with the amount of £25,864 15s. 3d. distributed during the
Quinquennium, as certified by the Actuary, is equal in reversion to over
£400,000.
In view of the fall in the rate of interest throughout the world, the Directors,
after most careful consideration, have deemed it desirable at once to make provision
for valuing on a3| per cent, basis. They feel assured that although this decision
involves a smaller bonus than might otherwise have been declared, they will receive
the hearty support of the members in their resolve thus to strengthen the reserves,
the effect of which will be to place the Society in an absolutely unassailable position
and lead to an increased bonus-earning power in the future.
P A he^:r” t : i r r T J theamOUntat ° redi * ° f the Fluctuation
nil o £102,776 Os. 3d. was arrived at after complete investigation of all the
assets of the Society. The Directors, however, hope that a smaller amount will in
actual experience, be found sufficient, but they have preferred to take a conservative
view rather than discount a possible or probable improvement in values in the
future.
The last five years have constituted an important period in the Society’s
history, the Directors having during that time modified the former policy of expan-
■on, and devoted themselves specially to consolidating the business and strengthen-
ing the position of the Society. The figures above quoted indicate how successful
that policy has proved resulting, as it has, in so large a surplus being disclosed,
notwithstanding the low rates of interest which have prevailed, and enabling the
Society at once to change the valuation rate to per cent.
The Directors feel that this is a result upon which they may congratulate the
members of the Society.
HENRY HENTY, Chairman.
LESLIE J. PARK, General Manager.
Melbourne, 4th October, 1900.

George Gordon M‘Crae, in The Arena,
sets down a memory of old-time Melbourne :
In those early times the gaol lay on the westernmost
edge of the town, somewhere, as I seem to recollect, close
to Batman’s Hill. The then new gaol (still standing, and
included within the walls of the gaol of to-day) was not
yet ready to receive prisoners. Nevertheless, in capital
cases, the execution was always carried into effect at “ the
new gaol on the hill ” ; possibly, as being the more public
and conspicuous place of the two. As the distance to be
traversed between the gaols was something considerable,
the condemned had to be conveyed by cart to the place of
axecution ; and, from being followed in the first instance
by a few boys and idle persons, the crowd would grow and
grow, and take form until it found itself a procession. I
remember once, while on a visit to my uncle, Mr. David
John Thomas, at his house in Bourke-st. West, opposite St.
James’s (there were no intervening buildings then), I was
roused one morning by someone, and rushed out of bed
on to the verandah, whence I witnessed one of these pro-
cessions. I afterwards came to compare this one with the
last picture in the Idle Apprentice series ; the progress to
Tyburn ; and, certainly, it was to the full, as sad, as
“ outrC,” and as replete with grim humour as anything
Hogarth ever committed to canvas. First, yes 1 I think
it was first, came the cart, with three men (bushrangers
all) seated on three coffins, a clergyman or priest in the
cart with them, book in hand. Immediately behind
walked Jack Ketch, with a great sack over his shoulder,
then a guard of soldiers ; next, the “ procession ” grow-
ing bigger and bigger as the cart creaked along slowly on
its way. Some hours later I witnessed the return of the
cart and guard, for the procession, apparently tired of
the show, had dispersed and melted away. Here we had
Mr. Ketch again, with his hag ; this time he was riding
in the cart, still smoking contentedly, and his bag (so I
was told) filled with the clothes and boots of his late
charges, which, if not actually bequeathed to him, were
in any case his lawful and traditional perquisites.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.*
The recently reported death of Henry
Ward Beecher’s brother recalls the unsavory
Ward Beecher-Tilton divorce case which stirred
up the States many years ago. Rutherford Hayes
and Sam Tilden were contesting the Presidential
election and unscrupulous supporters of Hayes
took advantange of the similarity of names to
confuse the electors as between Tilton and lilden.
The dodge worked, too, especially amongst the
backblock puritans. Hayes (about the weakest
president the TT.S. has had, but a most simple and
honorable man) was indignant at the deception,
and did all in his power to disillusionise the
electors. At the close of his presidency Hayes is
reported to have said “Well, they say I have not
been a success as president. I’m going back to my
chicken ranch, they can’t say I’m not a success at
that.” Then he dropped so completely out of
public sight that few of the present generation of
Americans know that ex-President ■ Hayes is
peacefully tending his flocks in the Ohio valley.
“Yang Anyut ” : A simple, cheap, and
exceedingly useful idea by way of celebrating the
beginnings of the Commonwealth would be the
adoption of a decimal coinage -say 10 pennies,
one federal; 10 federals, one florin ;10 florins, one
pound. And at the same time let us adopt the
decimal system all round, and do away with those
fearful tables of weights and measures in which
30J square yards or 272 J square feet make a pole,
and yards make a perch. It takes the average
small child about three months to learn these
brain-racking curiosities, where the tables on the
decimal system could be learned in a week.
Twelve weeks gone out of everybody’s life —
230,000 years wasted out of the lifetime of a
million people—by this antiquated British foolish-
ness.
FOUR YEARS ILL HEALTH,
Sleepless, Weary and Depressed.
RELIEF DESPAIRED OF.
But a “Friend in Need.”
The Case of
MRS. SUSAN BASTOCK.
(For the Star
Mi's Susan Bastock, who lives at No. 72
J&ay-street, Glebe, Sydney, welcomed the
Siewspaper man with much politeness, and
on the latter announoing that his visit was
ipaid with the object of eliciting the facts
Relating to that lady’s critical illness and her
tatraordinary recovery therefrom through
*3 administration of Clements Tonic, he
y/as soon seated in the front parlor, where
Mrs. Bastock readily assented to supply him
with every detail connected with her case.
“To look at me now you would hardly
think that I am the mother of a family of
thirteen children, and considering the great
deal of suffering which I have gone through
I ought to be—and indeed I am—very
thankful for my present good state of
health. The great physical strain of rearing
such a large family must have weakened my
system terribly, for it ultimately resulted in
my having a premature confinement, which
culminated in inflammation of the bowels.
It took a long time before I could get about
again, and for four years afterwards I never
possessed good health; and at the expiration
of that time I was visited with a sickness of
a more serious kind than my previous one.
It is considered an extremely interesting and
critical period of a woman’s existence, and
one which is fraught with important con-
sequences ; for, according as it is passed
with safety or otherwise will her future
health be. olt was at the period when my
change of life took place, and never shall I be
able to describe the pain which I had to en-
dure. Women usually regard the approach of
the change of life with a good deal of anxiety
and fear, for at that time certain diseases
which may have lain dormant, as it were,
in the system, are likely to make their
appearance, and I was in dread of any extra
derangement, because my constitution was
not strong enough to withstand it. lam
glad to say that I was not attacked by any
disease during my illness, but I had quite
enough to bear as it was, without any other
disorders taking place.”
“ Were your sufferings very acute, Mrs.
Bastock ? ”
“They were extremely so. The pains
were so bad that I could not bear anyone
to touch me. When anyone approached
very close to me I would give a spasmodic
flinch as though anticipating their touch.
Sleepless nights I passed without number,
and I was always weary and depressed. I
think there are very few women who have
gone through the same ordeal and have ex-
perienced such dreadful headaches as mine
were. My eyesight became affected, and I
could not see to read ; in fact, I did not have
much inclination for reading. I was too
much worried with my deplorable condition
to take any interest in books or papers.
Frequently, hot flushing of the face would
take place, and then I would be conscious of
a feeling that can only be described as ner-
vous irritation. It was quite a common
thing for me to faint, and I would lie and
think of all the evils that may occur if I did
not soon get better. My digestive organs
were quite upset, for although I lay for
fully a fortnight without touching anything
in the way of food excepting a little toast
and water, and even that light nourishment
was too much for my stomach to digest. I
was never free from pain of some sort, and
in a state of desperation I would sometimes
take a little brandy or whisky in the hope of
acquiring reliei But stimulants only aggra-
vated my suffering, and I always felt worse
after them I had got awfully thin, and my
face looked as though there was not a drop
of blood left in me. The intense mental
depression experienced during my sickness
was sometimes more than I like to think of
even now.”
“What did your doctor think of your
case ? ”
“I had three doctors, and I am sure they
all despaired of my ever pulling through it.
But they were not the only ones who thought
so. One day a lady friend, who had just
returned from the funeral of her sister, said
to me: ‘ Ah, old girl, you will be the next I
shall have to follow.’ It is not very en-
couraging to be spoken to like that when a
person feels half-dead. My medical advisers
were very attentive, I must say ; but their
medicines all failed to have a beneficial effect
upon me. Goodness knows how much I had
taken, but I still remained in the same sorry
plight. So low did I sink that I believed I
was drawing near the grave. My husband’s
belief in the restorative and curative qualities
of Clements Tonic had so often been drummed
in my ears that at last I consented to see
what it was like. I had lost faith in all
medicines, and when my husband brought
me a bottle of Clements Tonic I succeeded
in livening myself up with hope again, for I
slowly, but surely, began to mend under its
remarkable influence. The bodily pains
were quickly under control, my dreadful
headaches were soon completely cured, and
the newly-found remedy gave me a very
healthy appetite. I continued taking Cle-
ments Tonic regularly, as I was convinced
it was doing me a lot of good. My blood
was once again running through my veins
with increased vitality, and I could always
depend upon having a good night’s sleep.
With the departure of my severe headaches
I could sit down and enjoy a read, my
eyesight being completely repaired. The
feelings of depression departed with the
advent of new life, and as the time passed
on it was soon my pleasant privilege to
resume my customary duties. The doctors
were greatly astonished at my rapid recovery,
and so were all my friends. They could see
by the way I had brightened up, and the
way I was putting on. flesh, that Clements
Tonic was making a new woman of me. I
am nearly as active now as when I was a
young woman, and I am thoroughly healthy
in every respect. My children are always
given a dose of Clements Tonic whenever
they have anything the matter with them,
and lam under such an obligation to that
remedy for my new lease of life that I never
neglect to recommend it to my friends.”
“We will give publicity to your state-
ments with your permission.”
“ I hope you will do so, as others may be
cured in the same way. ”

KUTNOW’S POWDER dispels the Low Spirits, Languor,
Biliousness, Nausea, Nervousness, Headache, and
Insomnia that arise from a Disordered Liver by
removing the cause.
A disordered and torpid liver is caused by carelessness in eating and drinking. The liver being the
largest and one of the most important and hard-worked organs of the body, one must expect to suffer a good deal
of misery when it becomes deranged. \Y hen the liver is not in a clean, active condition the entire system is dis-
ordered, and a score of distressing symptoms, such as lassitude, nausea, nervousness, low spirits, biliousness, and
insomnia, naturally follow. If the liver is allowed to remain in a deranged condition long, jaundice, which fre-
quently results in the formation of gall-stones, is likely to occur, and even more serious symptoms.
Most people have not the ability or inclination to discontinue the carelessness in the matter of food and
drink which causes the liver to become disordered, and in this case it is better to remedy the evil consequences.
Prevention is better than cure, but cure is better than permanent ill-health or death.
Nothing will be found more effective in restoring health, strength, and activity to a disordered liver than
the use of Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carlsbad Powder. There are a hundred preparations for remedying
liver disorders, but none is more reliable, safe, and pleasant, and none has received such strong medical
endorsement.
Kutnow’s Powder is composed of the same purifying, healing, and medical properties that attract
thousands of sufferers from liver, stomach, and kidney disorders to the famous mineral springs of Europe. It is
made by dessicating the salts of the most noted of these springs, and adding Effervescence and a few health-
giving ingredients. Kutnow’s Powder strengthens, cleanses, and stimulates the liver, just as do the waters of
the springs. It washes out the impurities that impede the liver’s action, and expels them from the system. At
the same time Kutnow’s Powder has none of the nauseous, drastic, and lowering properties of the mineral
waters.
SOLD BY ALL CHEMISTS

The Maxims of Billy Pagan.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If you’re shovelling sand, be thorough—Glory lies
in degree;
Be the Greatest and Lordliest Dustman or the
Highest Berg at Sea.
When poverty comes, although used to cake be
content with a crumpet;
And thankfully take to the drum in the band
where you once were the trumpet.
Do well or do ill, but do something—rather than
rust and rot;
Sickness comes not to the busy—nor flies to the
boiling pot.
Lay out the work of to-morrow before you go to
your bed;
Only begin the weaving, and- the world will find
the thread.
When thou art joyed be drunken ! Art sad ? take
a horsedrench and pill;
But the man who drowns his sorrow is piling ill on
ill.
Walk out sober to love, and walk far after thy
supper,
Lest to-morrow bring Sin in the saddle while Re-
pentance sits on the crupper.
Deride not the manners of men—nor laugh if a
friend should stutter;
Flatter his accent —so shall ye win to rivers of
honey and butter.
Lend not except to the rich —not then till his
riches be proven;
And give a piece of your cake to a man who has
pies in the oven.
Hurt not a worm without cause ; slay not even a
draper—
Unless he leave the department of sox, and write
for a paper.
Go not by rule or by rote—let expedience be the
token ;
For horses and cakes and laws were only made to
be broken.
Waste not thy time with the mother when thy
heart is drawn to the daughter;
Be not the foolish pack-horse, carrying wine and
drinking water.
Know thine own faults—as .a friend; there let the
news be done;
And claim thou art almost perfect—are there not
spots on the sun ?
Be not vainglorious in boom-time—you’ll appear
the worse in the slump;
The higher the monkey climbeth the more doth
he show his rump.
Show not your teeth if you cannot bite : to your
enemy turn not south;
And if no money you have in your purse, honey
must be in your mouth. .
Give the public what it asks for—gamble on sure
three per cent. —
Sell bicycle suits at Christmas, and turn fish-
monger in Lent.
Give them dirt if they call for dirt, each as his wit
supposes;
For the public is often a pig, and pigs love filth
more than roses.
If silent fools do blame thee because thy speech is
young,
Talk on to their woe, for a brain is worth little
without a tongue.
To him who can take all thou hast, give that he
asks for straight,
And strike with a golden hammer when thou
com’st to an iron gate.
Worse pigs than thou in the litter get the best
pears—what of that ?
If thy father hatli given thee a head, thy head
will soon get thee a hat.
Be friends with a rogue in office hours—but after
be not beguiled ;
The sun passes over the garbage, and yet is not
defiled.
If you must do a good deed, cover it up in a
flurry;
But wait time and place for revenge—that’s never
well done in a hurry.
Change your heart for a gizzard—so shall you live
at ease ;
Back no bills for your friends—and sleep soundly,
forgetting the fleas.
Bite on occasion so that men fear and know how
to treat you ;
Be a dove, and the sky will be dark with the
hawks that come to eat you.
If you can feel no shame, the earth’s yours—sky,
sea and clover ;
And, shame or no shame, legs and a tongue will
take you the wide world over.
Go softly and you go safely—go safely and you go
far ;
But when occasion arises—strut, so that your head
hits a star.
Make your price double your wants, so you may
lower a tittle ;
He who wants a great deal must never ask for a
little.
Pray to your God to guard you from those in whom
you trust;
From those you mistrust you can guard yourself,
and bring them, down to the dust.
Employ a teetotal clerk—wine’s a foe to clear
amounts;
But make a daily audit of that teetotal clerk’s
accounts.
Get all you want, and more—no law the greedy
breaks;
This is a world that gives a man just as much as
he takes.
Let not the world get above thee —he is its slave
who prizes it;
Keep the world for thy servant—he is its king
who despises it.
Do, or undo, or pretend—but at one of these never
be ending ;
For these are the wings of the world —doing, un-
doing, pretending.
If ill-luck come to thee, sleep—and make thy
patience quadruple;
For ill-luck comes by the ton, and goeth away by
the scruple.
Be not content with thy strength, but harden
thyself with guile;
For age will find joints in thine armor for Time’s
inaudible file.
Make thy sins fit thy credit —and woe if they be
worse;
For little thieves are hanged by the neck—great
thieves are hanged by the purse.
Enjoy thy money while yet thou hast seeing eyes
in thy sockets;
Clothe thyself bravely ere ever thou wearest the
last shirt —made without pockets.
In a venture be ready for all—the high roads, or
the treacherous bogs;
And in a strange street carry handsful of meat
(or of stones) for the dogs.
And ever be merry-hearted though dolorous be
the days;
If your house catch fire in the winter, laugh, and
warm yourself at the blaze.
And follow these maxims of Pagan, and if you
follow them straight
You’ll die Councillor William Gutswalk, or the
gallows will be your fate.
And whether the gallows or Gutswalk —and the
diffrence is mostly fudge ;
Die laughing—you waste your tears when you
weep before the judge.
V.
RANDORIAN BEDFORD.
Parson King’s Happy Day.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Parson King was a Maoriland minister, and
catered for one of those small communities where
the people go to sleep at eight o’clock and are not
rightly awake at other times. He was a small-
headed, broad-shouldered man with the reddish-
ness of hair that goes with an energetic disposi-
tion. His father had been a saw-miller in the
district, and Jim had filially desired to follow in
his footsteps ; but a little legacy had determined
the enterprising parent to put Jimmy into the
“ meenistrv.” And now Jim recognised that only
what had got him in could get him out, and
prayed for the root of all evil accordingly.
His other petitions were of a similarly secular
nature. The hereditary instinct to chop was
strong in him, and when sent for to administer
consolation to the sick he thanked heaven when
the family was poor, because then he could cut short
his ghostly comfortings on the plea of devoting
some of his time to giving practical assist-
ance in the backyard. . Other household jobs
were appreciated in a less degree; a broken window
was easier to mend than a shattered and contrite
spirit, and he was happier studying the inside of
a parishioner’s clock than investigating the work-
ings of his soul.
When he had been about three vears in the
ministry he became engaged, conditionally, to a
settler’s daughter who wasn’t going to marry a
minister on any account. Whereupon King’s
desire to get away from beneath his halo became
frantic.
King conducted service in different decrees of
irritability. He snapped at the hymns viciously
and barked out the sermon, which consisted
merely of some biblical story told at . m) fP l
greater length and a great deal worse than in the
Bible; His best girl used to sit in the middle ot
the church and watch him with a fine, cold smile,
and when they met outside would cheer him up
by telling him hard truths about his elocution.
When lie had been told this about 20 times the
Parson decided to expend a certain small per-
centage of his clerical stipend in lattersalls
tickets, in the hope that a big prize would call
him away from his clerical duties. With about bIOU
he could leave the ministry and start a business
where he might chop wood all day long without
being disturbed by any irrelevant details about
religion.
Owing to the fact that Parson King’s best girl s
father objected to the engagement, she did not
meet her “boy” as often as both could- have
wished, but she always knew the state of affairs
by his demeanour as he entered the pulpit. Three
weeks or so before the announcement of the
winning number he would begin to. announce
hymns of an increasingly hopeful kind, and to
preach from texts of a bright and soul-lifting
order. On the Sunday before the announcement
of the result lie was always specially fervent, fre-
quently holding an extra weekly service. Ihe
Sunday following, however, when news of his
failure reached him, he would express his resent-
ment by curtailing his sermon one-third of
its usual length and delivering it in
his dog - voice, while the hymn with which
he began operations was invariably “Plunged
in a Gulf of Dark Despair.” There were plenty
of other melancholy hymns, but “ Plunged was
always reserved for this great occasion, as embody-
ing the only series of remarks that could adequately
express his feelings.
It was a bright Spring morning when the future
but long-postponed Mrs. King entered her “ boy s
church as usual. As she took her seat, she sud-
denly recollected that this was one of these Black
Sundays, and she knew that, as on this occasion,
King had invested in an extra ticket, his disap-
pointment would be proportionately greater. She
gazed across the haylike heads of several tillers of
the soil to catch a glimpse of her beloved wood-
chopper. She wanted to comfort him to assure
him, in her gentle, womanly way, that, if she
could not marry him, she would never take any
other man —unless, of course, the other man hap-
pened to have £3OO a-year. In Maoriland, no girl
is expected to refuse £3OO a-year.
Her eyes, however, only saw the crown of his
well-covered red head as he stooped over the
volume before him. She lifted up her hymn-book
and turned automatically to “Plunged. She
was clearing her throat automatically for
“ Plunged,” when suddenly, like an electric shock,
there rang a clear, joyous voice throughout the
sacred building, “Brethren, we will praise the
Lord by singing hymn No. 222, “ Oh, happy
day that fixed my choice.” After which we will
discourse from the text 11 And she gave to the
King 150 talents of gold.”

Drought.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
These are the realms of Hell. The barren blue
Glows like a furnace o’er the barren land ;
And there are shrivelled heaps whose bones gleam
through
The black and grey decay on either hand.
The stagnant pool shines green against the sun;
Drowning in shallows, massed upon the brink,
Strewn on the slopes, they sank down, one by one,
And suv Fate mock them as they stooped to
drink.
Over grim stepping-stones that fail beneath
His feeble hoofs—a thing of hide and bone,
With lolling tongue parched by the drought-
wind’s breath, —
The last gaunt bullock lurches on alone
Down the. dark road of Death. His white-shot
eye
Rolls in his head ; he turns, and sinks upon
The gripping mud that sucks him down to die—
And of ten thousand head the last has gone !
Thou furnace-breath of desolation, blow !
Thou Sun, burn ruin from thy skies of brass !
The land is bare, and bare of Hope men go :
God in his Heaven is silent as they pass.
V.
GEO. ESSEX EVANS.
The Return of Jack Scuttle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was frightfully hot. The commercials in the
smoking-room relinquished the eternal “euchre,”
and sprawled, thirsty and ill-tempered, on the
lounges. From the engine-rooms the heat-waves
rose like a blast from Avernus, and the perspira-
tion rolled in a steady cascade from the chief-
officer’s brow. Only under the awning on the
poop there was some approach to a cool breeze,
and the ladies in the deck-chairs fanned them-
selves vigorously, and were even silent.
“This is very different from what I had imagined
of the South Seas,” murmured a dark-haired girl
with poetical black eyes.
The man who was tramping up and down the
deck in front of the row of chairs laughed.
“ Things are mostly different from what you read
of,” he remarked, without invitation. “There’s
precious little of the sunny isles of Eden business
in these latitudes.”
The ladies regarded him with languid interest.
He was brown-faced and big-chested, and, though
an interloper, he looked as if he might prove
interesting.
“ Do you know much about these—these lati-
tudes ?” asked the dark-eyed girl.
The man laughed rudely, and leaning with his
back against the poop-rails spoke inconsequently.
“ I had a pal once,” he said, “ who knew more
about ’em than anyone living. His name was
Jack Scuttle, and he’s gone home now.”
“Oh !” cried the ladies with disappointment.
“Most people do that,” said the dark-eyed girl.
“Not the way he went, though,” chuckled the
man. “ For why ! He was eaten.”
He paused to enjoy the manifestations of sur-
prise at this adventure of his friend, and as it died
down in ejaculations of “How horrid!” “Poor
wretch ! ” “ What fearful creatures !” he plunged
into his yarn.
“ Jack Scuttle and me were at school together,
apprentices together, mates, first and second,
together, and when he married Polly Crowley,
whose father was a ship-chandler, I was his best
man. A sweet little woman was Polly; tender
and lovin’-hearted as any man could wish for a
wife. So when Jack sailed —two weeks after
their wedding—in the barque Belladonna, bound
for the South Seas, I thought her heart ’ud break,
she toolc-on so. ‘ Suppose them beastly cannibals
should eat ’im, Bill,’ she said to me, and 1 laughed,
little thinking that her ‘ suppose ’ was pretty near
to the mark. At anyrate they wrote long letters
whenever they could —but fortunately the post
don’t carry everything a young couple would like
to send. No offence, ladies; none meant, none
taken—but you know!
“However, month after month passed, and one
day along comes a big official-looking letter from
the captain of the Belladona to say that Jack
had been taken and eaten by some cannibals in
the Sandwich Islands.
“At first Polly would only believe they were
pulling her leg, an’ she says, quite skittish-like, ‘ A
poor sandwich my Jack ’ud make;’ so she ups and
puts on her bonnet and down she runs to the
shipping-office.
“ ‘ Now just you tell the truth, young man ’ she
says to the clerk there; ‘ what have you done with
my Jack ? ’
“The clerk looks very serious, and he puts his
pen behind his ear and folds his hands on the desk
like a Judge going to hang a ‘crook ’un,’ and he
says :
“‘He’s been eaten, ma’am—positively eaten.
There’s no doubt about it; I have here the report
of the ’ole case from the captain of the Bella-
donna, an’ sad news it is, too.’
‘•‘Polly gives a shriek, an’ down she flops,
faintin’ on the floor. An’ you can imagine her
feelin’, ladies; only married a few weeks—a bee,
fresh on honey—no time to get tired of ’im; an’
she was that sort who don’t leave a hold once
they’ve got a good bite —like a little dog I had
once—though ttiat’s neither here nor there.
“Also, you must remember that Polly was the
best of good Catholics, an’ here she was in a nice
pickle. No tomb for poor Jack, where she could
pray for him an’ have masses said. No place to
hang flowers and light candles on. It was simply
shockin’ to so pious a little woman.
“When she comes to —the clerk undoin’ her
dress havin’ soon fetched her round—she begins
questionin’ them.
“ ‘ How did it happen ? ’ says she.
“ ‘ It was this way, ma’am,’ says the clerk.
‘ The crew of the Belladonna were watering at a
certain island, when the cannibals swooped down
and collared your Jack before he could reach his
boat. They took him to their camp inland, an’
there they cooked an’ ate ’im.’
“ ‘ How do you know this, if no one but these
cannibal devils was there ?’ asks widow Polly, for
she was always a sharp ’un.
“ ‘ Well, ma’am, it was like this. Cap-
tain Howard of the Belladonna landed some
of his men on the island, marched up to the
niggers’ camp, killed most of ’em, and took
their chief prisoner. This chief, whose name was
Fekai, confessed to ’em that, Jack being an officer,
etiquette ordered him to be served as a chief’s
supper. So Fekai himself ate Jack piecemeal.’
“ ‘ What! screamed the widow.’ ‘D’ you mean
to say this Fekai has got my Jack in his dirty
black stomach ? ’
“ The clerk coughed behind his hand, and said
he supposed so. The widow thought for a while,
then she says all of a flash, ‘ What became of this
Fekai ?’
“‘Well, ma’am, it appears from Captain
Howard's report that the nigger did a bolt, and
managed to reach a plantation in Tahiti, where he
is now a slave—l mean to say, a bound-labourer.’
“ ‘ Give me his name,’ she cried, excitedly.
“And when she got it off she goes home and
writes straight to that planter.
“‘Send me this nigger f.o.b. Sydney, and I’ll
pay all freights, and give you twenty-five pounds.’
“Due to time comes the nigger, an’ the widow
meets him at the wharf and takes him home.
“‘Now, then,’says she to him, ‘I your mis-
tress —you do all I tell you.’
“ ‘Io, ma’am,’ says the nigger.
“ ‘You eat white man—officer.’
“ ‘lo—he broody nice, me eat he three days.’
“ ‘So he in there,’ says Polly, and bangs Fekai
on the waist-band.
“ ‘ lo—he mostly there.’
“ ‘You lie down flat,’ orders Polly, and when
he lays down, bless me if she didn’t kneel on his
stomach and begin praying, for she had got it in
her head that Fekai was nothing more nor less
than her poor Jack’s tomb.
“Then she had a nice brass plate made, and
hung it on her walking grave, with this inscrip-
tion :
“ Here lies entombed Jack Scuttle,
Devoured in his 25th year.
Mourned by his loving, faithful wife.”
Not satisfied with that, I’m blowed if she didn’t
hang flowers round the beastly nigger’s neck, and
lead him up and down the street with a chain to
church, where she used to stand him up in a side-
aisle and light something like 42 tapers round his
feet, which caused such a hullaballoo that the
police had to interfere. It couldn’t last long. The
neighbors talked awful scandal about her, and
completely took the dear girl’s character away;
and so, remembering that no man in British
dominions ought to be a slave, I unchained the
blasted nigger one day and kicked him out into
the street, and he was never seen again.
“Polly nearly went dotty over this, and she
advertised all over the country: ‘ Lost, a tomb,
answering to the name of Fekai.’ ”
“What became of the widow ? ” asked the dark-
eyed girl, with intense eagerness.
“Lunatic asylum, ma’am —lunatic asylum,”
said the man ; and, sticking his hands into his
pockets, he strolled away down the deck.
The dark-eyed girl looked enquiringly at the
other ladies. “It was very interesting,” she said,
with a sigh, “but Ido hope the man’s not telling
the truth.”
And she looked sadly at a distant island.
M.L.
EDGAR M. DELL
Patriotism.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[“ Patriotism,” -says an amiable French cynic, “ is the
feeling that induces a man to lie about his country.”]
A crowd of fervid Jingoes (Australian) stood gaz-
ing at the illustrated prints in the window of a
bookshop. Attention was directed particularly
to an English weekly called Under the Union
Jack. The front page was decorated with a pic-
ture entitled “Shooting of John M‘Lachlan at
Harrismith by the Boers, for refusing to fight in
the ranks of an Orange River Free State com-
mando.” Indignation was freely expressed among
the little crowd ; the illustration appealed forcibly
to their excited imaginations. M‘Lachlan was
pourtrayed as springing into the air in his death-
agony, pierced by the bullets of a number of dis-
reputable-looking bearded Boers a few paces in
front. A little grizzly man among the crowd at
the window seemed particularly moved, and gave
vent to his feelings every now and again by ejacu-
lating ferociously : “ Them blanky Boers ! ”
“ But,” remonstrated a quiet-looking citizen at
his side, “ the story isn’t true. Mr. Chamberlain
expressly stated in the House of Commons that
there was not the least foundation for the story.”
“But there’s the picture!” said the patriot,
angrily.
“ It’s untrue, all the same,” said the unpatriotic
spectator.
“Oh, well, if they didn’t do it, they would ha ’
done it if they could.” Again ferociously : “ Them
blanky Boers ! ”
Corollary to the Frenchman’s proposition :
“ Patriotism is the feeling that induces a man to
believe lies about another man’s country.”

“ C.F.” to The Bulletin re Chinese
coin:—
You are not quite accurate in reply to “ E.O.’ (25/8/’00)
re Chinese coin. The standard unit of weight and cur-
rency is the tael or oz. of silver, equal to l£oz. avoirdu-
pois. No such coin as a tael exists. A shoe or sycee, so
called from a fanciful resemblance to the cramped foot of
a Chinawoman, varies from 70 taels down. Till recently
there was no coin but the “ cash,” but now 5, 10, 20 and
25 cent pieces are coined. The decimal system obtains in
China :10li or cash = 1 fan or candarin ;10 fan =l tain
or mace ; 10 tsin— l leuruj or tael. Heavier goods are
weighed by the kati=lj English pounds, and 100 kati =
1 pikul. A tael is worth ljdol. (Mexican), or at present
rates 2s. Bd. to 2s. lOd. European retail accounts are
kept in Mexican dollars—though Jap. yen and British
(Bombay coined) silver of equivalent value have almost
supplanted the Mexican coins ; but bankers and mer-
chants keep accounts in taels.
Concerning a lucky find :
W. Dunstan, a well-known Melbourne contractor, when
demolishing an old building on the premises of the Bank
of Australasia some time ago, struck a patch in the shape
of a soz. slug of melted gold, and after panning off a few
tons of rubbish bagged £350 worth of gold-dust. The
premises had once been the bank’s boiling-down establish-
ment, Fine gold is as insidious as quicksilver, and so the
ground under the floors had been converted into very
profitable wash during the good old early days when
“the dust” was planked down and shuffled about
as recklessly as if it had been bran. After this
scoop the lucky contractor kept his eye open for
other likely shows, and has just struck a second. He is
now rooting on the site of the old Port Phillip smelting-
works in Collins-st., above Russell-st., on the south side
—having undertaken the contract to remove the buildings
off the site—and is getting better prospects than any
panged out at Mt. William. Present writer, as a small
boy, struck it lucky like this on the site of the office of an
abandoned alluvial mine outside Ballarat, but had his
claim jumped by two adults just as affluence was staring
him in the face. Re the above, it may be
remembered that a deluded Dutchman was dis-
covered to have sunk a shaft on the Collins-
street premises mentioned in quest of fabulous
masses of virgin gold, which, he was convinced, invited
him somewhere down under. Traces of gold in the sur-
face dirt started him on his wild downward career. No-
body recollected the old smelting works when this pros-
pector’s story got into the papers, and although he was a
“mug” miner, he narrowly missed a good thing after
all.

died from Consumption in Sydney last year, and this dire disease is spreading day by day. And no wonder. If citizens are so careless about their supply of Milk, the wonder is that there
are not more deaths. There will be ! !
EESRE’S JV SHOKT STORY. *
A milk supplier to Sydney bad three cows suffering from Tuberculosis, and as he was'afraid his healthy cattle would become contaminated, he sold them at less than £1 each to another
supplier who sent his milk to the same distributors. The seller now feels annoyed to think that the Sydney purchaser had been paying for all the milk from the diseased animals for some
months past. THIS MILK IS BEING- SOLD IN SYDNEY TO-DAY ! This is no fairy tale, but .an absolute fact, capable of being easily proven. This is only an individual ease;
there are many, alas! too many of a similar nature. 9
nSTSPBCTIOKT OB DAIRIES AIXUO cattle
The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Coy., Ltd., have every cow in every dairy thoroughly inspected, as wefl as the premises and utensils used. They have their own railway trucks. All milk
arriving under standard quality is condemned. They laugh at local inspection. They lose suppliers because of their strict methods of inspection.
The Company sells milk in bulk or in bottle that is PASTEURISED. If delicate people use other milk, they may meet a premature death. Use only the milk supplied by the
F.F. &I. Coy., and they will get healthy and strong. Strong robust people will grow the stronger. If, after this NOTE OF WARNING}, you continue to use milk from cows suffering
from Tuberculosis —or, in plain words, Consumption—you deserve to die, for the fault rests with yourself.
The F.F. and I. Coy., Ltd., is the only Company who has an Inspector in its regular employ* If you want to rear a healthy family, deal ONLY with us ; but if you want to rear
weaklings, or be weak, then Don’t.
